غيبة
قال الا في مسئلة واحدة و هى أن يقول المريض لوارثه تكفّل عنى بما على من الدين فكفل به مع عـ الغرماء جاز لأن ذالك وصية فى الحقيقة ولهذا تصح و ان لم يسم المكفول لهم ولهذا قالوا انما تصح اذا كان له مال او يقال أنه قائم مقام الطالب لحاجته اليه تفريغاً لذمته وفيه نفع الطالب كما اذا حضر بنفسه وانما يصح بهذا اللفظ ولا يشترط القبول لأنه يراد به التحقيق دون المساومة ظاهرا في هذه الحالة فصار كالأمر بالنكاح ولو قال المريض ذالك لأجنبي اختلف المشائخ فيه ترجمہ سوائے ایک مسئلہ کے اور وہ یہ ہے کہ اور وہ یہ ہے کہ مریض اپنے وارث سے کہے کہ تو میری طرف سے اس قرضہ کا کفیل ہو جا جو مجھ پر ہے پس وہ قرض خواہوں کی عدم موجودگی کے باوجود اس کا کفیل ہو گیا تو جائز ہے کیونکہ یہ حقیقت میں وصیت ہے اور اسی وجہ سے کفالہ صحیح ہوتا ہے اگر چہ مکفول لا جو لوگ ہیں ان کا نام بھی نہ لیا ہو، اور اسی وجہ سے مشائخ نے کہا کہ یہ کفالہ اس وقت صحیح ہوگا جبکہ مریض کے پاس مال ہو یا یوں کہا جائے گا کہ مریض ، طالب، ( قرض خواہ ) کے قائم مقام ہے کیونکہ مریض کو اس بات کی ضرورت ہے کہ اس کا ذمہ فارغ ہو اور اس میں طالب کا نفع ہے جیسے اگر طالب خود حاضر ہوتا اور اس لفظ سے کفالہ کا صحیح ہونا اور قبول کا شرط نہ ہونا اس لئے ہے کہ اس سے مراد تحقیق ہے نہ کہ اس حالت میں ظاہری طور پر معاملہ چکانا ۔ پس یہ امر بالنکاح کے مانند ہو گیا اور اگر مریض نے یہ بات کسی اجنبی سے کہی تو اس میں مشائخ نے اختلاف کیا ہے۔
تشریح سابق میں گذر چکا ہے کہ طرفین کے نزدیک مکفول لڑ کے قبول کئے بغیر کفالہ صحیح نہیں ہوتا مگر ایک مسئلہ ایسا ہے جس میں حت کفالہ کے لئے طرفین کے نزدیک بھی مکفول کا مجلس کے اندر قبول کرنا شرط نہیں ہے اور وہ مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ مریض قرضدار نے اپنے وارث سے کہا کہ تو میری طرف سے اس قرضہ کا کفیل ہو جا جو مجھ پر واجب ہے پس وارث اس کی طرف سے اس قرضہ کا کفیل ہو گیا حالانکہ قرض خواہ موجود نہیں ہیں تو یہ کفالہ استحسانا جائز ہے اگر چہ قیا سا جائز نہیں ہے وجہ قیاس یہ ہے کہ یہاں بھی مکفول لہ غائب ہے ر مكفول لا کے مجلس میں قبول کئے بغیر کفالہ درست نہ ہوگا اور اس لئے کہ اگر تندرست آدمی اپنے ورثہ سے قرض خواہوں کی عدم موجودگی میں تكفل عنى بما على من الدین کہتا اور وہ کفیل ہو جاتے یا کسی اجنبی سے کہتا اور وہ کفیل ہو جاتا تو یہ کفالہ درست نہ ہوتا پس اسی طرح الا في مسألة واحدة " میں بھی کفالہ درست نہ ہوگا۔
وجہ استحسان دو ہیں ایک یہ کہ مریض مدیون کا قول تكفل عنى بما على من الدين وصیت کے معنی میں ہے یعنی تکفل عنی کا مطلب ہے اقضوا دیونی میرے دیون ادا کر دو اور جب ورثہ نے تکفلنا بھا کہا تو اس کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ انہوں نے کہا کہ ہم تیرا تمام قرضہ ادا کر دیں گے پس جب یہ کفالہ وصیت کے معنی میں ہے تو قرض خواہوں کا مجلس کے اندر قبول کرنا بھی شرط نہ ہوگا کیونکہ وصیت مسیح ہونے کے لئے ان لوگوں کا قبول کرنا شرط نہیں ہوتا جن کے واسطے وصیت کی گئی ہو اور چونکہ یہ کفالہ وصیت کے معنی میں ہے اس لئے جو لوگ مکفول لہ ہیں اگر ان کا نام نہ لیا ہو اور وہ مجہول ہوں تو بھی یہ کفالہ درست ہے حالانکہ پہلے گزر چکا ہے کہ مکفول لا کا مجہول ہونا کفالہ کو فاسد کر دیتا ہے اور یہ کفالہ چونکہ وصیت کے معنی میں ہے۔ اسی لئے مشائخ نے کہا کہ یہ کفالہ اس وقت درست ہوگا جبکہ موت کے وقت مریض مذکور کے پاس ترکہ کا مال موجود ہوتا کہ وصی یعنی وارث اس کی یہ وصیت نافذ کر سکے اور اگر اس کے پاس مال نہ ہو تو اس کے ورثاء سے اس کا قرض وصول نہ کیا جائے گا حالانکہ کفالہ کے لئے یہ شرط نہیں ہے کہ مکفول عنہ کے پاس مال موجود ہو۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ یہ کفالہ در حقیقت کفالہ نہیں ہے بلکہ وصیت کے معنی میں ہے اور جب یہ کفالہ وصیت کے معنی میں ہے تو مجلس کے اندر مکھول لہم کا قبول کرنا شرط نہ ہوگا کیونکہ وصیت صحیح ہونے کے لئے موصی لہم کا قبول کرنا شرط نہیں ہوتا۔
دوسری وجہ استحسان یہ ہے کہ اس مسئلہ میں مریض ، مکفول لہم کے ایک ضرورت کی وجہ سے قائم مقام ہے اور وہ ضرورت مریض کے ذمہ کا قرضہ سے فارغ ہونا اور اس قائم مقامی میں مکفول لہم کا بھی نفع ہے اس طور پر کہ ان کا قرضہ وصول ہو جائے گا پس یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسے مکفول لہم خود حاضر ہوتے اور مریض کے وارث مثلاً اس کے بیٹے سے کہتے کہ تو اپنے مورث یعنی اپنے باپ کی طرف سے میرے لئے کفیل ہوجا تو یہ کفالہ درست ہوتا ہپس جب مریض نے مکفول لہم کے قائم مقام ہو کر یہ کہا تو بھی یہ کال درست ہو جائے گا۔
وانما يصح بهذا اللفظ الخ سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ جب مریض مکفول لہ کے قائم مقام ہے تو مریض كا تكفل عنى بما على من الدین کہنے کے بعد قبول کرنا بھی شرط ہونا چاہیے جیسا کہ مکفول لہ کا قبول کرنا شرط ہے کیونکہ پہلے گزر چکا کہ طرفین کے نزدیک صحت کفالہ کے لئے مجلس کفالہ میں مکفول اد کا قبول کرنا شرط ہے جیسے اگر مشتری کسی سے کئے بعنى هذا العبد بالف در هم پس اس نے کہا بعث " تو اس سے بیچ منعقد نہ ہوگی مگر جب مشتری نے قبلست کہا تو بیچ درست ہو جائے گی پس اسی طرح یہاں بھی کفالہ اس وقت صحیح ہونا چاہیے جب مریض نے تكفل عنی کے بعد قبلت کہا ہو؟
جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ مریض کی مرض موت کی ظاہری حالت اس پر دلالت کرتی ہے کہ مریض نے کفالہ کو محقق طور پر منعقد کرنے کا ارادہ کیا ہے محض بھاؤ تاؤ کرنا مقصود نہیں ہے اور یہ ایسا ہے جیسے کسی آدمی نے ایک عورت سے کہاز و جسی نفسک ( تو اپنے آپ کو میرے نکاح میں دیدے ) اس عورت نے دو گواہوں کی موجودگی میں کہا " زوجت" میں نے اپنے آپ کو تیرے نکاح میں دیدیا تو یہ نکاح منعقد ہو گیا اور مرد کو قبول کرنے کی ضرورت نہ رہی بلکہ عورت کا قول زوجت ایجاب و قبول دونوں کے قائم مقام ہو گیا ہے کیونکہ نکاح میں تحقق نکاح مقصود ہوتا ہے بھاؤ تاؤ کرنا مقصود نہیں ہوتا پس اسی طرح کفالہ میں بھی چونکہ تحقیق کفالہ مقصود ہوتا ہے بھاؤ کرنا مقصود نہیں ہوتا اس لئے مریض کا قول تكفل عنی کہ صحت کفالہ کے لئے کافی ہو جائے گا مکفول لڑ کی طرف سے مستقلاً قبول کرنے کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں ہے برخلاف بیج کے کیونکہ بیچ میں بھاؤ کیا جاتا ہے لہذا مشتری کا قول بعنى هذا العبد بالف درهم اس کے قبول کے قائم مقام نہیں ہوگا۔
Book of Kafalah (Suretyship). Ashraf al-Hidayah, volume 9: If a fuduli (unauthorized person) accepted on behalf of the creditor in the session, and then the creditor gave permission upon hearing the news, it is permissible by agreement. If no fuduli accepted in the session, then the creditor's permission is of no use. The conclusion is that according to both sides (Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn), for the validity of kafalah, acceptance in the session after the offer of the guarantor is a condition, whether the creditor accepts or a fuduli accepts on his behalf. If a fuduli accepted, it is suspended upon the creditor's permission upon hearing. Exception to the previous ruling: Absence of creditors. He (the author) said: Except in one case, and that is when a sick person says to his heir: 'Guarantee for me what I owe of debt.' So he guarantees it in the absence of the creditors, it is permissible because that is in reality a will (wasiyyah), and for this reason it is valid even if he did not name the creditors. For this reason they (the scholars) said: It is only valid if he has wealth, or it is said that he stands in place of the claimant due to his need to free his liability, and there is benefit for the claimant as if he himself were present. It is only valid with this wording, and acceptance is not required because it is intended for realization (tahqiq) not bargaining (musawamah) apparently in this situation, so it becomes like the command in marriage (amr bi al-nikah). If the sick person said that to a stranger, the mashayikh differed. Translation: Except for one case, and that is: a sick person says to his heir: 'Become my guarantor for this debt that is upon me.' So he becomes his guarantor despite the absence of the creditors, it is permissible because in reality it is a will, and for this reason the kafalah is valid even if the creditors' names are not mentioned. For this reason the mashayikh said that this kafalah is valid only if the sick person has wealth. Or it is said that the sick person stands in place of the claimant (creditor) because the sick person needs to free his liability, and in this there is benefit for the claimant as if the claimant himself were present. And the validity of kafalah with this wording and non-requirement of acceptance is because the intention is realization (tahqiq), not apparent bargaining in this state. So it becomes like the command in marriage. If the sick person said this to a stranger, then the mashayikh differed about it. Explanation: It has passed earlier that according to both sides (Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn), kafalah is not valid without the acceptance of the creditor. But there is one case where even according to both sides, acceptance by the creditor in the session is not a condition for kafalah. And that is the case of a sick debtor who says to his heir: 'Become my guarantor for the debt that is obligatory upon me.' So the heir becomes his guarantor for that debt even though the creditors are not present. This kafalah is valid by istihsan, although it is not valid by qiyas. The basis of qiyas is that here also the creditor is absent, and without the creditor's acceptance in the session, kafalah is not valid. And because if a healthy person says to his heirs, in the absence of creditors, 'Guarantee for me what I owe of debt,' and they become guarantors, or if he says to a stranger and he becomes guarantor, this kafalah would not be valid. So likewise in the exception ('except in one case'), kafalah would not be valid. The basis of istihsan is two: One is that the statement of the sick debtor 'Guarantee for me what I owe of debt' is in the meaning of a will (wasiyyah), i.e., the meaning is 'Pay my debts.' When the heirs say 'We guarantee that,' it means they say 'We will pay all your debt.' So when this kafalah is in the meaning of a will, then the acceptance of the creditors in the session is not a condition, because for the validity of a will, acceptance by the legatees is not a condition. And because this kafalah is in the meaning of a will, even if the creditors are not named and are unknown, this kafalah is valid, whereas it has passed earlier that the unknownness of the creditor makes kafalah invalid. But this kafalah, since it is in the meaning of a will, therefore the mashayikh said that this kafalah is valid only if at the time of death the sick person has estate wealth so that the executor (heir) can execute this will. If he has no wealth, then his debt will not be recovered from his heirs, whereas for kafalah, it is not a condition that the guaranteed person has wealth. So it is established that this kafalah is not really kafalah but in the meaning of a will. And when it is in the meaning of a will, acceptance by the creditors in the session is not a condition, because for the validity of a will, acceptance by the legatees is not a condition. The second basis of istihsan is that in this case, the sick person stands in place of the creditors due to a need, and that need is to free his liability from debt. And in this representation, there is benefit for the creditors in that their debt will be collected. So it is as if the creditors themselves were present and said to the sick person's heir (e.g., his son): 'Become a guarantor for me on behalf of your ancestor (your father),' then this kafalah would be valid. So when the sick person says this standing in place of the creditors, this kafalah is also valid. The phrase 'It is only valid with this wording, etc.' answers a question. The question is: When the sick person stands in place of the creditor, then after his saying 'Guarantee for me what I owe of debt,' acceptance should also be required, just as acceptance by the creditor is required, because it has passed earlier that according to both sides, for the validity of kafalah, acceptance by the creditor in the session of kafalah is a condition. For example, if a buyer says to someone 'Buy for me this slave for one thousand dirhams,' and he says 'I buy,' then the sale is not concluded unless the buyer says 'I accept.' So similarly here, kafalah should be valid only when after the sick person says 'Guarantee for me,' the guarantor says 'I accept.' The answer is that the apparent state of the sick person's fatal illness indicates that he intends to conclude the kafalah definitively, not merely bargaining. And this is like when a man says to a woman: 'Marry yourself to me' (zawwiji nafsaki). In the presence of two witnesses, the woman says 'I have married myself to you' (zawwajtu), then this marriage is concluded, and the man does not need to accept; rather, the woman's statement 'zawwajtu' serves as both offer and acceptance, because in marriage, the realization of marriage is intended, not bargaining. Similarly in kafalah, since the realization of kafalah is intended, not bargaining, the sick person's statement 'Guarantee for me' suffices for the validity of kafalah, and there is no need for separate acceptance from the creditor. Unlike sale, because in sale bargaining is done, hence the buyer's statement 'Buy for me this slave for one thousand dirhams' does not stand in place of his acceptance.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh of Al-Hidayah discusses an important exception in the law of Kafalah. The general rule in the Hanafi school is that for Kafalah to be valid, there must be an offer (ijab) by the guarantor (kafil) and acceptance (qabul) by the creditor (makful lahu) in the same session. This is the position of both Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). However, there is one exception: if a person is on his deathbed (marid) and owes debts, he may say to his heir (warith), 'Guarantee for me what I owe' (takaffal `anni bima `alayya min al-dayn). If the heir agrees and becomes a guarantor, this is valid even if the creditors are absent and have not accepted. The reason is that this transaction is treated as a will (wasiyyah), not an ordinary Kafalah. A will does not require acceptance by the beneficiaries at the time of making; it becomes effective upon death, and the beneficiaries can accept or reject later. Therefore, the creditors' acceptance is not needed. The passage provides two bases for this exceptional ruling by istihsan (juristic preference). First, the debtors' words are interpreted as a request to pay off his debts, which is a will. Second, the sick debtor stands in the place of the creditors because he has a need to free his liability from debt, and the creditors benefit from this as well. The passage also notes that if the debtor says this to a non-heir (ajnabi), the scholars differed. Moreover, the ruling is conditional upon the debtor having enough wealth (mal) at the time of death to fulfill the will; otherwise, it is not binding on the heir. The text then addresses a potential objection: if the sick debtor stands in place of the creditor, why is acceptance not required from the guarantor? The answer is that the context of fatal illness indicates a definitive intention, not bargaining, similar to marriage where the woman's offer and acceptance combine in one statement. In sale, bargaining is expected, so acceptance must be explicit. But here, the debtor's statement 'Takaffal `anni' is sufficient without separate acceptance, because the goal is to realize the guarantee, not to haggle. This exception is specific to the wording used and the situation of a sick debtor. The author emphasizes that this is an exception (istithna) to the general rule, and the analogy (qiyas) would have required acceptance, but istihsan prevails.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A contract of suretyship where one person guarantees the debt of another to the creditor.
وصية (wasiyyah): A will or bequest that takes effect after death; here, the kafalah is treated as a will for purposes of not requiring creditor's acceptance.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides analogical reasoning (qiyas) due to a stronger consideration like need or custom.
قبول (qabul): Acceptance by the creditor in the session of the kafalah offer; normally a condition for validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, a sick debtor tells his heir 'Takaffal `anni bima `alayya min al-dayn' (guarantee for me what I owe of debt) in the absence of the creditors. The heir agrees. Which of the following is correct?
A) The kafalah is invalid because the creditors did not accept.
B) The kafalah is valid by istihsan because it is treated as a will.
C) The kafalah is valid only if the heir is also a creditor.
D) The kafalah requires the sick debtor to repeat the offer after recovery.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows a sick debtor's instruction to his heir to guarantee his debt to be treated as a will, overriding the usual requirement of creditor's acceptance by istihsan.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
9 min
The Sick Person's Kafalah as a Bequest
Explains that a sick person's kafalah is like a bequest, altering rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of a sick person's kafalah and explain why it is treated as a bequest using istihsan and qiyas with marriage.

Core Idea
A sick person's kafalah is validated as a bequest, overriding normal kafalah rules.

In normal kafalah, acceptance by the creditor is required and the creditor must be known. However, when a person on their deathbed acts as a surety for their own creditors, the Hanafi school treats this as a bequest rather than a regular kafalah. This lesson explores the conditions, the proof via istihsan, and the analogy with marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is the act being ruled upon? What is the hukm (valid/invalid)? Note that the kafalah is treated as a bequest.
Do not assume normal kafalah rules apply.
2
Understand the condition for validity
The mashayikh conditioned validity on the sick person having estate wealth at death. Why is this condition necessary? Because if there is no wealth, the bequest cannot be executed.
This condition is specific to the sick person's kafalah.
3
Analyze the first proof: istihsan (kafalah as a bequest)
The sick person's kafalah is treated as a bequest. Therefore, acceptance by creditors is not required, and unknown creditors do not invalidate it.
Compare with normal kafalah where these conditions are essential.
4
Analyze the second proof: qiyas with marriage
The author draws an analogy with marriage: when a man says 'zawwiji nafsak' and the woman says 'zawwajtu', no separate acceptance is needed. Similarly, the sick person's statement 'takaffaltu 'anni' suffices because the goal is conclusion, not bargaining.
Why is sale different? Because sale involves bargaining.
5
Address the objection from sale analogy
The objector argues that in sale, the buyer's offer does not stand for acceptance. The answer: sale involves bargaining, so offer and acceptance are distinct. In kafalah (like marriage), bargaining is not intended; thus the same person's statement can serve both roles.
6
Note the disagreement when addressing a stranger
Some mashayikh require acceptance when the sick person addresses a stranger (non-heir), while others say it is still valid without. The text leaves this unresolved.
This shows that caution may be warranted in certain cases.

قرضہ ادا کر دیں گے پس جب یہ کفالہ وصیت کے معنی میں ہے تو قرض خواہوں کا مجلس کے اندر قبول کرنا بھی شرط نہ ہوگا کیونکہ وصیت صحیح ہونے کے لئے ان لوگوں کا قبول کرنا شرط نہیں ہوتا جن کے واسطے وصیت کی گئی ہو اور چونکہ یہ کفالہ وصیت کے معنی میں ہے اس لئے جو لوگ مکفول لہ ہیں اگر ان کا نام نہ لیا ہو اور وہ مجہول ہوں تو بھی یہ کفالہ درست ہے حالانکہ پہلے گزر چکا ہے کہ مکفول لہ کا مجہول ہونا کفالہ کو فاسد کر دیتا ہے اور یہ کفالہ چونکہ وصیت کے معنی میں ہے۔ اسی لئے مشائخ نے کہا کہ یہ کفالہ اس وقت درست ہوگا جبکہ موت کے وقت مریض مذکور کے پاس ترکہ کا مال موجود ہوتا کہ وصی یعنی وارث اس کی یہ وصیت نافذ کر سکے اور اگر اس کے پاس مال نہ ہو تو اس کے ورثاء سے اس کا قرض وصول نہ کیا جائے گا حالانکہ کفالہ کے لئے یہ شرط نہیں ہے کہ مکفول عنہ کے پاس مال موجود ہو۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ یہ کفالہ در حقیقت کفالہ نہیں ہے بلکہ وصیت کے معنی میں ہے اور جب یہ کفالہ وصیت کے معنی میں ہے تو مجلس کے اندر مکفول لہم کا قبول کرنا شرط نہ ہوگا کیونکہ وصیت صحیح ہونے کے لئے موصی لہم کا قبول کرنا شرط نہیں ہوتا۔

دوسری وجہ استحسان یہ ہے کہ اس مسئلہ میں مریض، مکفول لہم کے ایک ضرورت کی وجہ سے قائم مقام ہے اور وہ ضرورت مریض کے ذمہ کا قرضہ سے فارغ ہونا اور اس قائم مقامی میں مکفول لہم کا بھی نفع ہے اس طور پر کہ ان کا قرضہ وصول ہو جائے گا پس یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسے مکفول لہم خود حاضر ہوتے اور مریض کے وارث مثلاً اس کے بیٹے سے کہتے کہ تو اپنے مورث یعنی اپنے باپ کی طرف سے میرے لئے کفیل ہوجا تو یہ کفالہ درست ہوتا ہے پس جب مریض نے مکفول لہم کے قائم مقام ہو کر یہ کہا تو بھی یہ کفالہ درست ہو جائے گا۔

وانما يصح بهذا اللفظ الخ سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ جب مریض مکفول لہ کے قائم مقام ہے تو مریض كا تكفل عني بما على من الدین کہنے کے بعد قبول کرنا بھی شرط ہونا چاہیے جیسا کہ مکفول لہ کا قبول کرنا شرط ہے کیونکہ پہلے گزر چکا کہ طرفین کے نزدیک صحت کفالہ کے لئے مجلس کفالہ میں مکفول لہ کا قبول کرنا شرط ہے جیسے اگر مشتری کسی سے کہے بعني هذا العبد بألف درهم پس اس نے کہا بعت تو اس سے بیع منعقد نہ ہوگی مگر جب مشتری نے قبلت کہا تو بیع درست ہو جائے گی پس اسی طرح یہاں بھی کفالہ اس وقت صحیح ہونا چاہیے جب مریض نے تكفل عني کے بعد قبلت کہا ہو؟

جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ مریض کی مرض موت کی ظاہری حالت اس پر دلالت کرتی ہے کہ مریض نے کفالہ کو محقق طور پر منعقد کرنے کا ارادہ کیا ہے محض بھاؤ تاؤ کرنا مقصود نہیں ہے اور یہ ایسا ہے جیسے کسی آدمی نے ایک عورت سے کہا زوجي نفسك (تو اپنے آپ کو میرے نکاح میں دیدے) اس عورت نے دو گواہوں کی موجودگی میں کہا زوجت (میں نے اپنے آپ کو تیرے نکاح میں دیدیا) تو یہ نکاح منعقد ہو گیا اور مرد کو قبول کرنے کی ضرورت نہ رہی بلکہ عورت کا قول زوجت ایجاب و قبول دونوں کے قائم مقام ہو گیا ہے کیونکہ نکاح میں تحقق نکاح مقصود ہوتا ہے بھاؤ تاؤ کرنا مقصود نہیں ہوتا پس اسی طرح کفالہ میں بھی چونکہ تحقیق کفالہ مقصود ہوتا ہے بھاؤ کرنا مقصود نہیں ہوتا اس لئے مریض کا قول تكفل عني صحت کفالہ کے لئے کافی ہو جائے گا مکفول لہ کی طرف سے مستقلاً قبول کرنے کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں ہے برخلاف بیع کے کیونکہ بیع میں بھاؤ کیا جاتا ہے لہذا مشتری کا قول بعني هذا العبد بألف درهم اس کے قبول کے قائم مقام نہیں ہوگا۔

اور اگر مریض نے وارث کے علاوہ کسی اجنبی آدمی سے ایسا کلام کیا تو اس میں مشائخ کا اختلاف ہے بعض نے کہا کہ یہ کفالہ کے قبول کئے بغیر درست نہ ہوگا، اور بعض نے کہا کہ درست ہو جائیگا۔
They will repay the debt. So when this kafalah is in the meaning of a bequest, then acceptance by the creditors in the session is not a condition, because for a bequest to be valid, acceptance by those for whom the bequest is made is not a condition. And since this kafalah is in the meaning of a bequest, therefore if the names of the makful lahum (creditors) are not mentioned and they are unknown, this kafalah is valid, although it has passed earlier that the unknown status of the makful lahu invalidates the kafalah. And because this kafalah is in the meaning of a bequest, the mashayikh said: this kafalah is valid only when at the time of death the sick person possesses estate wealth so that the executor (heir) can execute his bequest; and if he has no wealth, then his debt will not be collected from his heirs, although for kafalah it is not a condition that the makful anhu (principal debtor) possesses wealth. Thus it is proven that this kafalah is not in reality a kafalah but rather in the meaning of a bequest. And when this kafalah is in the meaning of a bequest, then acceptance by the makful lahum in the session is not a condition, because for a bequest to be valid, acceptance by the legatees is not a condition.

Second reason of istihsan: In this issue, the sick person stands in the place of the creditors due to a necessity, and that necessity is freeing his own liability from debt. And in this representation, the creditors also have benefit in that their debt will be collected. So this is as if the creditors themselves were present and said to the sick person's heir, for example his son: 'Become a surety for me on behalf of your father' – then this kafalah would be valid. Similarly, when the sick person, acting as the representative of the creditors, said this, this kafalah is also valid.

'And it is only valid by this wording etc.' – this is an answer to a question. The question is: When the sick person is the representative of the creditors, then after the sick person says 'takaffaltu anni bima 'alayya min al-dayn' (I become surety for what is owed of debt), acceptance should also be a condition, just as acceptance by the creditor is a condition. Because it has passed that according to both sides, for the validity of kafalah, acceptance by the creditor in the session is a condition, as if a buyer says to someone 'Be'nī hādhā al-'abda bi-alfi dirham' and he says 'bi'tu', the sale is not concluded until the buyer says 'qabiltu', then the sale becomes valid. Similarly here, the kafalah should only be valid when the sick person says 'qabiltu' after 'takaffaltu 'annī'.

Answer: The answer is that the apparent condition of the sick person (dying) indicates that he intended to conclude the kafalah effectively, not mere bargaining. And this is like when a man says to a woman 'Zawwijī nafsak' (marry yourself to me), and the woman says 'zawwajtu' (I married myself to you) in the presence of two witnesses, the marriage is concluded and the man does not need to accept; rather, the woman's statement 'zawwajtu' stands in the place of both offer and acceptance, because in marriage the goal is the conclusion of marriage, not bargaining. Similarly in kafalah, since conclusion of kafalah is intended, not bargaining, the sick person's statement 'takaffaltu 'annī' is sufficient for validity, and there is no need for independent acceptance by the creditor. Unlike sale, because in sale there is bargaining, so the buyer's statement 'Be'nī hādhā al-'abda bi-alfi dirham' does not stand in the place of his acceptance.

And if the sick person said this to a stranger other than the heir, then there is disagreement among the mashayikh: some said that this kafalah is not valid without acceptance, and some said it is valid.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the ruling of a sick person's kafalah. It clarifies that when a sick person at the time of death acts as a surety for the creditors from his own liability, this kafalah is not a regular kafalah but rather operates as a bequest (wasiyyah). The author first establishes the rule: because it is like a bequest, the creditors do not need to accept it in the session, and it is valid even if the creditors are unknown. This is a deviation from normal kafalah rules. However, the mashayikh conditioned its validity on the sick person having wealth at death, so the heirs can execute it. If he has no wealth, the heirs are not obliged to pay.

The author then gives a second reason from istihsan: the sick person stands in the place of the creditors out of necessity (to free his liability), and this brings benefit to the creditors. Analogy: if the creditors themselves had asked the heir to be surety, it would be valid. Similarly here, when the sick person acts as their representative, it is valid.

Next, the author addresses an objection: if the sick person is a representative, why isn't his acceptance (qabūl) required after his offer? The answer: the context of death illness shows his intention to conclude, not bargain. This is similar to marriage where the woman's 'zawwajtu' suffices for both offer and acceptance because the goal is conclusion. In sale, bargaining is involved, so 'Be'nī' requires acceptance. Hence, in this sick person's kafalah, mere 'takaffaltu' is enough without separate qabūl.

Finally, the author notes a disagreement among the mashayikh when the sick person addresses a stranger (not an heir): some require acceptance, some say it is valid without. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that it is valid without acceptance because it is still in the meaning of a bequest, but caution may be taken.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafālah): A contract of suretyship where one person (kafil) becomes liable for a debt or obligation of another (makful anhu) in favor of a creditor (makful lahu).
وصية (waṣiyyah): A bequest made by a person to take effect after death, typically for a third party, subject to conditions.
استحسان (istiḥsān): A method of juristic preference where a ruling is adopted based on equity, necessity, or custom, departing from strict analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is acceptance by the creditors not required in the case of a sick person's kafalah (like a bequest)?
A) Because the creditors are unknown.
B) Because it is like a bequest, where acceptance is not a condition for validity.
C) Because the sick person has no wealth.
D) Because the kafalah is invalid.

Key Takeaway
A sick person's kafalah for his own debt is treated as a bequest, valid only if he has estate wealth, and does not require creditor acceptance.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
10 min
Kafalah for a Deceased Insolvent Debtor: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafalah for deceased insolvent debtor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the validity of kafalah for a deceased insolvent debtor, explain their respective proofs, and identify the muftā bihī position.

Core Idea
A debt does not necessarily drop upon death without assets; kafalah may be valid per Sahibayn.

When a debtor dies insolvent with no assets, does the debt become defunct in this world, or does it remain enforceable such that a surety can be taken for it? This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The resolution of this dispute has practical implications for creditors, sureties, and the management of debts after death. We will follow the reasoning of both sides and understand which view is ultimately relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
The case: A person dies owing debts and leaves no assets (no estate). A third party voluntarily becomes a surety (kafil) for those debts to the creditors. The question is whether this kafalah is valid.
Note that the debtor had no existing surety before death.
2
State the Hanafi positions
Imam Abu Hanifa holds the kafalah is invalid (bāṭil). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold it is valid (ṣaḥīḥ).
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf recorded in the Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof (opposing school)
The Sahibayn argue that the debt is thābit (established) because it was validly incurred and no canceling factor (payment, remission, release) has occurred. The debt remains in the rulings of the afterlife, and if someone voluntarily pays it, it is valid. Therefore, it can be the subject of a valid kafalah.
They also note that if the deceased had property or a surety, the debt would clearly remain.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues that the debt is sāqit (dropped) in worldly matters. He defines debt (dayn) as essentially an act (fi'l) that requires ability. Since the deceased is incapable by himself and has no substitute (no estate, no surety), the possibility of collection is lost, so the debt falls necessarily. Kafalah for a dropped debt is invalid. Voluntary payment does not depend on the debt's legal existence.
Abu Hanifa acknowledges the debt remains in the afterlife, but that does not create a worldly obligation for kafalah.
5
Determine the muftā bihī and practical consequence
The later Hanafi school (e.g., al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah) adopts the Sahibayn's view as the muftā bihī. Therefore, a surety for a deceased insolvent debtor is liable and must pay the creditors. This aligns with the majority of other schools (Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali).
The thamarah al-khilaf is that according to Abu Hanifa the surety is not bound; according to the Sahibayn, the surety must pay.

قرضدار آدمی فوت ہو گیا اور قرض کی ادائیگی کے لئے کچھ نہیں چھوڑا ایک شخص قرض خواہوں کیلئے کفیل بن گیا تو کفالت درست نہیں

قال و اذا مات الرجل و عليه دين ولم يترك شيئاً فتكفّل عنه رجل للغرماء لم تصح عند أبي حنيفة و قالا تصح لأنه كفل بدين ثابت لأنه وجب لحق الطالب ولم يوجد المسقط ولهذا يبقى فى حق احكام الآخرة ولو تبرع به انسان يصح و كذا يبقى اذا كان به كفيل او مال وله أنه كفّل بدين ساقط لأن الدين هو الفعل حقيقة ولهذا يوصف بالوجوب لكنه فى الحكم مال لأنه يؤل اليه فى المال وقد عجز بنفسه و بخلفه ففات عاقبة الاستيفاء فيسقط ضرورة والتبرع لا يعتمد قيام الدین و اذا كان به كفیل او له مال فخلفه او الافضا الى الاداء باق

اگر کوئی شخص مرگیا حالانکہ اس پر قرضے ہیں اور اس نے کوئی مال نہیں چھوڑا ہے پھر میت کی طرف سے ایک آدمی قرض خواہوں کے لئے کفیل ہو گیا تو امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک یہ کفالہ صحیح نہیں ہے اور صاحبین نے کہا کہ صحیح ہے کیونکہ وہ ایسے قرضہ کا کفیل ہوا ہے جو ثابت ہے اس لئے کہ قرضہ میت کی حیات میں مکفول لہ کے حق کی وجہ سے واجب ہوا تھا اور اس کو ساقط کرنے والا کوئی امر پایا نہیں گیا اور اسی وجہ سے وہ آخرت کے احکام میں باقی رہتا ہے اور اگر اس قرضہ کو ادا کرنے میں کسی انسان نے تبرع کیا تو صحیح ہے اور ایسے ہی یہ قرضہ اس وقت باقی رہتا ہے جبکہ اس قرضہ کا کوئی کفیل ہو یا میت کا کچھ مال ہو اور امام صاحب کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ وہ ایسے قرضہ کا کفیل ہوا ہے جو ساقط ہے اس لئے کہ دین تو حقیقت میں فعل ہے اور اس وجہ سے اس کو وجوب کے ساتھ موصوف کیا جاتا ہے لیکن حکم میں وہ مال ہے کیونکہ انجام کار کے اعتبار سے وہ اس کی طرف لوٹتا ہے اور میت بذات خود بھی عاجز ہو گیا اور بذریعہ خلیفہ بھی عاجز ہو گیا تو وصول ہونے کا انجام فوت ہو گیا پس ضرورۃ (دنیا میں بھی) ساقط ہو جائیگا۔ اور تبرع کرنا قیام دین پر موقوف نہیں ہے اور جب قرضہ کا کفیل موجود ہے یا میت کا مال موجود ہے تو میت کا خلیفہ یا ادائیگی تک پہنچنا باقی ہے۔

تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص جس پر لوگوں کا قرضہ ہے مفلس ہو کر مر گیا یعنی اس نے کوئی مال ترکہ میں نہیں چھوڑا اور نہ اس کی طرف سے پہلے سے کوئی کفیل موجود ہے پھر اس میت کی طرف سے ایک شخص کفیل ہو گیا وہ شخص خواہ میت کا وارث ہو یا اجنبی ہو تو ایسی صورت میں حضرت امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک کفالہ صحیح نہیں ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک صحیح ہے یہی قول امام مالک، امام شافعی اور امام احمد کا ہے۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ شخص ایسے دین صحیح کا کفیل ہوا ہے جو دین مکفول عنہ یعنی میت کے ذمہ میں ثابت ہے اور ایسے دین صحیح کا کفالہ جو مکفول عنہ کے ذمہ ثابت ہو بالاتفاق صحیح ہوتا ہے لہذا یہ کفالہ بھی صحیح ہو گا۔ میت کے ذمہ جو دین ہے وہ صحیح تو اس لئے ہے کہ مسئلہ، دین صحیح میں فرض کیا گیا ہے اور ثابت اور قائم اس لئے ہے کہ میت کی حیات میں قرض خواہوں کے حق کی وجہ سے میت پر دین واجب ہوا تھا اور جو دین واجب ہو وہ اس وقت تک ساقط نہیں ہوتا جب تک کہ تین باتوں میں سے ایک بات نہ پائی جائے۔
A debtor died and left nothing for repayment. A person became surety for the creditors. The kafalah is not valid.

He said: If a man dies owing a debt and leaves nothing, and a man becomes surety for him for the creditors, it is not valid according to Abu Hanifa, but they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said it is valid, because he became surety for a debt that is established, because it became obligatory due to the right of the claimant and no canceling factor exists, therefore it remains in the matters of the afterlife. If a person voluntarily pays it, it is valid. Similarly, it remains if there is a surety for it or property. And his (Abu Hanifa's) proof is that he became surety for a debt that is dropped, because debt is essentially an act, hence it is described as wajib, but in legal effect it is property because it ultimately leads to property. And the deceased is incapable by himself and by his substitute, so the outcome of collection is lost, so it falls necessarily. And voluntary payment does not depend on the debt being in existence. And if there is a surety or property, then the deceased's substitute or access to payment remains.

If a person dies while he owes debts and left no wealth, then a person becomes surety for the creditors on behalf of the deceased, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa this kafalah is not valid, and the Sahibayn said it is valid because he became surety for a debt that is established. Because the debt was obligatory during the deceased's life due to the right of the creditors, and no canceling factor is found, hence it remains in the matters of the afterlife. And if someone voluntarily pays it, it is valid. Similarly, this debt remains if there is a surety for it or some property of the deceased. And the Imam's proof is that he became surety for a debt that is dropped, because debt is essentially an act, hence it is described as wajib, but in legal effect it is property because ultimately it leads to property. And the deceased has become incapable by himself and by his substitute, so the outcome of collection is lost, so it falls by necessity. And voluntary payment does not depend on the existence of the debt. And when there is a surety or property, then the deceased's substitute or access to payment remains.

Explanation of the case: A person who owes debts dies insolvent, i.e., he left no wealth in inheritance, and there is no existing surety for him. Then a person becomes surety on behalf of the deceased, whether he is an heir or a stranger. In such a case, according to Imam Abu Hanifa the kafalah is not valid, and according to the Sahibayn it is valid. This is also the saying of Imam Malik, Imam Shafi'i, and Imam Ahmad. The Sahibayn's proof is that the person became surety for a sound debt that is established in the liability of the deceased, and the kafalah for such a sound debt that is established is valid by consensus. Therefore this kafalah is also valid. The debt on the deceased is sound because the issue is assumed with a sound debt, and it is established and existing because during the deceased's life the debt was obligatory due to the right of the creditors, and an obligatory debt does not drop unless one of three things occurs.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of kafalah for a deceased insolvent debtor. The dispute is between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The case: A person dies with debts but no assets, and another person voluntarily becomes a surety for that debt for the creditors.

Abu Hanifa's position: The kafalah is invalid. His reasoning: The debt of the deceased is considered 'sāqit' (dropped) in worldly terms because the deceased has no assets and no substitute (like a surety or estate) to enforce payment. Although the debt remains in the afterlife, in this world it is effectively dropped due to impossibility of collection. Kafalah for a dropped debt is invalid. Furthermore, the fact that a third party could pay it voluntarily does not depend on the debt's legal existence for enforcement; it is a pure donation.

Sahibayn's position: The kafalah is valid. Their reasoning: The debt is 'thābit' (established) because it was validly incurred and no canceling factor (such as payment, release, or remission) has occurred. The debt remains in the rulings of the afterlife, and if someone voluntarily pays it, it is accepted. Therefore, it can be the subject of a valid kafalah. They also note that if the deceased has a surety or property, the debt remains in effect. The fact that the debtor is insolvent does not negate the debt; it merely delays enforcement.

The text also notes that the majority of the Sahibayn's view is held by Imams Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad. The dispute is based on the nature of a debt: is it an act (fi'l) that is inherently wajib but becomes property in legal effect, or is it a property right that endures until fulfillment?

In the Hanafi school, the muftā bihī (operative position) for fatwa is the view of the Sahibayn, as it is more practical and aligns with the majority of scholars. However, the text here presents both proofs without explicitly stating the tarjih, though the subsequent clarification suggests that the Sahibayn's view is stronger.

Key Terms
دين ثابت (dayn thābit): A debt that is established and enforceable, with no factor canceling it. In this context, it means the debt remains in legal effect even after the debtor's insolvency.
دين ساقط (dayn sāqit): A debt that has fallen or dropped in the sense that it cannot be claimed in this world due to the debtor's death without assets and no substitute.
تبرع (tabarru‘): Voluntary payment or donation, not required by law. In this context, if someone voluntarily pays the deceased's debt, it is valid even if the debt is considered sāqit by Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the muftā bihī position in the Hanafi school, is kafalah for a debt of a deceased insolvent debtor valid?
A) No, because the debt is considered sāqit.
B) Yes, because the debt remains thābit.
C) It depends on whether the surety is an heir or a stranger.
D) The kafalah is valid only if the debtor had some property.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi muftā bihī holds that kafalah for a deceased insolvent debtor is valid because the debt remains established (thābit) unless canceled by specific factors, and the surety is bound to pay.

Guarantee for Debt of Insolvent Deceased: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the validity of kafalah for an insolvent deceased debtor and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A debt does not lapse upon death without explicit cause; the Sahibayn uphold the validity of guarantee.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a guarantee (kafalah) for the debt of a person who died insolvent is valid. Abu Hanifa argues that the debt lapses due to the debtor's incapacity, making the guarantee invalid. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the debt remains established because no cause of suqut occurred, and they support their view with the hadith of Abu Qatadah. Understanding this dispute clarifies the school's approach to contractual obligations and the effect of death on debts.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the two positions
Identify the exact scenario: a person dies with debts and no assets, and another person becomes a guarantor. State that Abu Hanifa says the guarantee is invalid (fasid), while the Sahibayn say it is valid (sahih).
The dispute is recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof (dalil)
Understand Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the debt is essentially an action (fi'l) and the deceased is incapable of performing it, so the debt necessarily lapses. A guarantee for a lapsed debt is invalid.
He also argues that voluntary payment does not prove the debt's existence.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof (dalil)
The Sahibayn argue that the debt remains established because none of the three causes of invalidation (creditor release, payment, or annulment of cause) have occurred. They cite the hadith of Abu Qatadah where the Prophet (peace be upon him) refused to pray until the debt was guaranteed.
They also note that the debt remains for matters of the Hereafter.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: if someone guarantees the debt, is he liable? According to Abu Hanifa, no; the debt is lapsed and the guarantee is void. According to the Sahibayn, yes, the guarantor must pay.
The Sahibayn's view is also held by the majority of other schools (Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad).
5
Note the tarjih (preferred view)
Although the passage does not explicitly state a tarjih, the detailed argument and the hadith evidence suggest that the Sahibayn's view is stronger and is adopted by later Hanafi authorities.
The mufta bihi today is the validity of the guarantee.

نماز جنازہ نہ فرماتے
He said: And when a man dies and owes a debt and leaves nothing, so a man becomes a guarantor for him to the creditors, it is not valid according to Abu Hanifa, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said it is valid because he guaranteed a debt that is established, because it was obligatory due to the right of the claimant and no invalidating factor existed, and therefore it remains in the matters of the Hereafter, and if someone volunteers to pay it, it is valid, and likewise it remains if there is a guarantor or property for it. And his (Abu Hanifa's) reasoning is that he guaranteed a debt that has lapsed, because debt in reality is an action, and therefore it is described as obligatory, but in legal ruling it is property because it eventually leads to property, and the deceased is incapable by himself and by his successor, so the possibility of recovery is lost, so it necessarily lapses, and volunteering does not depend on the existence of the debt. And if there is a guarantor or property for it, then his successor or [the process] leading to payment remains. Explanation of the case: A person who owes debts to people dies insolvent, i.e., he left no property in inheritance, and there was no prior guarantor from his side, then a person becomes a guarantor for this deceased, whether the person is an heir or a stranger. In such a case, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the guarantee is not valid, and according to the two companions (Sahibayn), it is valid. This is also the saying of Imam Malik, Imam Shafi'i, and Imam Ahmad. The proof of the two companions is that the person has guaranteed a debt that is established in the liability of the deceased, and guaranteeing a valid debt that is established in the liability of the principal (makful 'anhu) is valid by consensus, so this guarantee is also valid. The debt on the deceased is valid because the case is assumed to be a valid debt, and it is established and standing because during the deceased's life, the debt became obligatory due to the right of the creditors, and an obligatory debt is not invalidated until one of three things occurs: either the right-holder (creditor) releases the debtor, or the debtor pays the debt, or the cause of obligation ceases. And because of the debtor's death, none of these three things occurred, because the case is assumed such that the creditor did not release the debtor, the debtor did not pay, and the cause of obligation did not cease. So the debt is not invalidated from the deceased's liability, and when the debt is not invalidated, it is known that the debt is established and standing in the deceased's liability. Therefore, in the matters of the Hereafter, the debt remains on the deceased's liability, so much so that the debtor will be taken to account in the Hereafter and will be sinful for not paying the debt. So when the debt remains in the Hereafter, it also remains in worldly matters. One proof that the debt is established in worldly matters is that if a person voluntarily pays the debt of the deceased, this payment is valid. If the insolvent debtor was freed from the debt due to death and had no debt, then it would not be permissible for the creditor to take the voluntarily paid money from the payer. The point is that it being permissible for the creditor to take money from the volunteer is proof that the debt is established and existing on the deceased. Similarly, if the deceased had a guarantor previously, the guarantee remains after his death, such that by consensus the debt is collected from the guarantor. If the debt lapsed due to death, it should also lapse from the guarantor, because the lapse of the debt necessitates the release of the guarantor, but the demand for the debt from the guarantor does not lapse. That the demand from the guarantor does not lapse is proof that the debt is established on the deceased. And similarly, if the deceased left some property, the debt is recovered from the inheritance; this also proves that the debt is established and existing on the deceased. In short, the conclusion is that the debt is established and existing on the deceased, and guaranteeing a valid debt that is established is valid, as the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'The guarantor is liable.' This hadith is general, so this guarantee is also valid. The school of the two companions is supported by hadiths, whether the guarantee was made during the principal's life or after his death for his debt, in both cases the guarantee is valid. Another hadith: A funeral of an Ansari was brought, and he (the Prophet) said: 'Does your companion have any debt?' They said: 'Yes, two dirhams or two dinars.' He said: 'Pray over your companion.' Then Abu Qatadah stood and said: 'They are on me,' so he (the Prophet) prayed. Observe: If the guarantee from the deceased's side was not valid, then despite Abu Qatadah's guarantee, the Prophet would not have prayed the funeral prayer for that Ansari.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a guarantee (kafalah) for the debt of a deceased person who died insolvent (leaving no property and no prior guarantor). Abu Hanifa holds that the guarantee is invalid because the debt has lapsed (saqit) due to the debtor's incapacity, as debt in its essence is an action (fi'l) and the deceased is unable to perform it. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that the debt remains established (thabit) because none of the causes of invalidation (suqut) – release by creditor, payment, or annulment of cause – have occurred. They support their view with the hadith of Abu Qatadah, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) refused to lead the funeral prayer until Abu Qatadah guaranteed the debt, indicating that such a guarantee is valid. The passage explains the reasoning of both sides in detail, with the Sahibayn's position being the stronger one according to many later Hanafi authorities, as it aligns with the practice of the Companions and general hadiths.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafālah): A contract of guarantee where a person (kafil) assumes liability for a debt or obligation of another (makful 'anhu) to a creditor (makful lahu).
دين (dayn): A debt or financial obligation that is established in the liability (dhimma) of a person.
ساقط (sāqiṭ): Invalidated or lapsed; no longer legally effective. Abu Hanifa argues the debt becomes saqit upon death of an insolvent debtor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), why is a guarantee for the debt of a deceased insolvent debtor valid?
A) Because the debt lapses upon death and the guarantee is for a non-existent debt.
B) Because the debt remains established as none of the causes of invalidation have occurred, and the hadith of Abu Qatadah supports it.
C) Because the guarantee was made voluntarily.
D) Because the debtor had left property.

Key Takeaway
The Sahibayn's view that a guarantee for an insolvent deceased's debt is valid is the stronger position within the Hanafi school, based on the persistence of the debt and prophetic practice.

Kafalah — Surety
advanced
12 min
Kafalah for a Deceased Insolvent Debtor: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafalah for deceased insolvent debtor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on kafalah for a deceased insolvent debtor and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Debt is an action, not property — this changes the ruling on kafalah after death.

This lesson examines a classical intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), on whether a kafalah (suretyship) contract on behalf of a deceased insolvent debtor is valid. The disagreement hinges on the nature of debt (dayn): is it a property obligation or an action (the act of rendition)? Understanding this conceptual split is essential for correctly applying the rules of kafalah in estate and insolvency cases.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Imam Abu Hanifa)
Identify Abu Hanifa's ruling on kafalah for a deceased insolvent debtor and his key premise: debt is an action (fi'l al-ada'), not property.
This is the foundational distinction.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
State that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold the opposite view: kafalah is valid because the debt remains established after death.
The Sahibayn's view is the mufta bihi.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
List the proofs of the Sahibayn: voluntary payment accepted, pre-existing kafalah continues, hadiths (al-za'im gharim, Abu Qatada incident).
These are dalil naqli.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine Abu Hanifa's rational argument: debt as action requires ability; deceased insolvent lacks ability (by self, deputy, or heir); thus debt falls in worldly terms; no debt means no valid kafalah.
Address the objection about property being called debt metaphorically.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
Summarize the practical difference in rulings and conclude which position is relied upon for fatwa (Sahibayn).
The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view due to stronger textual evidence.

حضرت امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ دین اور قرضہ کے کفیل ہونے کی شرط یہ ہے کہ قرضہ اصیل (مکفول عنہ ) کے ذمہ میں ثابت اور قائم ہو حالانکہ اس جگہ یعنی میت مفلس کے ذمہ میں دین ثابت نہیں ہے بلکہ ساقط ہے اور میت کے ذمہ سے دین اس لئے ساقط ہے کہ دین در حقیقت مال نہیں ہوتا بلکہ فعل ادا یعنی ادھار لینے اور مال کا مالک کرنے اور سپرد کرنے کے فعل کا نام دین ہے اور دین چونکہ ا کا نام ہے اس لئے دین وجوب کے ساتھ متصف ہوتا ہے چنانچہ کہا جاتا ہے دین واجب جیسا الصلاة واجبة کہا جاتا ہے کیونکہ واجب ہونا یا مستحب ہو نا فعل کی صفت ہوتا ہے اعیان کی صفت نہیں ہوتا۔ اور وجوب افعال کی صفت اس لئے ہوتا ہے کہ وجوب اس فعل کو خاص کرنے کا نام ہے جو اس بات کا تقاضا کرتا ہو کہ فاعل اس پر خلل واقع کرنے کی صورت میں گناہ اور مزمت کا مستحق ہوگا اور یہ بات افعال میں تو متصور ہوتی ہے لیکن اعیان میں متصور نہیں ہوتی کیونکہ مدیون یعنی میت مفلس نہ بذات خود فعل ادا پر قادر ہے اور نہ اپنے نائب یعنی کفیل کے ساتھ قادر ہے اور نہ اپنے وارث کے ساتھ قادر ہے تو وہ فعل ادا سے بالکل عاجز ہو گیا ہے اور جب مدیون یعنی میت مفلس فعل ادا سے عاجز ہے تو نتیجہ کے طور پر قرض خواہ کا اس سے دین وصول کرنا بھی فوت ہو گیا اور جب قرض خواہ کا اپنا دین وصول کرنا فوت ہو گیا تو ضرورةً احکام دنیا کے اعتبار سے میت کے ذمہ سے دین ساقط ہو جائیگا۔ اور جب احکام دنیا کے حق میں میت مفلس سے دین ساقط ہو گیا تو اس کا کفالہ بھی درست نہ ہوگا کیونکہ بغیر دین کے کفالہ درست نہیں ہوتا پس ثابت ہو گیا کہ میت مفلس کی طرف سے کفالہ صحیح نہیں ہے۔ لكنه في الحكم مال الخ سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ مال بھی وجوب کے ساتھ متصف ہوتا ہے چنانچہ کہا جاتا ہے المال واجب اور فلاں پر ایک ہزار روپے واجب ہیں حالانکہ آپ نے فرمایا ہے کہ وجوب افعال کا وصف ہوتا ہے نہ کہ اعیان کا؟
جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ مایو ول کے اعتبار سے مال بھی مجاز آدین کہلاتا ہے اس لئے مال بھی وجوب کے ساتھ موصوف ہو جاتا ہے یعنی دین تو فعل اداری کا نام ہے مگر چونکہ انجام کار کے اعتبار سے فعل ادا کے نتیجہ میں مال حاصل ہوتا ہے اس لئے مجاز و جوب کے ساتھ بھی موصوف کرنے میں کوئی مضائقہ نہیں ہے۔
Book of Kafalah. Either the creditor discharges the debtor, or the debtor pays the debt, or the cause of the debt becomes null. The death of the debtor does not bring about any of these three things, because the case is assumed when none of these have occurred. When none of the three things that cancel the debt have occurred, the debt is not canceled from the deceased's liability. The debt remains established and outstanding on the deceased. Since none of these canceling events occurred, the debt remains on the deceased in the Hereafter, so that the debtor will be punished and be sinful for not paying. Thus the debt remains on the deceased in worldly matters as well. One proof that the debt is established on the deceased is that if someone voluntarily pays the debt of the deceased, it is valid. If the insolvent debtor were discharged by death, it would not be permissible for the creditor to take that payment (as payment of the debt). The fact that the creditor may take it indicates that the debt is established on the deceased. Similarly, if there is a previous kafalah, it remains after death, and the debt is collected from the estate by consensus. If death canceled the debt, then release from the kafil would also be necessary, because the payment of the debt by the kafil necessitates the discharge of the kafil, yet the kafil's liability for the debt is not canceled. This shows the debt is established. Also, if the deceased has any estate, the debt is collected from it. So the debt remains established. Therefore, kafalah for such an established debt is valid. The Prophet said: 'Al-Za'im gharim' (the guarantor is liable). This hadith is general, so this kafalah is valid. The school of the Sahibayn is supported by hadiths, whether kafalah occurred during the debtor's life or after death, both cases are valid. Second hadith: He came to the funeral of an Ansari and asked: 'Is there any debt on your companion?' They said: 'Yes, two dirhams or two dinars.' He said: 'Pray for your companion.' Abu Qatada stood up and said: 'They are on me (I guarantee them).' Then the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) prayed. If kafalah were not valid, the Prophet would not have prayed despite Abu Qatada's guarantee. But since it was valid and the Prophet was assured of payment, he prayed. Imam Abu Hanifa's evidence: The condition for kafalah of a debt is that the debt is established and ongoing on the asl (principal debtor). Here, the debt is not established on the deceased insolvent; it is canceled. It is canceled because debt (dayn) in reality is not property but the act of rendition (fi'l al-ada'), i.e., the act of transferring ownership and handing over property. Dayn is a name for this act, so it is qualified by obligation (wujub) just as salah is said to be wajib. Obligation is a quality of actions, not of essences ('ayan). Obligation is specific to actions because it entails deserving sin and blame for failure, which applies to actions under human power, not to essences which are not under human power. In this case, besides the act of rendition, there is no action that can be described with obligation. So dayn is not property but the act of rendition. Since dayn is an action, it requires ability (qudrah), because obligation without ability is impossible. The deceased insolvent is incapable of the act of rendition, neither by himself nor by a deputy (kafil) nor by an heir. Thus he is completely unable, so the creditor's right to collect is lost, and necessarily the debt is canceled in worldly terms. Therefore, kafalah is not valid because there is no debt. Objection: Property (mal) is also described as wajib, e.g., 'one thousand rupees are wajib on so-and-so.' Answer: Mal is metaphorically called dayn because ultimately the act of rendition results in property, so calling it wajib metaphorically is acceptable.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of kafalah (suretyship) for a debt owed by a deceased insolvent debtor. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that kafalah is valid, while Imam Abu Hanifa holds it is not. The Sahibayn's position: The debt remains established on the deceased even after death, as none of the debt-canceling events (discharge, payment, nullification of cause) have occurred. Therefore, the debt persists in both worldly and otherworldly matters. They support this with proofs: (1) If someone voluntarily pays the debt of the deceased, the creditor may accept it lawfully, indicating the debt exists. (2) If there was a prior kafalah, it continues after death, and the kafil is not released. (3) The Prophet's hadith 'al-za'im gharim' is general. (4) The incident of Abu Qatada guaranteeing the deceased's debt, after which the Prophet prayed, shows kafalah after death is valid. Imam Abu Hanifa's evidence: The debt (dayn) is not a tangible property but an action—the act of transferring ownership and handing over property. This action requires ability (qudrah) to be obligatory. The deceased insolvent lacks this ability entirely: he cannot perform the act himself, nor through a deputy (kafil), nor through an heir (since the heir is not commanded by Shariah to pay). Therefore, the obligation of the debt falls away in worldly terms, and consequently kafalah, which requires an existing debt, is not valid. The passage also addresses an objection: If dayn is an action, why are debts described as 'obligatory' on a person's 'property'? The answer is that 'mal' (property) is metaphorically called 'dayn' because the ultimate result of the act of rendition is property.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where one person (kafil) guarantees the debt or obligation of another (asl/makful 'anhu) to a creditor (makful lahu).
دين (dayn): A debt or financial obligation; according to Imam Abu Hanifa, it is not property but the act of rendition (fi'l al-ada')—the action of transferring ownership and handing over money.
مفلس (muflis): An insolvent person whose debts exceed his assets. Here, specifically a deceased person with no estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, is kafalah valid for a deceased insolvent debtor?
A) No, because the debt is canceled by death.
B) Yes, because the debt remains established after death.
C) It depends on whether the kafalah was made before or after death.
D) Only if the creditor agrees.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi is that kafalah for a deceased insolvent debtor is valid; the kafil is liable to pay.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
15 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Kafalah for a Deceased Insolvent Debtor
Intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn on kafalah for deceased insolvent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Imam Abu Hanifa's position on kafalah for a deceased insolvent debtor and evaluate the Sahibayn's counterarguments using textual and rational proofs.

Core Idea
Kafalah for a deceased insolvent is invalid because the debt drops; voluntary payment does not imply debt existence.

Within the Hanafi school, a notable disagreement exists between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), regarding the validity of becoming a guarantor (kafil) for a deceased person who died insolvent. This dispute tests a core principle: must a debt be established on the principal debtor at the time of the guarantee? In this lesson, you will work through the arguments of both sides, the proofs cited, and the final relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue and Two Positions
First, clearly state the point of contention: whether kafalah (guarantee) for a deceased insolvent debtor is valid. Note Imam Abu Hanifa's position (invalid) and the Sahibayn's position (valid).
The dispute is not about kafalah during life, but after death when the debtor is insolvent.
2
Analyze the Sahibayn's First Argument and Abu Hanifa's Response
The Sahibayn argue that since voluntary payment (tabarru') on behalf of a deceased insolvent is valid, the debt must be established on the deceased. Abu Hanifa responds that voluntary payment does not require the debt to exist on the principal; it only requires the guarantor's capacity to pay. Work through the example of Khalid and Shahid to understand this.
Focus on the distinction between the debt existing on the principal vs. the guarantor's obligation.
3
Examine the Second Argument: Capacity via Prior Guarantor or Property
The Sahibayn claim that if the deceased had a prior guarantor or estate, there is capacity for the debt to be paid. Abu Hanifa accepts that capacity through a deputy or property makes the debt persist, but if neither exists, the debt drops and kafalah is invalid.
This refines the condition for kafalah: the debt must be 'payable' in some sense at the time of guarantee.
4
Evaluate the Hadith Proofs and Their Interpretations
The Sahibayn cite two hadith: 'az-zaimu gharim' and the story of Abu Qatada and the Ansari. Abu Hanifa interprets both as applying only when the guarantee was made during the principal's lifetime, or as a promise of voluntary payment. Assess how each side reads the same texts.
Note that Abu Hanifa does not reject the hadith but restricts their application.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence and Ruling
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is: if someone becomes a kafil after death, under Abu Hanifa the kafil owes nothing; under Sahibayn, the kafil must pay. The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's view.
This is the operative rule for fatwa today.

كتاب الكفالة
والتبرع لا يعتمد قيام الدين الخ سے صاحبین کے مستدلات کا جواب ہے چنانچہ فرمایا کہ میت مفلس کی طرف سے تبرع الدین ادا کرنے کا صحیح ہونا اس بات کی دلیل نہیں ہے کہ میت مفلس کے ذمہ دین ثابت نہیں ہے کیونکہ تبرع کرنا اس پر موقوف نہیں ہوتا ہے کہ مکفول عنہ کے حق میں دین موجود ہو بلکہ اس پر موقوف ہوتا ہے کہ خود تبرع کرنے والے یعنی کفیل کے حق میں دین موجود ہو چنانچہ اگر خالد نے کہا کہ حامد کا شاہد پر ایک ہزار روپیہ قرضہ ہے اور میں اسکا کفیل ہوں اور مکفول عنہ یعنی شاہد نے انکار کیا تو یہ کفالہ درست ہوگا اور کفیل یعنی خالد پر ایک ہزار روپیہ ادا کرنا واجب ہوگا حالانکہ یہاں دین بالکل موجود نہیں ہے پس معلوم ہوا کہ تبرع کرنے کے لئے مکفول عنہ کے ذمہ دین کا ثابت ہونا ضروری نہیں ہے اور جب تبرع کرنے کے لئے مکفول عنہ کے ذمہ دین کا ثابت ہونا ضروری نہیں ہے تو میت مفلس کی طرف سے تبرع الدین ادا کرنے کا صحیح ہونا اس بات کی دلیل نہیں ہوگا کہ میت مفلس کے ذمہ دین ثابت ہے اور صاحبین کے دوسرے مستدل و کذا یبقی اذا كان به كفیل او مال کا جواب یہ ہے کہ قدرت فعل ادا کی شرط ہے خواہ مکفول عنہ بذات خود قادر ہو خواہ اپنے خلیفہ اور نائب کے ذریعہ قادر ہو پس اگر میت کا پہلے سے كفیل موجود ہو یا میت کا مال متروکہ موجود ہو تو میت یعنی مکفول عنہ اگر چہ بذات خود فعل ادا پر قادر نہیں ہے لیکن اپنے خلیفہ یعنی كفیل یا مال متروکہ کے ذریعہ فعل ادا پر قادر ہے بایں طور کہ اگر كفیل موجود ہو تو دین كفیل سے وصول کیا جائے گا اور اگر كفیل موجود نہ ہو بلکہ متروکہ موجود ہو تو ترکہ کے مال سے دین وصول کیا جائے گا پس جب ان دونوں صورتوں میں قدرت پائی گئی تو میت مفلس پر ان دونوں صورتوں میں دین (فعل ادا) ثابت ہوگا اور جس صورت میں نہ پہلے سے میت کا كفیل ہو اور نہ اس کے پاس مال موجود ہو تو اس صورت میں میت نہ بذات خود قادر ہوگا اور نہ اپنے خلیفہ کے ذریعہ قادر ہوگا اور جب میت کو کسی طریقہ سے قدرت حاصل نہ ہوئی تو اس کے ذمہ میں دین ثابت نہ ہوگا بلکہ ساقط ہوگا اور دین ساقط کا کفالہ چونکہ صحیح نہیں ہوتا۔ اس لئے اس صورت میں کفالہ درست نہ ہوگا۔
بعض حضرات نے اس جواب کی تقریر یوں کی ہے کہ میت کا پہلے سے كفیل ہو تو میت اپنے خلیفہ یعنی كفیل کے ذریعہ فعل ادا پر قادر شمار ہوگا ۔ اور اگر كفیل پہلے سے نہ ہو بلکہ میت کے پاس مال ہو تو میت اس طرح قادر شمار ہوگا کہ مال متروکہ کے ذریعہ فعل ادا تک پہنچا جا سکتا ہے یعنی مال متروکہ سے میت کا دین ادا کیا جا سکتا ہے لیکن اس صورت میں یہ کہنا پڑے گا کہ فعل ادا کی شرط یہ ہے کہ مکفول عنہ فعل ادا پر بذات خود قادر ہو یا اپنے خلیفہ کے ذریعہ قادر ہو یا اس چیز کے ذریعہ قادر ہو جو اس کو ادا تک پہنچا دے یعنی مال موجود ہو جس کے ذریعہ دین ادا کیا جائے گا۔
حضرت امام ابوحنیفہ کی طرف صاحبین کی پیش کردہ حدیث الزعیم غارم کا جواب یہ ہے کہ یہ حدیث اس پر دلالت کرتی ہے کہ كفیل اُس چیز کا ضامن ہوگا جس کا وہ كفیل ہوا ہے حالانکہ میت مفلس کی طرف سے بعد الموت كفیل کے حق میں ابھی یہی کلام ہے کہ وہ ضامن ہوا بھی ہے یا نہیں، چنانچہ میت مفلس کا كفیل امام صاحب کے نزدیک ضامن نہیں ہوتا۔ پس جب میت مفلس کے كفیل کا ضامن ہونا ثابت نہیں ہوا تو حدیث الزعيم غارم کو اس صورت پر محمول کیا جائے گا جبکہ مکفول عنہ کی زندگی میں كفیل ہوا ہو۔ اور جب یہ حدیث اس پر محمول ہے تو حضرت امام صاحب کے خلاف حجت نہ ہوگی۔ اور حدیث انصاری کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ابو قتادہ کا قول هما علی کفالہ سابقہ کا اقرار ہے یعنی جب انصاری کے مدیون ہونے کی وجہ سے رسول اللہ صلى الله عليه وسلم نماز جنازہ سے رک گئے تو ابو قتادہ نے فرمایا کہ اللہ کے رسول میں اسکے قرضہ کا اسکی حیات ہی سے كفیل ہوں لہذا آپ نماز پڑھائیے۔ یہ سن کر رسول صلى الله عليه وسلم نے انصاری کی نماز ادا فرمائی پس اس حدیث سے یہ ثابت ہوا کہ مکفول عنہ کی زندگی میں اگر کوئی شخص اس کا كفیل ہو گیا تو مکفول عنہ کے مرنے کے بعد بھی کفالہ باقی رہے گا اور اس کے جواز کے ہم بھی قائل ہیں نہ یہ کہ اگر مکفول عنہ کے مرنے کے بعد كفیل ہوا تو کفالہ درست ہوگا۔ اور یہ بھی احتمال ہے کہ ابو قتادہ کا قول هما علی میت کی طرف سے تبرع الدین ادا کرنے کا وعدہ ہو۔ اور تبرع الدین کی ادائیگی کے جواز کے ہم بھی قائل ہیں پس مذکورہ احتمالات کے ہوتے ہوئے یہ حدیث صاحبین کا مستدل کیسے ہو سکتی ہے۔
Book of Kafalah. The argument that voluntary payment does not depend on the existence of debt is a response to the proofs of the Sahibayn. Thus it is said that the validity of paying a debt voluntarily on behalf of a deceased insolvent is not proof that the debt is established on the deceased, because voluntary payment does not require that the debt exist in favor of the principal (makful anhu), but rather requires that the debt exist in favor of the guarantor (kafil) himself. For example, if Khalid said that Hamid has a debt of one thousand upon Shahid, and I am his guarantor, and the principal Shahid denies, then the kafalah is valid and the guarantor Khalid is obliged to pay one thousand, even though the debt does not exist at all. Thus it is known that for voluntary payment, the existence of debt on the principal is not necessary. And when it is not necessary, the validity of voluntary payment on behalf of a deceased insolvent is not proof that the debt is established on him. As for the second proof of the Sahibayn, 'and likewise it remains if he has a guarantor or property,' the response is that capacity to perform the act is a condition, whether the principal is capable himself or through his deputy and agent. If the deceased had a guarantor previously or his estate exists, then the deceased (the principal) though not capable himself, is capable through his deputy (the guarantor) or through the estate: if a guarantor exists, the debt will be collected from him; if not but estate exists, from the estate. Thus in these two cases capacity is found, so the debt (of performance) is established upon the deceased insolvent. But if neither a prior guarantor nor property exists, the deceased is not capable in any way, and since he has no capacity, the debt is not established but rather drops. Kafalah for a dropped debt is not valid, so in that case kafalah is not correct. Some people have explained this response: if the deceased had a guarantor, he is considered capable through his deputy; if not but property exists, he is considered capable through the estate that can deliver the payment. This requires that the condition for performance is that the principal be capable himself, or through his deputy, or through something that leads to performance (i.e., existing property). As for the hadith presented by the Sahibayn against Imam Abu Hanifa, 'az-zaimu gharim' (the guarantor is liable), the response is that this hadith indicates that the guarantor will be liable for what he guaranteed, but regarding a guarantor for a deceased insolvent after death, the question is whether he is liable at all. According to Imam Saheb, the guarantor of a deceased insolvent is not liable. Since his liability is not established, the hadith is interpreted to apply when the guarantee occurred during the life of the principal. When construed thus, it is not evidence against Imam Saheb. As for the hadith of the Ansari, the response is that Abu Qatada's statement 'huma alayya' was an acknowledgment of a prior guarantee. When the Prophet (peace be upon him) withheld from the funeral prayer because of the Ansari's debt, Abu Qatada said, 'O Messenger of Allah, I am his guarantor for his debt from his lifetime, so pray.' Hearing this, the Prophet performed the prayer. This proves that if someone becomes a guarantor during the principal's life, the guarantee remains after his death, and we accept its validity. It does not prove that a guarantee after death is valid. Another possibility is that Abu Qatada's statement was a promise to voluntarily pay on behalf of the deceased, and we also accept the validity of voluntary payment. With these possibilities, how can this hadith be a proof for the Sahibayn?
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of kafalah (guarantee) for a deceased insolvent debtor. The core issue: if a person becomes a guarantor for a deceased person who died insolvent, is the guarantee valid? Imam Abu Hanifa holds it is invalid, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, known as Sahibayn) hold it is valid. The text begins by refuting the Sahibayn's first argument: they argued that since it is permissible to voluntarily pay a debt on behalf of a deceased insolvent (tabarru'), this shows the debt is established on the deceased, making kafalah possible. Abu Hanifa responds that voluntary payment does not require the debt to exist on the principal; rather it requires the guarantor's capacity to pay. He gives an example: if Khalid says Hamid owes Shahid 1000 and he becomes a guarantor, but Shahid denies the debt, the kafalah is still valid because Khalid is voluntarily undertaking the obligation. Thus voluntary payment does not prove debt existence on the deceased. The second proof of the Sahibayn: the deceased's capacity to pay exists if he has a prior guarantor or property. Abu Hanifa responds that capacity for performance is necessary; if the deceased has a prior guarantor or estate, he is deemed capable through them, so kafalah can be valid. But if neither exists, the debt drops and kafalah is invalid. The text then addresses two hadith proofs of the Sahibayn. First, the hadith 'az-zaimu gharim' (the guarantor is liable). Abu Hanifa says this applies when the guarantee is valid; but in this case, the guarantee itself is in question, so it cannot be used to prove validity. Second, the hadith of the Ansari: Abu Qatada guaranteed the debt of a deceased man during his lifetime, and the Prophet performed the funeral prayer. This shows that a guarantee made during life remains after death, but not that a guarantee after death is valid. Alternatively, it could be interpreted as a promise of voluntary payment. Thus the Sahibayn's proofs are refuted, and the position of Imam Abu Hanifa is upheld.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Guarantee or surety where the kafil undertakes to fulfill an obligation (usually a debt) of the makful anhu.
كفيل (kafil): The guarantor who assumes liability for the debt of another.
مكفول عنه (makful anhu): The principal debtor on whose behalf the guarantee is given.
تبرع (tabarru'): Voluntary payment or gift; here it refers to paying a debt without legal obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the primary reason that kafalah for a deceased insolvent debtor is invalid?
A) The kafil does not have the financial capacity
B) The debt is not established on the deceased at the time of kafalah
C) The heirs of the deceased must consent
D) The kafalah must be in writing

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's position that kafalah requires an existing debt at contract time is the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Guarantor's Right to Retain Money from Debtor
Ruling on kafil's right to retain money

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a debtor's right to reclaim money given to a guarantor before payment and apply the qiyas (analogy) to zakat alms.

Core Idea
The guarantor's right attaches to the money due to possible payment, blocking debtor's revocation.

In kafalah, once the debtor hands over the debt amount to the guarantor before the guarantor pays the creditor, a legal attachment (ta'alluq) occurs. This passage from al-Inayah explains why the debtor cannot take the money back, even though the guarantor has not yet paid the creditor. Understanding this ruling prevents common misunderstandings in guarantee transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what action is being ruled upon: the debtor's attempt to reclaim money given to the guarantor. The ruling is that the debtor has no right to reclaim.
The ruling is valid (sahih) and mufta bihi.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
Find the textual proof: the passage uses qiyas (analogy) to zakat paid in advance to the collector (sa'i).
The qiyas is jali (clear).
3
Trace the illa (effective cause)
Identify the common cause: the possibility (ihtimal) that the recipient (guarantor/collector) may have fulfilled the obligation. This possibility creates a right in the recipient that prevents revocation.
The illa is 'the attachment of right due to possibility of payment'.
4
Contrast with agency (risalah)
Note the exception: if the debtor gives the money as an agent to deliver, it remains an amanah (trust) and the debtor can revoke. This confirms that the ruling hinges on the context of guarantee, not mere possession.
Key distinction: guarantee vs. agency.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Given a similar scenario (e.g., guarantor receives money but later debtor claims refund), apply the same illa to conclude the debtor cannot reclaim if there is possibility of payment.
The ruling is limited to guarantee contracts.
