مکفول لہ کا پیش کردہ بینہ قبول کر لیا جائے گا اس کے برخلاف گذشتہ مسئلہ میں کہ اس میں دعوی اور مدعی بہ (مکفول بہ) کے درمیان مطابقت نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے بینہ قبول نہ ہوگا۔
اشرف الہدایہ جلد نہم
صاحب بدایہ انما یختلف بالامر وعدمه لا نهما یتغایران الخ سے کفالہ بالامر اور کفالہ بغیر الامر کے درمیان اختلاف حکم کی وجہ بیان کرتے ہیں چنانچہ فرماتے ہیں کہ کفالہ بالامر کی صورت میں قاضی کا حکم کفیل اور مکفول عنہ دونوں پر نافذ ہوگا اگرچہ قضاء علی الغائب ناجائز ہے اور کفالہ بغیر الامر کی صورت میں فقط کفیل پر نافذ ہوگا اور مکفول عنہ جو غائب ہے اس پر نافذ نہ ہوگا وجہ یہ ہے کہ کفالہ بالامر اور کفالہ بغیر الامر دونوں باہم متغایر ہیں۔ اس طور پر کہ کفالہ بالامر ابتدا تبرع ہے اور انتہاء معاوضہ ہے اور کفالہ بغیر الامر ابتداء بھی تبرع ہے اور انتہاء بھی تبرع ہے پس جب کفالہ بالامر اور کفالہ بغیر الامر کے درمیان تغایر ثابت ہو گیا تو مکفول لہ کے ان دونوں میں سے ایک کا دعوی کرنے سے اس کے لئے دوسرے کا فیصلہ نہ کیا جائے گا کیونکہ حاکم اسی سبب کے ساتھ فیصلہ کرتا ہے جس سبب سے مدعی دعوی کرتا ہے چنانچہ مدعی نے اگر ملک کا دعوی خرید سے کیا یعنی یہ کہا کہ میں اس چیز کا مالک اس لئے ہوں کہ میں نے اس کو خریدا ہے تو حاکم کے لئے یہ جائز نہ ہوگا کہ وہ ہبہ کے ذریعہ مالک ہونے کا فیصلہ کرے۔ اگرچہ خرید اور ہبہ دونوں کا حکم ایک ہے۔ یعنی دونوں کا حکم ملک ہے پس جب مدعی یعنی مکفول لہ نے کفالہ بالامر کا دعویٰ کیا اور قاضی نے بینہ کے ساتھ کفالہ بالامر کا فیصلہ کیا تو یہ ثابت ہو گیا کہ مکفول عنہ نے کفیل کو کفالت کرنے کا حکم دیا ہے اور کفالہ کا حکم دینا اس بات کو متضمن ہے کہ مکفول عنہ نے مکفول لہ کے واسطے مال کا اقرار کیا ہے کیونکہ مکفول عنہ کفیل کو مال ادا کرنے کا حکم اسی وقت کرے گا جبکہ وہ مکفول لہ کے واسطے مال کا اقرار کرے اور جب مکفول عنہ کا مال کا اقرار کرنا ثابت ہو گیا تو یہ اقرار بھی قاضی کے فیصلہ کے تحت داخل ہو گا گویا قاضی نے حکم دیدیا کہ مکفول عنہ نے اس مدعی یعنی مکفول لہ کے واسطے مال کا اقرار کیا ہے۔ اب اس فیصلہ کے بعد اگر مکفول عنہ غائب حاضر ہو گیا تو اس کے خلاف دوبارہ بینہ پیش کرنے کی ضرورت نہیں ہے اور اگر مکفول لہ نے کفالہ بغیر امرہ کا دعوی کیا یعنی یہ کہا کہ فلاں پر میرا اس قدر مال ہے اور یہ شخص بغیر اس کے حکم کے اس کا کفیل ہے تو یہ کفالہ مکفول عنہ (غائب) کی جانب کو نہیں لگتا۔ یعنی اس صورت میں مال کے سلسلہ میں قاضی کا فیصلہ فقط کفیل پر نافذ ہوگا اور مکفول عنہ پر نافذ نہ ہوگا اور کفیل پر مال لازم ہونے سے مکفول عنہ پر مال کا واجب ہونا لازم نہیں آتا چنانچہ اگر کسی نے یہ کہا کہ فلاں کا فلاں پر ایک ہزار درہم ہے اور میں اس کا کفیل ہوں تو کفیل پر مال واجب ہوگا اگرچہ مکفول عنہ پر واجب نہیں ہوتا دلیل یہ ہے کہ کفالہ بغیر امرہ کی صحت اس پر موقوف ہے کہ کفیل کے گمان میں دین موجود ہو یعنی صرف کفیل کا گمان ہو کہ مکفول عنہ پر مکفول لہ کا دین ہے اور ایک آدمی کا گمان چونکہ دوسرے پر لازم نہیں ہوتا اس لئے یہ دین کفیل سے مکفول عنہ کی طرف متعدی نہ ہوگا اور انسان چونکہ اپنے گمان میں ماخوذ ہوتا ہے اس لئے یہ مال کفیل سے لیا جائے گا۔
وفي الكفالة بامره يرجع الكفيل .. الخ سے صاحب بدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر کفالہ، مکفول عنہ کے حکم سے ہو تو کفیل مکفول عنہ کی طرف سے جو کچھ ادا کرے گا اس کو مکفول عنہ سے رجوع کر سکتا ہے اور حضرت امام زفر نے فرمایا کہ مذکورہ مسئلہ میں کفالہ بامرہ کے باوجود کفیل کو مکفول عنہ سے رجوع کرنے کا کوئی حق نہیں ہے۔ امام زفر کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مذکورہ مسئلہ میں مکفول لہ نے بینہ کے ذریعہ کفالہ ثابت کیا ہے اور بینہ سے ثابت کرنے کی ضرورت اس وقت پیش آتی ہے جبکہ مدعی علیہ منکر ہو پس معلوم ہوا کہ کفیل، کفالہ کا منکر ہے اور جب کفیل کفالہ کا منکر ہے اور مکفول لہ نے بینہ کے ذریعہ کفالہ کو ثابت کیا ہے تو گویا کفیل کے خیال کے مطابق مکفول لہ نے کفیل پر ظلم کیا ہے اور مظلوم کو کسی دوسرے پر ظلم کرنے کا حق نہیں ہوتا لہذا کفیل کو یہ حق نہ ہوگا کہ وہ مکفول عنہ سے مقدار ما ادی رجوع کر کے اس پر ظلم کرے
Book of Suretyship. It is a donation at beginning and end, so if one of them raises a claim, judgment is not given for him on the other. And if judgment is given with the order, his order is established, and it includes the acknowledgment of the debt, so he becomes a judgment debtor. And the suretyship without his order does not affect his side because its validity depends on the existence of the debt in the surety's belief, so it does not extend to him. And in suretyship with his order, the surety recovers from the principal what he paid. Zufar said: He does not recover, because when he denied, he wronged his belief, so he should not wrong another. We say: He has been legally rebutted, so what was in his belief is nullified. Translation: And if someone establishes evidence that so-and-so owes him such-and-such money, and this person is surety on his behalf with his order, then judgment on that money will be given against both the surety and the principal. And if the suretyship is without his order, then judgment will be given specifically against the surety, and the evidence is accepted because the subject of suretyship is absolute money, unlike the previous case. And the difference in judgment being effective or not is because these two are mutually different: suretyship with order is a donation at the beginning and a compensation at the end, while suretyship without order is a donation at both beginning and end. So if he claims one of these two, a judgment on the other type will not be given for him. And when judgment is given for suretyship with order, the ruling against the principal is established, and this includes acknowledgment of the money, so this acknowledgment becomes a judgment against the principal. And suretyship without order does not attach to the principal's side because the validity of suretyship depends on the existence of the debt in the surety's belief, so the debt does not extend to the principal. And in suretyship with order, the surety will take back from the principal what he paid. Imam Zufar said he will not take back, because when the surety denied, he wronged his own belief, so he should not wrong others. And we say that the mentioned surety has been legally rebutted, so whatever was in his belief is nullified. Elaboration of the case: If a person, for example Hamid, becomes surety on behalf of Khalid with his order for a deferred debt, then the principal Khalid goes missing, then the creditor Shahid claims in the qadi's court and presents evidence that the missing person Khalid owes him such amount, and this Hamid is surety on his behalf with his order, then the qadi's ruling on the mentioned money will be binding on both the surety and the principal, meaning that the ruling on the present surety will also apply to the absent principal. And if the suretyship was not with the principal's order but without, then the ruling on the money will be only against the surety, and the qadi's ruling will not be binding on the absent principal. The conclusion is that in this case, whether the suretyship is with order or without, the creditor's evidence will be accepted. The proof is that in this case the subject of suretyship is absolute money because the surety did not restrict it with 'already judged' or 'to be judged', so it is absolute, and the creditor's claim is also absolute, so the claim is valid due to correspondence. And when the claim is valid, the evidence is accepted. This differs from the previous case where the claim and the subject did not correspond. Ashraf al-Hidayah volume nine. The author of al-Bidayah says: The difference only lies in the order and its absence, because they are different... etc. He explains the reason for the difference in ruling between kafalah bi-amr and kafalah bi-ghayr amr. He says that in the case of kafalah bi-amr, the qadi's ruling is binding on both the surety and the principal, even though judgment against an absent person is not permissible, while in the case of kafalah bi-ghayr amr, it is only binding on the surety and not on the absent principal. The reason is that both are mutually different: kafalah bi-amr is a donation at the beginning and a compensation at the end, while kafalah bi-ghayr amr is a donation at both the beginning and the end. So when difference is established, if the creditor claims one, judgment cannot be given on the other, because the ruler decides based on the same cause on which the claimant based his claim. For example, if the claimant claims ownership by purchase, the ruler cannot decide by gift, even though both result in ownership. So when the creditor claimed kafalah bi-amr and the qadi decided on it with evidence, it is established that the principal ordered the surety, and ordering the suretyship implies acknowledgment of the debt by the principal. This acknowledgment becomes part of the qadi's ruling, so if the absent principal later appears, no further evidence is needed. If the creditor claimed kafalah bi-ghayr amr, it does not attach to the principal, so the ruling is only on the surety, and the debt does not become obligatory on the principal. The proof is that the validity of kafalah bi-ghayr amr depends on the existence of the debt in the surety's belief, and one person's belief is not binding on another, so the debt does not extend to the principal. Since a person is held accountable for his own belief, the money is taken from the surety. And from 'In kafalah bi-amr, the surety recovers...' the author of al-Bidayah says: If the suretyship is with the principal's order, the surety can recover from the principal what he paid. Imam Zufar said that even in this case, the surety has no right of recourse against the principal. His proof: The creditor established the suretyship through evidence, which is only needed when the defendant denies it, so the surety is a denier. Since he is a denier, according to his belief the creditor wronged him, and a wronged person cannot wrong another, so the surety cannot wrong the principal by claiming recourse. The Hanafi response: The surety has been legally rebutted, so his belief is invalidated.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Kafalah (Book of Suretyship) deals with two related issues: (1) the effect of a qadi's judgment when a creditor presents evidence that a surety is bound either with or without the principal's order, and (2) the surety's right of recourse against the principal after payment, including a dispute with Imam Zufar. The passage is an expansion (sharh) on the matn of al-Hidayah, explaining the legal reasoning. First, the ruling: If the surety is with the principal's order (kafalah bi-amr), and the creditor presents evidence, the qadi's judgment binds both the surety (present) and the absent principal. This is despite the general rule that judgment against an absent person is not allowed. The reason is that the principal's order implies his acknowledgment of the debt, and the judgment includes that acknowledgment. If the suretyship is without order (kafalah bi-ghayr amr), the judgment binds only the surety, not the principal. The distinction hinges on the nature of the contract: kafalah bi-amr begins as a donation (the surety volunteers) but ends as a compensated transaction (the surety can reclaim), while kafalah bi-ghayr amr is a pure donation from start to finish. Therefore, a claim for one type cannot be ruled under the other, as the qadi decides based on the specific cause pleaded. The proof for accepting the creditor's evidence in both cases is that the subject of suretyship (the money) is absolute (mutlaq), not restricted to a past or future judgment, and the claim is also absolute, so they match. This is unlike a previous case where the money was restricted. Second, the dispute on recourse: In kafalah bi-amr, the surety can recover what he paid from the principal. Imam Zufar argues that since the surety denied the suretyship (which is why evidence was needed), he believed himself not liable, so the creditor's proof 'wronged' his belief. A wronged person cannot wrong another, so the surety cannot seek recourse. The Hanafi response is that the surety's denial is legally overridden; he is legally rebutted (mukadhdhab shar'an) by the evidence, so his belief is irrelevant. Thus, the surety still has the right of recourse.

Key Terms
کفالہ (kafalah): A contract of suretyship where a person (kafil) guarantees the debt or obligation of another (makful 'anhu) to a creditor (makful lahu).
کفالہ بالامر (kafalah bi-amrihi): Suretyship undertaken with the explicit order or authorization of the principal (makful 'anhu).
رجوع (ruju'): Recourse; the surety's right to recover from the principal the amount paid to the creditor.
مكذب شرعا (mukadhdhab shar'an): Legally rebutted; a person whose claim or denial is overridden by legal proof (bayyinah).
ظلم (zulm): Wrongdoing or injustice; in this context, the surety's perception that the creditor wronged him by proving the suretyship despite his denial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority, why does a surety in kafalah bi-amr retain the right of recourse even after denying the suretyship?
A) Because the surety's denial is irrelevant once he pays.
B) Because the surety is legally rebutted (mukadhdhab shar'an) by the evidence, so his belief is nullified.
C) Because the principal must always reimburse the surety regardless of the circumstances.
D) Because the surety's denial automatically transfers the debt to the principal.

Key Takeaway
Even if a surety denies suretyship, evidence establishes the principal's liability, so the surety retains the right to recover from the principal in kafalah bi-amr.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
10 min
Kafalah bil-Amr vs. Kafalah bila-Amr: The Right of Recourse and the Intrạ-Hanafi Dispute
Explains the legal distinction between two types of kafalah and the effect on judgment and recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between kafalah bil-amr and kafalah bila amr, and explain the Hanafi majority position on the surety's right of recourse including Imam Zufar's dissent and the reasoning behind the tarjih.

Core Idea
In kafalah bil-amr, the judge's ruling can bind the absent principal; the surety's right of recourse is upheld by the majority despite initial denial.

This passage from al-Hidayah clarifies the essential distinction between two types of suretyship: one with the principal's command (kafalah bil-amr) and one without (kafalah bila amr). The distinction has practical effects on the judge's ruling and the surety's ability to recover what he paid. The passage then delves into an internal Hanafi disagreement on whether a surety who initially denied the kafalah can still seek recourse after being proven bound by the creditor's evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
In kafalah bil-amr, the judge's ruling applies to both surety and absent principal. The surety can recourse against the principal for what he paid.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) holds that in the same scenario, the surety cannot recourse.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Zufar argues: since the creditor proved by evidence because the surety denied, the surety is considered wronged; a wronged person cannot wrong another by taking recourse.
The surety's denial is key.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The majority respond: the judge's ruling legally contradicts the surety's denial, so his claim of being wronged is null. The ruling proves the principal's debt and order, permitting recourse.
The authoritative ruling overrides subjective belief.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, if the surety denied and the creditor proved, the surety's right of recourse depends on this dispute. The majority say yes; Zufar says no. The mufta bihi is the majority view.
The practical consequence is whether the surety can recover the money.

مال ہے اور یہ شخص بغیر اس کے حکم کے اس کا کفیل ہے تو یہ کفالہ مکفول عنہ ( غائب) کی جانب کو نہیں لگتا۔ یعنی اس صورت میں مال کے سلسلہ میں قاضی کا فیصلہ فقط کفیل پر نافذ ہوگا اور مکفول عنہ پر نافذ نہ ہوگا اور کفیل پر مال لا زم ہونے سے مکفول عنہ پر مال کا واجب ہونا لازم نہیں آتا چنانچہ اگر کسی نے یہ کہا کہ فلاں کا فلاں پر ایک ہزار درہم ہے اور میں اس کا کفیل ہوں تو کفیل پر مال واجب ہوگا اگر چہ مکفول وفي الكفالة بامره يرجع الكفيل .. الخ سے صاحب بدائیہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر کفالہ، مکفول عنہ کے حکم سے ہو تو کفیل مکفول عنہ کی طرف سے جو کچھ ادا کرے گا اس کو مکفول عنہ سے رجوع کر سکتا ہے اور حضرت امام زفر نے فرمایا کہ مذکورہ مسئلہ میں کفالہ بامرہ کے باوجود کفیل کو مکفول عنہ سے رجوع کرنے کا کوئی حق نہیں ہے۔
امام زفر کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مذکورہ مسئلہ میں مکفول لہ نے بینہ کے ذریعہ کفالہ ثابت کیا ہے اور بعینہ سے ثابت کرنے کی ضرورت اس وقت پیش آتی ہے جبکہ مدعی علیہ منکر ہو پس معلوم ہوا کہ کفیل ، کفالہ کا منکر ہے اور جب کفیل کفالہ کا منکر ہے اور مکفول لہ نے بینہ کے ذریعہ کفالہ کو ثابت کیا ہے تو گویا کفیل کے خیال کے مطابق مکفول لہ نے کفیل پر ظلم کیا ہے اور مظلوم کو کسی دوسرے پر ظلم کرنے کا حق نہیں ہوتا البدائنیل کو یہ حق نہ ہوگا کہ وہ مکفول عنہ سے مقدار ما ادی رجوع کر کے اس پر ظلم
لیکن ہماری طرف سے اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ جب قاضی نے کفیل کے خلاف فیصلہ دے کر کفالہ ثابت کر دیا تو کفیل اپنے اس قول میں کہ کفیل نہیں ہوں شرعاً مکذب ہو گیا ہے یعنی شرعاً کفیل کی تکذیب کر دی گئی ہے اور جب شرعاً کفیل کی تکذیب کر دی گئی تو جو کچھ میں کے گمان میں تھا یعنی اس کا مظلوم ہونا وہ بھی باطل ہو گیا اور جب کفیل کا گمان باطل ہو گیا تو یہ ثابت ہوگا کہ مغول عنہ پر مال تھا اور کفیل نے اس کے حکم سے کفالت قبول کی ہے اور جب کفیل نے مکفول عنہ کے حکم سے کفالت قبول کی ہے تو دین ادا کرنے کے بعد کفیل کو مکفول عنہ سے رجوع کرنے کا بھی اختیار ہوگا۔
The author of Bidaya states that the difference between kafalah bil-amr and kafalah bila amr is explained. He says: In kafalah bil-amr, the judge's ruling applies to both the surety and the principal debtor, even though judgment against an absentee is not permissible. In kafalah bila amr, it applies only to the surety, not the principal. The reason is they differ: kafalah bil-amr is initially a donation but ends as compensation; kafalah bila amr is donation both initially and finally. So the creditor cannot claim one type and get judgment for the other. The judge judges based on the cause claimed. If the creditor claims kafalah bil-amr and proves it, it is established that the principal ordered the surety, implying acknowledgment of debt. Then if the principal returns, no new proof is needed. If he claims kafalah bila amr, judgment is only against the surety, not the principal. The surety's liability does not imply the principal's. The validity of kafalah bila amr depends on the surety's belief in the debt. One's belief is not binding on another, so the debt does not transfer to the principal. The surety is held to his belief and must pay. In 'wa fi al-kafalah bi'amrihi yarji' al-kafil...' the author says if kafalah is by order, the surety can recourse against the principal. Imam Zufar said no recourse. His proof: The creditor proved by evidence because the surety denied; so the surety considers himself wronged; a wronged person cannot wrong another. Our response: The judge's ruling legally contradicts the surety's denial; his claim of being wronged is invalid. So the principal's debt and order are confirmed, allowing recourse.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the right of recourse (ruju') for a surety in kafalah bil-amr. The author first clarifies the fundamental difference between kafalah bil-amr and kafalah bila amr. In the former, the judge's ruling can affect the absent principal because the order implies the principal's acknowledgment of debt. In the latter, only the surety is bound because it is based on the surety's personal belief. Then the dispute: Imam Zufar argues that if the surety denied the kafalah and the creditor proved it by evidence, the surety is effectively wronged by the judgment, so he cannot then turn around and claim recourse from the principal. The Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) respond that the judgment itself legally establishes that the surety was not wronged; the surety's denial is overruled, and the principal's debt and order are confirmed. Hence the surety can seek reimbursement. The relied-upon position is that the surety does have the right of recourse.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): A contract of suretyship where one person (kafil) assumes liability for a debt or obligation owed by another (makful 'anhu) to a third party (makful lahu).
الأمر (al-amr): The principal debtor's command or authorization for the surety to assume the suretyship.
بغير الأمر (bi-ghayr al-amr): Suretyship undertaken without the principal debtor's command or authorization.
الرجوع (al-ruju'): The surety's right to claim reimbursement from the principal debtor after paying the debt.
مظلوم (mazlum): One who is wronged; in this context, the surety who claims he was wronged by being held liable despite his denial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority, what is the effect of a judge's ruling in kafalah bil-amr when the surety denied the contract and the creditor proved it?
A) The surety is considered wronged and cannot recourse.
B) The ruling overrides the surety's denial and confirms the principal's debt and order, allowing recourse.
C) The principal is automatically released from liability.
D) The surety must pay but cannot recourse unless the principal admits.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi majority holds that a surety in kafalah bil-amr always has the right of recourse, because the judge's ruling legally establishes the principal's debt and command, overriding the surety's denial and any claim of being wronged.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate

Kafalah bil-Darak: Acknowledgment of Seller's Ownership
Explains why kafalah bil-darak implies acknowledgment of seller's ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal effect of kafalah bil-darak and explain why the surety cannot later claim ownership of the property.

Core Idea
Guaranteeing the seller's title constitutes acknowledgment; you cannot later dispute it.

In Hanafi law, a unique form of suretyship known as kafalah bil-darak (guarantee against eviction) serves as more than a mere promise. This passage explains that when a person becomes a surety for the seller, that act is legally equivalent to an acknowledgment (iqrar) that the seller owns the property. Once such an acknowledgment is made, the surety's subsequent claim to ownership is contradictory and therefore invalid. Let's explore the reasoning behind this ruling and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is kafalah bil-darak (guarantee against eviction) for the seller. The ruling: it is treated as an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership.
2
Understand the two scenarios
The guarantee may be conditioned in the sale or not. In both cases, it implies acknowledgment. If conditioned, the sale's completion depends on the surety's acceptance. If not conditioned, the guarantee aims to strengthen the sale and encourage the buyer.
The legal effect is the same in both scenarios.
3
Analyze the legal consequence
Because the surety has acknowledged the seller's ownership, any later claim by the surety contradicts his own admission and is not heard. The surety cannot undo what he has completed.
This is based on the principle that a person cannot contradict his own admission.
4
Apply the ruling to related rights
If the surety is also a pre-emptor (shafi'), his right of pre-emption is lost because his guarantee contradicts his claim to ownership.
The loss of shuf'ah illustrates the practical consequence of the acknowledgment.

قال و من باع دارا وكفل رجل عنه بالدرك فهو تسليم لان الكفالة لو كانت مشروطة في البيع فتمامه بقبوله ثم بالدعوى يسعى فى نقض ماتم من جهته وان لم تكن مشروطة فيه فالمرادبها احكام البيع وترغيب المشترى فيه اذ لايرغب فيه دون الكفالة فنزل منزلة الاقرار بملك البائع

A man sold a house, and a man became a surety for the seller for guarantee (kafala bil-darak); this is an acknowledgment. The author (said): 'Whoever sold a house and a person became a surety for him for guarantee, it is an acknowledgment, because if the guarantee was conditioned in the sale, its completion is by his acceptance, then he seeks by lawsuit to undo what was completed from his side. And if it was not conditioned, the intent is to strengthen the sale and encourage the buyer, since he would not desire it without the guarantee. So it is treated as being equivalent to acknowledgment of the seller's ownership.' Translation: Someone sold a house, and a person became a surety for the seller for guarantee. This is an acknowledgment because if the guarantee was conditioned in the sale, the completion of the sale depends on the surety's acceptance. Then he tries to undo what was completed from his side. If the guarantee was not conditioned, the purpose of the guarantee is to strengthen the sale and encourage the buyer, since the buyer would not desire it without the guarantee. So the guarantee contract is placed in the rank of acknowledgment of the seller's ownership. Explanation: The scenario is that if someone sold a house and a person became a surety for the seller to the buyer, meaning the surety said to the buyer, 'If this house is evicted (claimed by a rightful owner), I am the guarantor for returning the price.' So the surety's statement is an acknowledgment that the house belongs to the seller. After that, if the surety claims against the buyer that he is the owner, his claim will not be heard. Because the guarantee was either conditioned in the sale or not. If conditioned, the completion of the sale depends on the surety's acceptance, so he tries to undo what he completed; that is invalid. If not conditioned, the guarantee strengthens the sale. The surety's later ownership claim contradicts his earlier acknowledgment, so it is not accepted. Also, if the surety is a pre-emptor (shafi'), his right of pre-emption is nullified. And if there was no condition, the purpose of the guarantee is to strengthen the sale and attract the buyer, because...
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage deals with a specific type of suretyship called kafalat bil-darak (guarantee against eviction or defect in title). The ruling is that when a person becomes a surety for the seller in a property sale, this act is legally treated as an acknowledgment (iqrar) that the seller owns the property being sold. Consequently, the surety cannot later claim ownership of that property. The text provides two scenarios: (1) if the guarantee was a condition of the sale, then the sale's completion is contingent upon the surety's acceptance, and the surety cannot later undo what he caused; (2) if the guarantee was not a condition, its purpose is to reassure the buyer, and it still implies acknowledgment. In both cases, the surety's ownership claim is contradictory and invalid. The passage also notes that if the surety would otherwise have a pre-emption right (shuf'ah), it is lost due to this acknowledgment.

Key Terms
الكفالة بالدرك (al-kafalah bil-darak): A guarantee by a third party (kafil) to indemnify the buyer if the purchased property is found to belong to someone else (eviction).
تسليم (taslim): Acknowledgment or acceptance of the seller's ownership; the surety's act is treated as an acknowledgment.
إقرار (iqrar): A legal admission or acknowledgment that the property belongs to the seller.
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption (first refusal) that a co-owner or neighbor has to buy the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) His claim is accepted because he is the surety.
B) His claim is not heard because his guarantee implied acknowledgment of the seller's ownership.
C) It depends on whether the sale was conditional.
D) He can only claim if he was forced.

Key Takeaway
Becoming a surety for the seller's title (kafalah bil-darak) legally binds you as acknowledging the seller's ownership; you cannot later claim ownership yourself.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
6 min
Suretyship as Implied Acknowledgment of Ownership
Ruling that suretyship for defects confirms seller's ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a kafil bil-darak is deemed to have acknowledged the seller's ownership and apply the consequence that the surety cannot later claim ownership.

Core Idea
Guaranteeing against defects is an admission of the seller's title, barring later ownership claims.

In Hanafi fiqh, suretyship for defects (kafalah bil-darak) in a sale is not merely a guarantee; it carries a significant legal implication. When a person becomes a surety guaranteeing to refund the price if the property is reclaimed, that act is treated as an acknowledgment that the seller is the true owner. This ruling, based on rational reasoning (dalil aqli), prevents the surety from later asserting ownership or exercising the right of preemption. In this lesson, we will dissect the reasoning behind this ruling and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
Determine the act (becoming a kafil bil-darak) and the legal consequence: it is considered an acknowledgment (taslim) of the seller's ownership. Note that this is not a separate guarantee but a declaration that binds the surety.
The acknowledgment is implied from the act, not explicit.
2
Analyze the two scenarios
The text examines two cases: (1) suretyship stipulated in the sale contract, and (2) suretyship agreed upon later. In both, the effect is the same: the surety's acceptance completes or strengthens the sale, and later contesting ownership would contradict that.
The ruling applies irrespective of how the suretyship was arranged.
3
Understand the rational proof
The proof is aqli (rational): if the suretyship is stipulated, the sale is completed only by the surety's acceptance; he cannot undo what he himself completed. If not stipulated, the suretyship encourages the buyer, implying a guarantee of the seller's ownership. In both cases, the surety's act is equivalent to an acknowledgment.
This reasoning is based on istihsan and the principle that one's own actions cannot be repudiated.
4
Apply the ruling to related rights
The ruling also nullifies the surety's right of preemption (shuf'ah), because that right is based on a claim of ownership that contradicts the earlier acknowledgment.
Shuf'ah is a separate right, but here it is invalidated due to contradiction.

Source Text

تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے مکان فروخت کیا اور ایک شخص بائع کی طرف سے مشتری کے لئے کفیل بالدرک ہو گیا یعنی کفیل نے مشتری سے کہا کہ اگر یہ مکان مستحق ہو گیا تو [ثمن] واپس کرنے کا ضامن میں ہوں۔ پس کفیل کا یہ کہنا اس بات کو تسلیم کرنا ہے کہ یہ مکان بائع کی ملک ہے اس کے بعد اگر کفیل نے مشتری پر یہ دعوی کیا کہ اس مکان کا مالک میں ہوں تو کفیل کے اس دعوی کی سماعت نہ ہوگی اور اس کی طرف توجہ نہیں کی جائے گی کیونکہ کفالہ [بیع] کے اندر مشروط ہوگا یا مشروط نہ ہو گا یعنی یا تو بائع نے اس شرط کے ساتھ فروخت کیا ہو گا کہ فلاں آدمی مشتری کے لئے کفیل بالدرک ہے اور یا بغیر شرط کے فروخت کیا ہوگا مگر بعد میں کسی نے کفالت قبول کر لی ہوگی۔ پس اگر کفالہ [بیع] کے اندر مشروط ہے تو بیع کا تمام ہونا کفیل کے قبول کرنے پر موقوف ہوگا یعنی جب کفیل ، کفالہ بالدرک قبول کر لے گا۔ تب جا کر [بیع] پوری ہوگی گو یا عقد بیع کو واجب اور لازم کرنے والا کفیل ہے اس کے بعد اگر کفیل یہ دعوی کرے کہ مکان کا مالک میں ہوں اور یہ مکان میری ملک ہے تو گویا وہ اس عقد کو توڑنا چاہتا ہے جو خود اس کی طرف سے پورا کیا گیا تھا اور اس عقد کوتوڑنے کی کوشش کرنا جو خود اس کی طرف سے تمام ہوا ہو باطل ہے اس لئے اس مسئلہ میں کفیل کا یہ دعوی کرنا کہ مکان کا مالک میں ہوں یہ بھی باطل ہے یہی وجہ ہے کہ کفیل اگر اس مسئلہ میں شفیع ہو تو اس کا حق شفعہ باطل ہو جاتا ہے اس کو یوں بھی کہا جا سکتا ہے کہ کفیل نے پہلے بائع کی ملک کا اقرار کیا ہے اور اب اپنی ملک کا دعویٰ کرتا ہے تو اسکے دعوی میں تناقض ہو گیا اور تناقض کی صورت میں دعویٰ قبول نہیں ہوتا اس لئے کفیل کا یہ دعوی کہ مکان کا مالک میں ہوں قبول نہ ہوگا۔
اور اگر کذا عقد بیع میں شرط نہ ہو تو کفالہ کا مقصد عقد بیع کو محکم اور مضبوط کرنا اور مشتری کو اس عقد کی طرف راغب کرنا ہوتا ہے کیونکہ بسا اوقات مبیع کے مستحق ہونے کے اندیشہ سے مشتری اس مبیع کو خریدنے کی طرف رغبت نہیں کرتا ۔ پس کفیل مشتری کو اطمینان دلاتے ہوۓ کہتا ہے کہ تو مکان کو خرید لے اور فکر مت کر یہ مکان بائع ہی کی ملک ہے اور اگر خدانخواستہ کسی نے استحقاق کا دعوی کر کے اس مکان کو لے لیا تو [ثمن] واپس کرنے کا ضامن میں ہوں پس اس صورت میں بھی کفیل کی طرف سے بائع کی ملک کا اقرار پایا گیا اور جو شخص بائع کی ملک کا اقرار کر لے اس کے لئے جائز نہیں کہ وہ اس کے بعد اپنے مالک ہونے کا دعوی کرے۔
حاصل یہ کہ اس صورت میں کفالہ کفیل کی جانب سے بائع کے مالک دار ہونے کے اقرار کے مرتبہ میں ہے اور جب کفالہ کی وجہ سے کفیل نے اس بات کا اقرار کر لیا کہ اس مکان کا مالک بائع ہے تو اس کے بعد کفیل کا یہ دعوی کہ میں مالک ہوں تناقض کی وجہ سے قابل قبول نہ ہوگا۔
A man sold a house, and a man became a surety for defects on behalf of the seller. This is an acknowledgment. He (the author) said: "And whoever sold a house and a man became a surety for defects on his behalf, then it is an acknowledgment, because if the suretyship was stipulated in the sale, its completion is by his acceptance, then by lawsuit he seeks to revoke what was completed from his side; and if it was not stipulated in it, then the intended meaning is the strengthening of the sale and encouraging the buyer, since he would not be encouraged without the suretyship, so it is treated as an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership." Translation: And someone sold a house, and a man became a surety for defects on behalf of the seller, so this is an acknowledgment. Because if the suretyship is stipulated in the sale, then the completion of the sale depends on the surety's acceptance. Then by making a claim, he attempts to break that which was completed from his side. And if the suretyship was not stipulated in the sale, then the purpose of suretyship is to strengthen the sale and encourage the buyer, as the buyer would not be inclined without suretyship. Thus, the contract of suretyship is placed in the position of an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership. Explanation: The scenario is that if someone sold a house and a person became a surety for defects on behalf of the seller to the buyer, meaning the surety said to the buyer: 'If this house becomes subject to a claim, I am the guarantor for returning the price.' So the surety's statement is an acknowledgment that the house belongs to the seller. After that, if the surety claims to the buyer that he is the owner, his claim will not be heard, because the suretyship was either stipulated in the sale or not. If stipulated, the sale's completion depends on the surety's acceptance; thereafter, the surety cannot revoke it. If not stipulated, the suretyship is for strengthening the sale, and the surety's guarantee implies acknowledgment of the seller's ownership. Therefore, the surety cannot later claim ownership, as it contradicts his prior acknowledgment. Also, if the surety had a right of preemption (shuf'ah), it would be invalidated. Conclusion: In this case, suretyship is equivalent to an acknowledgment by the surety of the seller's ownership, so a later claim of ownership is inadmissible due to contradiction.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding a specific type of suretyship called kafalah bil-darak (suretyship for defects). The ruling is that when a person becomes a surety for the seller in a house sale, guaranteeing to return the price if the house is claimed by a rightful owner, this act constitutes an implied acknowledgment (taslim) that the seller is the true owner of the house. Consequently, the surety cannot later claim ownership of the house himself. The commentator explains two scenarios: (1) If the suretyship was stipulated as a condition in the sale contract, then the sale is completed only upon the surety's acceptance. If the surety later claims ownership, he is trying to undo what he himself has completed, which is invalid. (2) If the suretyship was not stipulated but was done later, its purpose is to reassure the buyer about the seller's ownership. The surety's guarantee effectively affirms the seller's ownership, so a subsequent claim to ownership is contradictory and thus unacceptable. The ruling is based on the principle that one cannot simultaneously affirm and deny a fact; the surety's earlier acknowledgment takes precedence. This case also nullifies any right of preemption (shuf'ah) the surety might have had, since that right is based on a false claim of ownership.

Key Terms
كفيل بالدرك (kafil bil-darak): A surety who guarantees that if the sold property is reclaimed by a rightful owner, he will return the price to the buyer.
تسليم (taslim): An acknowledgment or recognition of a fact, here specifically the seller's ownership of the property.
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of preemption, allowing a co-owner or neighbor to buy the sold property. In this case, the surety loses this right if he claims ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a person becomes a kafil bil-darak for the seller in a house sale, what is the legal consequence regarding a later claim of ownership by the surety?
A) The surety can still claim ownership because suretyship is separate from ownership.
B) The surety cannot claim ownership because his suretyship implies acknowledgment of the seller's ownership.
C) The surety can claim ownership only if the suretyship was not stipulated in the sale.
D) The surety can claim ownership only if he returns the price to the buyer first.

Key Takeaway
A kafil bil-darak's guarantee is legally equivalent to an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership, barring any subsequent contradictory claim.

Witnessing a Sale Without Suretyship Does Not Imply Acknowledgment
Ruling that witnessing a sale with seal but no suretyship is not an acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mere testimony (shahadah) and acknowledgment (iqrar) in the context of witnessing a sale, and apply the Hanafi rule that testimony without suretyship does not convey ownership acknowledgment.

Core Idea
Witnessing a sale with a seal is a record, not a confession of the seller's ownership.

In the previous lesson, we saw that becoming a surety (kafil bi al-darak) in a sale implies acknowledgment of the seller's ownership. But what if someone merely witnesses the sale and affixes his seal without undertaking suretyship? The Hanafi school makes a critical distinction: testimony alone does not constitute acknowledgment. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
What is the legal effect of witnessing and sealing a sale without suretyship? The passage states it is not an acknowledgment (taslim).
The witness retains the right to claim ownership later.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is rational: testimony is not stipulated in the sale, and the sale can occur from an owner or a non-owner.
The possibility of a non-owner sale (e.g., agent or usurper) severs the presumption that the witness acknowledges the seller's ownership.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Because a sale is valid even when the seller is not the owner (e.g., a guardian or subsequent ratification), testifying to the transaction does not require the witness to believe the seller is the true owner.
Thus, the testimony is a mere record of the event, not an admission of ownership.
4
Note the exception
If the deed explicitly states that the seller sold while he owned it, and the witness testifies to that specific clause, then it becomes an acknowledgment. But if the witness only testifies to the recital of the contracting parties, it remains mere testimony.
This exception shows that the wording of the testimony matters.

He gave testimony and affixed his seal, but did not undertake suretyship for defects, so this is not an acknowledgment. The scholars said: When it is written in the deed that the seller sold this house while he was its owner, or he sold a binding final sale, and the witness wrote that he is a witness to that, then this is an acknowledgment from the witness, except when the witness wrote the testimony on the acknowledgment of the contracting parties. Explanation: The scenario is that if someone sold a house and a person became a written witness to the sale and signed and affixed his seal, but he did not become a surety for defects, then this is not an acknowledgment from the witness of the seller's ownership. That is, this testimony is not an indication that the witness recognizes the seller as the owner. Consequently, if the witness later claims that he is the owner, his claim will be heard. And if someone else claims ownership, and he testifies to that other's ownership, his testimony will be accepted. The reason is that testimony is neither stipulated in the sale nor an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership. It is not stipulated because testimony is not appropriate for the sale. It is not an acknowledgment because the sale can be from the owner (the owner selling his property) or from someone else (like an agent or a usurper). Thus, testifying to the sale is not an acknowledgment that he sold his own property. And when the witness's testimony...

Commentary
This passage clarifies the distinction between two situations: witnessing a sale with a seal but without becoming a surety, and becoming a surety (as in the previous lesson). The ruling is that merely witnessing and sealing a sale document does not constitute an acknowledgment (taslim) of the seller's ownership. The witness retains the right to claim ownership of the property later, and his testimony about the sale does not prejudice his own claim. The commentator explains that testimony is not the same as suretyship; it is merely a record of the event. The sale could be valid even if the seller is not the owner (e.g., if a guardian or agent sells), so testifying to the transaction does not imply that the witness believes the seller is the true owner. However, an exception is noted: if the deed explicitly states that the seller sold while owning the property, and the witness testifies to that specific statement, then it becomes an acknowledgment. But if the witness only testifies to the recital of the contracting parties (that they agreed), it is not an acknowledgment. This ruling is based on the understanding that testimony is a factual report, not an admission of ownership. The previous case of suretyship is different because the surety's guarantee inherently affirms the seller's ownership.

Key Terms
شهادة (shahadah): Testimony or witnessing of a transaction, which in this context is a written record of the sale with seal.
ختم (khatm): Seal affixed by the witness to the document, indicating formal attestation.
اقرار (iqrar): Admission or acknowledgment of a fact, such as ownership. In this passage, it is distinguished from mere testimony.

Merely witnessing a sale with a seal is not an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership; the witness retains the right to claim ownership unless the deed explicitly states the seller owned the property and the witness testifies to that specific fact.

Distinction Between Testimony and Kafalah bi al-Darak in Acknowledging Ownership
Explaining that mere testimony to a sale does not acknowledge seller's ownership unlike kafalah bi al-Darak

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mere testimony to a sale and kafalah bi al-darak in terms of acknowledging the seller's ownership, and apply the Hanafi rule that testimony alone does not constitute taslim.

Core Idea
Testimony to a sale does not acknowledge seller's ownership; only kafalah bi al-darak does.

In Hanafi fiqh, the act of witnessing a sale is often confused with an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership. However, the school draws a sharp distinction: mere testimony, even if written and sealed, does not imply taslim (acknowledgment). This lesson examines the ruling from al-Hidayah and the reasoning behind it, including the crucial exception when the deed explicitly states the seller's ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
Focus on the act of a person testifying to a sale in writing, affixing his seal, but without undertaking kafalah bi al-darak. The legal effect is that this does not constitute taslim (acknowledgment) of the seller's ownership. The witness remains free to claim ownership himself.
Taslim here means acknowledgment of the seller's ownership.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The dalil is a rational proof based on the nature of sale and testimony: 'لان الشهادة لا تكون مشروطة في البيع ولا هی اقرارا باللك لان البيع مرة يوجد من المالك وتارةً من غيره'. This means testimony is not a condition for the validity of a sale, nor is it an acknowledgment of ownership, because a sale can occur from an owner or from a non-owner (e.g., agent, fuduli).
The proof is not based on a specific hadith but on legal reasoning (qiyas).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The specific word or phrase in the dalil that yields the ruling is the statement that testimony is not an acknowledgment of ownership. The reasoning is that since the sale can be valid from a non-owner, witnessing the sale does not imply that the witness believes the seller is the owner. The witness may have just recorded the event.
This refutes the idea that writing testimony implies belief in the seller's ownership.
4
Contrast with kafalah bi al-darak
Kafalah bi al-darak is a guarantee that the seller owns the property. By undertaking this guarantee, the surety (kafil) implicitly acknowledges the seller's ownership. Therefore, if the kafil later claims ownership, his claim is rejected due to contradiction (تناقض).
Kafalah bi al-darak is in the 'rank of acknowledgment' according to the text.
5
Note the exception
If the sale deed explicitly states 'he sold while he owned it' or 'a binding final sale', and the witness writes that he testifies to that, then this is considered taslim from the witness. However, if the witness testifies to the contracting parties' acknowledgment of ownership, it is not taslim.
The exception is based on the explicit language of the deed, not mere testimony.

تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی آدمی نے مکان فروخت کیا اور ایک شخص مکان فروخت ہونے پر تحریرا گواہ ہو گیا اور دستخط کر کے اپنی مہر لگا دی۔ مگر یہ گواہ کفیل بالدرک نہیں ہوا تو یہ گواہ کی طرف سے بائع کی ملکیت کو تسلیم کرنا نہیں ہوگا یعنی یہ گواہی اس بات کی علامت نہ ہوگی کہ گواہ بائع کو مکان کا مالک تسلیم کرتا ہے۔ چنانچہ اسکے بعد گواہ نے اگر یہ دعویٰ کیا کہ میں مکان کا مالک ہوں تو اس کا دعویٰ قابل سماعت ہوگا اور اگر کسی دوسرے نے مکان کے مالک ہونے کا دعویٰ کیا اور اس نے اس کے مالک ہونے کی گواہی دی تو بھی اس کی گواہی کو قبول کر لیا جائے گا۔
دلیل یہ ہے کہ شہادت نہ تو بیع کے اندر مشروط ہے اور نہ بائع کی ملک کا اقرار ہے شہادت بیع کے اندر مشروط تو اس لئے نہیں کہ شہادت بیع کے مناسب نہیں ہے اور بائع کی ملک کا اقرار اس لئے نہیں کہ بیع بھی تو مالک کی طرف سے واقع ہوتی ہے اس طور پر کہ مالک نے خود کسی کے ہاتھ اپنی مملوکہ چیز فروخت کی اور کبھی غیر کی طرف سے واقع ہوتی ہے اس طور پر کہ وکیل نے فروخت کی یا فضولی نے فروخت کی پس مکان کی بیع پر گواہ ہونا اس بات کا اقرار نہیں ہو گا کہ اس نے اپنا مملوکہ مکان فروخت کیا ہے اور جب گواہ کی گواہی ، بائع کی ملک کا اقرار نہیں ہے تو اب اگر گواہ خود اپنی ملک کا دعوی کر دے تو اس سے دعوئی میں تناقض نہ ہوگا اور جب دعوی میں تناقض نہ ہوا تو اس کا دعوی ملکیت بھی قابل توجہ اور قابل سماعت ہوگا۔ برخلاف مسئلہ سابق یعنی کفالہ بالدرک کے کہ کفالہ بالدرک کفیل کی جانب سے بائع کی ملک کا اقرار ہوتا ہے اور جب کفیل بالدرک نے بائع کی ملک کا اقرار کر لیا تو اب اس کو اپنی ملکیت کے دعوئی کا حق نہ ہو گا۔ حتی کہ اگر اس نے دعوی کیا بھی تو اس کو قبول نہ کیا جائیگا۔
سوال رہی یہ بات کہ جب مکان کی بیع پر گواہی بائع کی ملک کا اقرار نہیں ہے تو پھر اس شہادت کو لکھنے سے کیا فائدہ ہے؟ جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ممکن ہے گواہی اس مقصد سے لکھ لی گئی ہو کہ اس واقعہ کو یاد رکھے کہ اس مکان کی بابت ایسا واقعہ ہوا تھا پس اس سے ملکیت کے اقرار لازم نہیں آتا ۔
صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ مشائخ احناف نے کہا کہ اگر بیع نامہ میں لکھا گیا کہ بائع نے اس مکان کو فروخت کیا درانحالیکہ بائع اس کا مالک تھا یا بیع قطعی نافذ کے ساتھ فروخت کیا اور گواہ نے گواہی لکھی کہ میں اس کا شاہد ہوں تو یہ گواہ کی طرف سے بائع کی ملکیت کو تسلیم کرنا ہوگا کیونکہ بیع اسی وقت نافذ ہو سکتی ہے جبکہ مکان بائع کی ملکیت ہو ہاں اگر گواہ نے یہ گواہی لکھی کہ میں اس بات پر گواہ ہوں کہ عاقدین نے میرے سامنے اس کا اقرار کیا ہے کہ بائع اس مکان کا مالک ہے تو یہ بھی گواہ کی طرف سے بائع کی ملک کو تسلیم کرنا نہیں ہو گا۔ کیونکہ عاقدین کے اقرار سے حقیقی ملکیت ثابت نہیں ہوتی ہے اور اگر گواہ نے صرف یہ لکھا ہے کہ میں ایجاب وقبول کا شاہد ہوں تو بدرجہ اولی گواہ کی طرف سے بائع کی ملک کا اقرار نہ ہوگا۔ جمیل احمد عفی عنہ
Book of Kafalah. Sometimes the buyer does not incline to purchase the sold item due to fear that someone may claim entitlement. So the surety gives reassurance to the buyer saying: Buy the house and do not worry, this house is the seller's property, and if, God forbid, someone claims entitlement and takes the house, I am the guarantor for returning the price. Thus in this case also, from the surety's side there is acknowledgment of the seller's ownership. And whoever acknowledges the seller's ownership is not permitted thereafter to claim ownership himself. The conclusion is that in this case, kafalah is in the rank of the surety's acknowledgment that the seller is the owner. And when due to kafalah the surety acknowledged that the owner of this house is the seller, thereafter the surety's claim that 'I am the owner' will not be acceptable due to contradiction. He gave testimony and affixed his seal and did not undertake kafalah bi al-darak, so this is not taslim. He said: And if he testified and sealed and did not guarantee (kafalah), it is not taslim, and he remains upon his claim, because testimony is not a condition in the sale, nor is it an acknowledgment of ownership, because the sale sometimes occurs from the owner and sometimes from someone else, and perhaps he wrote the testimony to preserve the incident, contrary to what preceded. They said: If it is written in the deed 'he sold while he owned it' or 'a binding final sale' and he wrote 'he testified to that', then it is taslim, unless he wrote the testimony upon the acknowledgment of the contracting parties. Translation: And if someone testified and sealed and did not undertake kafalah bi al-darak, then this is not taslim, and he remains upon his claim, because testimony is not conditional in the sale, nor is testimony an acknowledgment of ownership, because the sale sometimes occurs from the owner and sometimes from someone else, and perhaps he wrote the testimony to remember the incident. Contrary to the previous issue. The Mashayikh said: When in the sale deed it was written that the seller sold this house while he was its owner, or he sold with a binding final sale, and the witness wrote that he is a witness to that, then this is taslim from the witness, unless the witness wrote the testimony upon the acknowledgment of the contracting parties. Explanation of the scenario: If a person sold a house, and another person became a witness in writing to the sale of the house and affixed his signature and seal, but this witness did not become kafil bi al-darak, then this from the witness is not an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership, meaning this testimony will not be a sign that the witness acknowledges the seller as owner of the house. Therefore, if thereafter the witness claimed that he is the owner of the house, his claim will be heard. And if someone else claimed ownership and he testified for him, his testimony will be accepted. The proof is that testimony is neither conditional in the sale nor an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership. Testimony is not conditional in the sale because testimony is not appropriate for the sale. And it is not an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership because the sale sometimes occurs from the owner in the manner that the owner himself sold his owned property to someone, and sometimes from someone else in the manner that an agent sold or a fuduli sold. So becoming a witness to the sale of the house is not an acknowledgment that he sold his owned house. And when the witness's testimony is not an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership, then if the witness himself claims his ownership, there will be no contradiction in his claim, and when there is no contradiction, his claim of ownership will be worthy of attention and heard. Contrary to the previous issue, i.e., kafalah bi al-darak, because kafalah bi al-darak is an acknowledgment from the surety of the seller's ownership, and when the kafil bi al-darak acknowledged the seller's ownership, he will no longer have the right to claim his own ownership. Even if he claims, it will not be accepted. Question: When testimony to the sale of a house is not an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership, what is the benefit of writing such testimony? Answer: It is possible that the testimony was written for the purpose of remembering the incident that such an incident occurred regarding this house, so acknowledgment of ownership is not necessary from it. The author of Hidayah said: The Hanafi Mashayikh said that if in the sale deed it was written that the seller sold this house while the seller was its owner, or he sold with a binding final sale, and the witness wrote testimony that 'I am a witness to that', then this will be an acknowledgment from the witness of the seller's ownership, because the sale can only be effective when the house is the seller's property. However, if the witness wrote testimony that 'I am a witness that the contracting parties acknowledged before me that the seller is the owner of this house', then this is not an acknowledgment from the witness of the seller's ownership, because real ownership is not proven by the acknowledgment of the contracting parties. And if the witness only wrote 'I am a witness to the offer and acceptance', then a fortiori it will not be an acknowledgment from the witness of the seller's ownership. Jamil Ahmad, may he be forgiven.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the book of Kafalah, distinguishing between mere testimony to a sale and kafalah bi al-darak regarding acknowledgment of ownership. The author begins by explaining the practical scenario: a buyer may be reluctant to purchase property due to fear of third-party claims. The surety (kafil) reassures the buyer by guaranteeing the return of the price if the property is taken away, thereby implicitly acknowledging that the seller is the owner. Once the surety acknowledges the seller's ownership, he cannot later claim ownership himself due to contradiction. The core legal question is whether a person who merely witnesses a sale (writes testimony, affixes seal) without undertaking kafalah bi al-darak is considered to have acknowledged the seller's ownership. The matn from al-Hidayah states: 'And if he testified and sealed and did not guarantee (kafalah), it is not taslim, and he remains upon his claim.' The reason is that testimony is not a condition in the sale nor an acknowledgment of ownership, because the sale can be performed by the owner or by a non-owner (e.g., agent or fuduli). The witness may have written the testimony merely to record the event. This contrasts with the earlier case of kafalah bi al-darak, where the guarantee itself constitutes an acknowledgment. The commentary then elaborates: a mere witness who does not provide kafalah can later claim ownership, and his testimony for another claimant is also acceptable. The proof is that testimony is not conditional in the sale nor an acknowledgment of ownership. The passage also addresses a question: what benefit does writing testimony have if it doesn't acknowledge ownership? The answer is that it serves to preserve the memory of the event. Finally, the Mashayikh clarify an exception: if the deed explicitly states that the seller sold while being the owner, or if the sale is described as binding and final, and the witness writes that he testifies to that, then it is considered taslim (acknowledgment). However, if the witness testifies to the contracting parties' acknowledgment of ownership, it is not an acknowledgment by the witness himself. Writing testimony to the offer and acceptance alone is even less indicative of acknowledgment. This is a nuanced discussion appropriate for the Hanafi madhhab's careful distinction between explicit and implicit acknowledgments.

Key Terms
كفالة بالدرك (kafalah bi al-darak): A guarantee undertaken by the seller or a third party to indemnify the buyer if the sold property is reclaimed by a rightful owner.
تسليم (taslim): Acknowledgment or admission; here specifically meaning acknowledgment of the seller's ownership through one's action.
شهادة (shahadah): Testimony; bearing witness to an event or contract, here specifically witnessing a sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person merely testifies in writing to a sale (without kafalah bi al-darak), can he later claim ownership of the sold property?
A) No, because his testimony implies he acknowledged the seller as owner.
B) Yes, because testimony is not an acknowledgment of ownership and he remains on his claim.
C) Yes, but only if he did not affix his seal.
D) No, unless he expressly stated he does not acknowledge the seller's ownership.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, mere testimony to a sale does not constitute an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership; only kafalah bi al-darak or explicit wording in the deed carries that effect.

Kafalah — Surety
beginner
6 min
Invalidity of Daman by Wakil or Mudarib for the Price
Ruling and reasoning for invalidity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a wakil or mudarib cannot validly guarantee the price for their principal, and explain the logical reasoning (dalil) based on 'iltizam al-mutalabah'.

Core Idea
A guarantee by a wakil or mudarib for the price they are responsible to collect is invalid—it creates a guarantee for oneself.

In this lesson, we explore a specific rule in the Hanafi law of suretyship (kafalah/daman): an agent (wakil) or investment partner (mudarib) cannot guarantee the price (thaman) for the person on whose behalf they are acting. The ruling is based on a clear logical principle: the guarantor must be subject to the creditor's demand, but here the demand right rests with the wakil or mudarib themselves. This leads to a circular guarantee that the law rejects. We will unpack the reasoning and see how it applies in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
First, note the two scenarios: a wakil who sells a garment for his principal and guarantees the price; a mudarib who sells goods of the capital owner and guarantees the price. The ruling is that such a guarantee (daman) is batil (invalid).
The invalidity is unconditional—it does not depend on the principal's consent.
2
Extract the legal reasoning (dalil)
The text provides the 'illa (cause): 'because kafalah is the undertaking of demand (iltizam al-mutalabah), and the right of demand belongs to them both, so each becomes a guarantor for himself.' The wakil and mudarib already have the right to demand the price from the buyer on behalf of their principals; therefore, if they guarantee that price, they are effectively guaranteeing their own obligation—a logical impossibility.
The second reason given is that the property is a trust (amanah) in their hands, and a trustee cannot be held liable for the price as a surety.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (proof linkage)
The key phrase is 'التزام المطالبة'—the essence of suretyship is that the surety becomes liable to the creditor's demand. In these cases, the wakil and mudarib are themselves the ones entitled to demand payment; thus, demanding from them as sureties would be demanding from the same person who owes the demand. This circularity invalidates the contract.
The ruling is derived through qiyas jali (clear analogy) rather than a direct text.
4
Note the cross-madhhab status
The Hanafi school considers this rule definitive, but other schools may allow such guarantees if there is a separate benefit (e.g., the guarantee is for a different obligation). Here, the Hanafi view is relied upon (mufta bihi).
The authority weight is zahir al-riwayah from al-Hidayah.
5
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Test your understanding: If an employee is authorized to collect debts for his employer, can he validly guarantee one of those debts? Apply the same 'illa: he would be guaranteeing his own collection duty, so the guarantee is invalid.
The principle is not limited to wakil and mudarib but extends to any trustee with a right to demand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الضمان
فیصل ضمان کے بیان میں ہے
تشریح کفالہ اور ضمانت دونوں ہم معنی ہیں اس وجہ سے اکثر فقہاء نے باب الکفالہ کے بجائے باب الضمان تحریر کیا ہے مگر چونکہ جامع صغیر میں چند مسائل ایسے ہیں جن میں بجائے کفالت کے عمان کا لفظ مذکور ہے اس لئے صاحب ہدایہ نے ان مسائل کو علیحدہ فصل میں اسی لفظ عمان کے ساتھ کر فرمایا ہے۔
وکیل موکل کے شمن کا ضامن بن گیا اور مضارب رب المال کیلئے ضامن بن گیا تو ضمان کا حکم
قال ومن باع لرجل ثوبا وضمن له الثمن او مضارب ضمن ثمن متاع رب المال فالضمان باطل لان الكفالة التزام المطالبة وهى اليهما فيصير كل واحد منهما ضامنا لنفسه ولان المال امانة في ايديهما والضمان
ـر نـحكم الشرع فيرد عليه كاشتراطه على المودع والمستعيـ
tرجمہ اور اگر کسی نے ایک شخص کے لئے تھان فروخت کیا اور اس کے لئے شمن کا ضامن ہو گیا یا مضارب ، رب المال کے سامان کے شمن کا ضامن ہو گیا تو یہ نعمان باطل ہے کیونکہ کفالہ مطالبہ توستلزم کرنے کا نام ہے اور مطالبہ کا حق انھیں دونوں کو ہے پس ان دونوں میں
Chapter on Daman. Explanation of Daman. Clarification: Kafalah and Daman are synonymous; therefore most jurists have written the chapter as Bab al-Daman instead of Bab al-Kafalah. But because in al-Jami' al-Saghir there are several issues in which the word Daman is used instead of Kafalah, the author of al-Hidayah has placed those issues in a separate chapter with the same word Daman. An agent (wakil) became guarantor for the price (thaman) of his principal, and a mudarib became guarantor for the price of the goods of the owner of capital (rabb al-mal). Then the ruling of the guarantee (daman). He said: And whoever sold a garment for a man and guaranteed the price for him, or a mudarib guaranteed the price of the merchandise of the owner of capital, then the guarantee is invalid because kafalah is the undertaking of demand (iltizam al-mutalabah), and the right of demand belongs to both of them, so each of them becomes a guarantor for himself, and because the property is a trust in their hands, and the guarantee... [text cut off]
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof introducing a new chapter on Daman (suretyship) within the book of Kafalah. The author notes that kafalah and daman are synonymous in Hanafi fiqh, though some jurists use one or the other. Al-Hidayah separates certain issues from al-Jami' al-Saghir that use the word 'daman' into a distinct fasl. The specific ruling discussed is about a wakil (agent) who guarantees the price for his principal, or a mudarib (investment partner) who guarantees the price for the rabb al-mal (capital owner). In both cases, the daman is batil (invalid). The reason given is that kafalah involves undertaking the obligation to be pursued for payment (iltizam al-mutalabah). But the right to demand payment (al-mutalabah) already belongs to these individuals themselves: the wakil has the right to demand the price from the buyer on behalf of his principal, and the mudarib has the right to demand the price for the business. So if they guarantee, they are essentially guaranteeing for themselves, which is invalid. Additionally, the property in their hands is a trust (amanah), not owned by them, so they cannot be sureties for its price. The Arabic text at the end is corrupted but likely continues to argue that imposing a guarantee on a trustee is like the invalid condition in a deposit or loan. The ruling is clear: a person who is already responsible for collecting a debt cannot act as surety for that same debt.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Suretyship; synonym of kafalah in this context, meaning an undertaking to be liable for a debt or obligation.
التزام المطالبة (iltizam al-mutalabah): Undertaking to be subject to demand for payment; the essence of kafalah is that the surety becomes liable to be pursued by the creditor.
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where a person (kafil) becomes liable for a debt or obligation of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school in this passage, why is a wakil's guarantee of the price (thaman) for his principal invalid?
A) Because the wakil has no authority to guarantee.
B) Because the wakil would be guaranteeing for himself, as he already has the right to demand payment.
C) Because the principal did not request the guarantee.
D) Because the sale is void.

Key Takeaway
A wakil or mudarib cannot guarantee the price they are responsible to collect because it creates a self-guarantee, which is logically invalid in Hanafi law.

Kafalah — Surety

Witness Testimony and Ownership Acknowledgment in Sale Deeds
Distinguishing witness to sale from ownership acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a witness's testimony in a sale deed constitutes an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership and apply the ruling to scenarios where the witness later claims ownership.

Core Idea
Testimony to a sale does not imply ownership acknowledgment; a witness can later claim the property unless the deed explicitly confirms the seller's title.

A common question in sale transactions: if a person witnesses a sale and later claims the property for himself, does his earlier testimony bar him? The Hanafi school distinguishes between mere witness to the transaction and explicit acknowledgment of the seller's ownership. This lesson unpacks the ruling from Al-Hidayah, using the contrast with kafalah bil-darak and three illustrative scenarios from the Hanafi mashayikh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage states that a witness who merely testifies to a sale does not thereby acknowledge the seller's ownership. If he later claims the property for himself, his claim is valid and not contradictory.
The key is what the testimony explicitly states—not the mere fact of witnessing.
2
Contrast with kafalah bil-darak
Kafalah bil-darak (suretyship for defects) inherently includes an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership. Therefore, a kafil cannot later claim the property. Contrast this with the witness in a sale deed.
This contrast highlights that the ruling depends on the nature of the legal act.
3
Analyze the three scenarios from the Hanafi mashayikh
Three wording possibilities are examined: (1) deed states seller sold as owner + witness testifies to sale → implied acknowledgment (because sale requires ownership). (2) witness testifies that parties acknowledged seller's ownership → not acknowledgment (parties' statement does not prove actual title). (3) witness only testifies to offer and acceptance → a fortiori not acknowledgment.
The decisive factor is whether the testimony links the sale to the seller's ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملک کا اقرار نہیں ہے تو اب اگر گواہ خود اپنی ملک کا دعوی کر دے تو اس سے دعوئی میں تناقض نہ ہوگا اور جب دعوی میں تناقض نہ ہوا تو اس کا دعوی ملکیت بھی قابل توجہ اور قابل سماعت ہوگا۔ برخلاف مسئلہ سابق یعنی کفالہ بالدرک کے کہ کفالہ بالدرک کفیل کی جانب سے بائع کی ملک کا اقرار ہوتا ہے اور جب کفیل بالدرک نے بائع کی ملک کا اقرار کر لیا تو اب اس کو اپنی ملکیت کے دعوئی کا حق نہ ہو گا۔ حتی کہ اگر اس نے دعوی کیا بھی تو اس کو قبول نہ کیا جائیگا۔
سوال رہی یہ بات کہ جب مکان کی بیع پر گواہی بائع کی ملک کا اقرار نہیں ہے تو پھر اس شہادت کو لکھنے سے کیا فائدہ ہے؟ جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ممکن ہے گواہی اس مقصد سے لکھ لی گئی ہو کہ اس واقعہ کو یاد رکھے کہ اس مکان کی بابت ایسا واقعہ ہوا تھا پس اس سے ملکیت کے اقرار لازم نہیں آتا۔
صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ مشائخ احناف نے کہا کہ اگر بینامہ میں لکھا گیا کہ بائع نے اس مکان کو فروخت کیا دراینحالیکہ بائع اس کا مالک تھا یا بیع قطعی نافذ کے ساتھ فروخت کیا اور گواہ نے گواہی لکھی کہ میں اس کا شاہد ہوں تو یہ گواہ کی طرف سے بائع کی ملکیت کو تسلیم کرنا ہوگا کیونکہ بیع اسی وقت نافذ ہو سکتی ہے جبکہ مکان بائع کی ملکیت ہو ہاں اگر گواہ نے یہ گواہی لکھی کہ میں اس بات پر گواہ ہوں کہ عاقدین نے میرے سامنے اس کا اقرار کیا ہے کہ بائع اس مکان کا مالک ہے تو یہ بھی گواہ کی طرف سے بائع کی ملک کو تسلیم کرنا نہیں ہو گا۔ کیونکہ عاقدین کے اقرار سے حقیقی ملکیت ثابت نہیں ہوتی ہے اور اگر گواہ نے صرف یہ لکھا ہے کہ میں ایجاب وقبول کا شاہد ہوں تو بدرجہ اولی گواہ کی طرف سے بائع کی ملک کا اقرار نہ ہوگا۔
If a witness has not acknowledged the seller's ownership, then if the witness himself claims ownership of the property, there will be no contradiction in his claim. And when there is no contradiction in the claim, his claim of ownership will be worthy of consideration and hearing. Contrary to the previous issue, i.e., kafalah bil-darak, because kafalah bil-darak is an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership on the part of the kafil. And when the kafil bil-darak has acknowledged the seller's ownership, then he will have no right to claim ownership for himself. Even if he makes a claim, it will not be accepted. 
The question remains: when testimony upon the sale of a house is not an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership, then what benefit is there in writing this testimony? The answer is: it is possible that the testimony was written for the purpose of remembering the event that such an incident occurred regarding this house. Thus, an acknowledgment of ownership does not necessarily follow. 
The author of al-Hidayah said that the Hanafi mashayikh stated: if it is written in the deed that the seller sold this house while he was its owner, or that he sold it with a final and binding sale, and the witness wrote the testimony saying, 'I am a witness to that,' then this will be considered an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership on the part of the witness, because a sale can only be effective when the house is the seller's property. However, if the witness wrote the testimony saying, 'I am a witness that the contracting parties acknowledged before me that the seller is the owner of this house,' then this too will not be considered an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership on the part of the witness, because the acknowledgment of the contracting parties does not prove actual ownership. And if the witness only wrote, 'I am a witness to the offer and acceptance,' then a fortiori it will not be an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership on the part of the witness.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from Al-Hidayah clarifying the effect of a witness's testimony in a sale deed regarding ownership. The core ruling: a witness who merely records a sale transaction does not thereby acknowledge the seller's ownership. If the witness later claims ownership of the property himself, his claim is valid and does not contradict his earlier testimony, because he never affirmed the seller's right. This is contrasted with kafalah bil-darak (suretyship for defects), where the surety explicitly acknowledges the seller's ownership as part of guaranteeing the sale, thus forfeiting his own claim to the property. The passage then addresses a practical question: if the testimony doesn't acknowledge ownership, why write it? The answer: it serves as a record of the event, not an admission of legal title. Finally, the text, citing the Hanafi mashayikh, provides three scenarios: (1) If the deed states that the seller sold while being the owner, and the witness testifies to the sale, this implies acknowledgment because the validity of the sale requires ownership. (2) If the witness testifies that the parties acknowledged the seller's ownership, this is still not an acknowledgment by the witness because the parties' acknowledgment is not proof of actual ownership. (3) If the witness only testifies to the offer and acceptance, it is even less an acknowledgment. The lesson follows the author's order, first stating the ruling with the kafalah bil-darak contrast, then the question and answer, then the detailed scenarios. The epistemological weight is from the later Hanafi authorities (mutakhkhirin) as transmitted in the commentary on Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
كفالة بالدرك (kafalah bil-darak): A type of suretyship where the surety (kafil) guarantees the seller's obligation to return the price in case the sold property is repossessed by a prior owner; this guarantee involves the kafil's acknowledgment of the seller's ownership.
إقرار (iqrar): Admission or acknowledgment of a right or fact; here, the witness's potential acknowledgment of the seller's ownership through the wording of the testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a witness writes in a sale deed that the seller sold the house and the witness is a witness to that transaction, and later the witness claims ownership of the house, what is the ruling?
A) The witness's claim is invalid because he already acknowledged the seller's ownership.
B) The witness's claim is valid because his testimony was not an acknowledgment of ownership.
C) The witness's claim is invalid because he contradicted his earlier statement.
D) The witness's claim is valid only if he did not write the deed himself.

Key Takeaway
A witness's testimony to a sale does not automatically acknowledge the seller's ownership; only specific wording that ties the sale to the seller's title creates an acknowledgment.
