Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
10 min
Kafala for Kharaj, Nawā'ib, and Qisma
Explains rulings on kafala for three types of levies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between kharaj muwazzaf and muqāsama, classify nawā'ib as just or unjust, and state the Hanafi rulings on kafala for each, including the intra-Hanafi dispute on unjust levies.

Core Idea
Kafala is valid only for debts fixed on the dhimmah; unjust taxes are disputed: some scholars require legal obligation, others consider the ruler's demand sufficient.

In this passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah, the commentator clarifies the rulings on suretyship (kafala) for three types of levies: land tax (kharaj), unforeseen impositions (nawā'ib), and apportioned shares (qisma). The key to understanding these rulings lies in whether the levy constitutes a dayn (debt) upon the dhimmah. The passage also presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding unjust taxes, where the later mashāyikh differ on the basis of kafala.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of levies and their definitions
Kharaj is land tax, divided into muwazzaf (fixed annual amount) and muqāsama (proportional share). Nawā'ib are unforeseen levies, divided into just (e.g., public works) and unjust (oppressive taxes). Qisma is an apportioned share, sometimes synonymous with nawā'ib or a fixed regular levy.
The passage also includes a Quranic verse (54:28) to define qisma.
2
Determine whether each levy is a dayn
Kharaj muwazzaf is a dayn because it is a fixed debt on the dhimmah. Kharaj muqāsama is not a dayn because it is a share of produce, not a personal liability. Just nawā'ib are dayn because they are obligatory upon Muslims. Unjust nawā'ib are disputed: some consider them not dayn, others consider them dayn due to the ruler's demand.
Dayn is defined as a substitute for destroyed property, loan, sold item, mahr, or hire.
3
Apply the general rule: Kafala is valid only for a dayn
Kafala is permissible only for something that is a dayn or wajib upon the dhimmah. Therefore, kafala for kharaj muwazzaf and just nawā'ib is valid by consensus. Kafala for kharaj muqāsama is invalid. For unjust nawā'ib, there is disagreement.
The rule is derived from the definition of kafala: it is a demand for something legally binding on the principal.
4
Analyse the analogy between kharaj and zakāh
The author of al-Hidayah contrasts kharaj with zakāh. Zakāh is an act of worship, not a dayn, and the wealth subject to zakāh is non-guaranteed. Hence kafala for zakāh is invalid, unlike kharaj muwazzaf.
Two proofs: (1) zakāh is pure worship and the wealth is not guaranteed; (2) zakāh does not fall under the definition of dayn.
5
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on unjust nawā'ib
Some mashāyikh argue kafala is invalid because unjust taxes are not legally binding. Imam Ali al-Bazdawi argues it is valid because kafala is based on the ruler's demand (muṭālabah), regardless of the tax's justice. Both positions are presented without explicit tarjih.
The practical consequence: if a person guarantees an unjust tax, is he liable? According to the first view, no; according to Bazdawi, yes.

وصول کئے جاتے ہیں شرعاً ان کا کوئی وجوب نہیں ہے۔ پس اگر نواب سے وہ مراد ہوں جو ٹیکس بجا اور برحق ہیں تو ان کا کفالہ بالا تفاق جائز ہے کیونکہ ہر نیا ٹیکس جس کو امام المسلمین نے مسلمانوں کے فائدے کیلئے الذمہ دین ہوتا ہے۔ اسلئے یہ نوائب دیون ہوں گے اور دیون کا کفالہ جائز ہے اسلئے ان کا کفالہ جائز ہوگا اور رہی دوسری قسم یعنی وہ نوائب اور ٹیکس جن کو بادشاہ نے ظلماً مقرر کیا ہو تو ان کی کفالت کے جواز اور عدم جواز میں مشائخ کا اختلاف ہے۔ چنانچہ بعض حضرات مشائخ نے فرمایا کہ اس طرح کے نوائب کا کفالہ جائز نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ کفالہ اس چیز کے مطالبہ کو لازم کرنے کیلئے مشروع ہوا ہے جو چیز خود اصیل یعنی مکفول عنہ پر لازم ہو ۔ حالانکہ جو نوائب خود اصیل یعنی مسلمانوں پر شرعاً لازم نہیں ہیں تو ان کا کفالہ بھی جائز نہ ہوگا اور بعض حضرات مشائخ مثلا فخر الاسلام الامام علی بزدوی نے فرمایا کہ اس قسم کے نوائب کا کفالہ بھی جائز ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ بادشاہ کی طرف سے جو ٹیکس بھی مقرر کیا جائے گا خواہ بجا اور حق طور پر ہو خواہ بے جا اور ظلما ہو وہ دین ہوگا۔ بایں معنی کہ بادشاہ کی طرف سے اس کا مطالبہ کیا جاتا ہے اور کفالہ میں مطالبہ ہی معتبر ہے۔ کیونکہ کفالہ التزام مطالبہ کیلئے مشروع ہوا ہے۔ پس جب ہر طرح کے ٹیکس کا مطالبہ کیا جاتا ہے تو ہر طرح کے ٹیکس کا کفالہ بھی درست ہوگا اور رہا لفظ قسمت تو بعض حضرات نے کہا کہ قسمت اور نوائب دونوں ایک ہی چیز ہیں ان میں کوئی فرق نہیں ہے۔ پس اس صورت میں نوائبہ اور قسمتہ کے درمیان واؤ ہو گا اور عطف، عطف تفسیری ہوگا اور جو حکم نوائب کا ہے وہی حکم قسمت کا ہوگا۔ اور یا قسمت نوائب کا ایک حصہ ہے۔ مثلا کسی حادثہ کے موقع پر مسلمانوں پر کچھ مقرر کر دیا ہو جیسے پل ٹوٹ گیا اور اس کا خرچہ تمام لوگوں پر پھیلا یا گیا تو ایک آدمی پر جو پڑا وہ اس کی قسمت ہے ۔ اب اگر کوئی شخص اس کی طرف سے کفیل ہو گیا تو جائز ہے اس صورت میں نوائبہ اوقسمتہ کے درمیان اؤ ہوگا۔ اور ترجمہ یہ ہوگا کہ نوائب کی کفالت کی یا قسمت کی کفالت کی ۔ اور بعض حضرات نے نوائب اور قسمت کے درمیان یہ فرق کیا ہے کہ قسمت سے مراد وہ نائبہ (ٹیکس) ہے جو مستقلاً مقرر کر دیا گیا ہو اور اس کو وقت خاص پر پابندی کے ساتھ وصول کیا جاتا ہو جیسے چوکیدار کی تنخواہ دینے کیلئے اہل محلہ پر ماہانہ مقرر کر دیا ہو اور نوائب سے میرا دوہ ہیں جو ہنگامی طور پر کبھی کبھار مقرر کر دیا جائے۔ مثلاً اچانک کوئی پل ٹوٹ گیا ہو اس کو درست کرانے کیلئے جو وظیفہ لوگوں پر مقرر کیا جائے گا۔ اس کو نائبہ کہتے ہیں اور نوائب کا حکم سابق میں مذکور ہو چکا کہ نوائب اگر بجا اور برحق ہوں تو ان کا کفالہ بالا تفاق جائز ہے۔
Book of Kafala. Ashraf al-Hidayah, Volume Nine. Kafala is permissible by consensus. And if by nawā'ib is meant those that are unjust, like the taxes (jibāyāt) in our time, then there is disagreement among the mashāyikh. Among those who incline toward validity is Imam Ali al-Bazdawi. As for the word qisma, it is said that it is the same as nawā'ib or a part of nawā'ib. And the narration is with the letter 'aw' (or). And it is said that qisma refers to a nā'iba that is fixed and established. And nawā'ib refers to those that come unexpectedly. And the ruling is what we have mentioned. Explanation: Kharaj is the produce of land, jizya. Nawā'ib is the plural of nā'iba, meaning calamity, difficulty. Qisma is a portion. Allah Most High said: 'And inform them that the water is divided between them.' The scenario: If a person becomes surety for another person's kharaj, nawā'ib, and qisma, then it is permissible by consensus. For kharaj, the detail of permissibility of kafala has passed, but note that kharaj is of two types: kharaj muqāsama and kharaj muwazzaf. Kharaj muqāsama is that the Imām divides a fixed share of the land's produce, e.g., the Imām takes a tenth or twentieth. And kharaj muwazzaf is that the Imām, after assessment, fixes an amount on someone that must be paid every year. So here, kharaj means kharaj muwazzaf, not muqāsama. So kafala for kharaj muwazzaf is permissible because it is a debt (qarḍ) that is valid to demand from the people. And any debt that is valid to demand, its kafala is permissible. So kafala for kharaj muwazzaf is permissible. And kharaj muqāsama is not wajib on the dhimmah, so it is not in the meaning of a debt. When it is not a debt, its kafala is not permissible. Because kafala is not permissible for something that is not a dayn or debt. The author of al-Hidayah says: The ruling of kharaj is opposite to the ruling of zakāh, i.e., kafala for kharaj muwazzaf is permissible, and for zakāh it is not permissible. The reason for the difference is that zakāh is purely an action, and that action is worship ('ibādah). That is, zakāh is giving ownership of a portion of wealth without compensation. And the wealth that is the subject of zakāh is not maddamān (guaranteed). So if the wealth perishes, no guarantee (ḍamān) for zakāh is necessary. The gist is that zakāh is an act of worship and the wealth that is the subject of zakāh is non-guaranteed, and kafala is valid neither for worship nor for non-guaranteed wealth. Therefore, kafala for zakāh is not valid. And because zakāh is an action, after the death of the person on whom it was wajib, it is not paid from his estate. However, if the deceased made a will that his zakāh be paid from his wealth, then it will be paid from his estate. The second proof: Kafala is valid for dayn, and zakāh is not a dayn. Dayn is wealth that is established in the dhimmah, that becomes wajib as a substitute for something destroyed, or as a substitute for a loan, or as a substitute for a sold item, or as a substitute for the right of intercourse (mahr), or as a substitute for hiring something (ujrah). Zakāh is none of these. So zakāh is not a dayn. When it is not a dayn, its kafala is not valid. As for kharaj muwazzaf, it is a dayn, so its kafala is permissible. As for the ruling of nawā'ib, nawā'ib are of two types: first, those that are just and rightful, like the king wanting to dig a canal that is not exclusive but for the benefit of the general public, and the treasury has no capacity, so the king imposes a small amount on the people; or like a watchman hired for the protection of a locality, and the treasury is empty, so some wealth is imposed on the people to pay his salary; or like preparing an army against the disbelievers when the treasury is empty; or like freeing Muslim prisoners from the disbelievers when the treasury is empty. Any imposition on Muslims in a rightful and permissible manner falls under this category. Second, those that are not rightful, but the king imposed them unjustly, like in Persia and other countries, taxes on tailors, dyers, and other artisans and servants on their earnings monthly or yearly, and especially nowadays in India – surely Allah is the Protector – there is tax on everything: tax on houses, tax on travel, tax on purchase, tax on sale. All these taxes fall into the second category, collected unjustly; according to Sharī'ah they have no obligation. So if by nawā'ib is meant taxes that are just and rightful, then their kafala is permissible by consensus, because every new tax that the Imām of the Muslims imposes on them for their benefit, its payment is obligatory on the Muslims, and it is obligatory because obedience to the Imām is obligatory. When the payment of these nawā'ib is obligatory, then these nawā'ib become what is wajib upon the dhimmah, and what is wajib upon the dhimmah is dayn. So these nawā'ib are debts, and kafala for debts is permissible, so their kafala is permissible. As for the second type, the nawā'ib and taxes that the king imposed unjustly, there is disagreement among the mashāyikh regarding the permissibility and impermissibility of their kafala. Some mashāyikh said that kafala for such nawā'ib is not permissible, because kafala is legislated to necessitate the demand of something that is obligatory upon the principal (aṣil, i.e., the guaranteed party). Whereas these nawā'ib are not obligatory upon the principal (the Muslims) according to Sharī'ah, so their kafala is not permissible. And some mashāyikh, e.g., Fakhr al-Islām, Imām Ali al-Bazdawi, said that kafala for this kind of nawā'ib is also permissible. The proof is that whatever tax the king imposes, whether rightfully and correctly or wrongfully and unjustly, it will be dayn, in the sense that the king demands it, and in kafala, demand (muṭālabah) is what matters. Because kafala is legislated for undertaking the demand. So when every kind of tax is demanded, kafala for every kind of tax is valid. As for the word qisma, some said that qisma and nawā'ib are the same thing; there is no difference. In that case, there is a wāw between nā'ibah and qismah, and the conjunction is explanatory (tafsīrī), and the same ruling applies. Or qisma is a part of nawā'ib: e.g., on the occasion of an incident, something is imposed on the Muslims, like a bridge breaking, and its expense is spread over all the people, so what falls on one person is his qisma. Then if a person becomes surety for him, it is permissible. In this case, it is 'nā'ibah or qismah'. And the translation is: 'he guaranteed the nawā'ib or guaranteed the qisma.' And some made a difference between nawā'ib and qisma: qisma refers to a tax that is fixed permanently and collected regularly at a specific time, e.g., monthly imposition on the residents of a locality for the watchman's salary. And nawā'ib are those that are imposed occasionally, e.g., a bridge suddenly breaks, so the duty imposed on the people to repair it is called nā'ibah. The ruling of nawā'ib has been mentioned earlier: if they are just and rightful, their kafala is permissible by consensus.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah (Ashraf al-Hidayah) discusses the ruling on kafala (suretyship/guarantee) for three types of levies: kharaj (land tax), nawā'ib (unforeseen levies), and qisma (apportioned share). The passage first states that kafala for kharaj is permissible by consensus. It then clarifies that the kharaj in question is kharaj muwazzaf (fixed annual tax), not kharaj muqāsama (proportional share of produce). The justification is that kharaj muwazzaf is a debt (dayn) that can be demanded from individuals, and kafala is valid for debts. Kharaj muqāsama, on the other hand, is not a fixed debt on the dhimmah, so kafala is not valid. The passage then compares kharaj with zakāh, emphasizing that zakāh is an act of worship, not a debt; therefore, kafala for zakāh is not valid. The proof for this is twofold: first, zakāh is a pure act of worship, and the wealth subject to zakāh is not guaranteed (ghayr maddamān), so kafala cannot apply. Second, zakāh does not fall under the definition of dayn (debt) because it is not a substitute for something destroyed, loan, sold item, mahr, or hire. The passage then explains that nawā'ib are divided into two categories: just and rightful (e.g., public works, watchmen, army preparation, freeing prisoners) and unjust (e.g., oppressive taxes). For just nawā'ib, kafala is unanimously permissible because these are obligations upon the Muslims (wājib fī al-dhimmah), making them debts. For unjust nawā'ib, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: some mashāyikh hold that kafala is not valid because these levies are not legally binding on the Muslims, so there is no debt to guarantee. Others, led by Imam Ali al-Bazdawi, argue that kafala is valid because the king demands these taxes, and kafala is based on the demand (muṭālabah) regardless of the justice of the original obligation. Both positions are presented without an explicit tarjīh, but the text notes that the first opinion (invalidity) is held by some mashāyikh, while Bazdawi's view represents the opposing camp. Finally, the passage discusses the term 'qisma', offering several interpretations: it may be synonymous with nawā'ib, or a subcategory, or distinct as a fixed regular levy vs. occasional ones. The ruling follows that of nawā'ib: if just, kafala is permissible; if unjust, the same disagreement applies. The passage is dense in legal reasoning, contrasting kharaj muwazzaf and muqāsama, distinguishing zakāh from debts, and classifying nawā'ib. The student should note the importance of the concept of 'dayn' (debt) in determining the validity of kafala, and the role of 'muṭālabah' (demand) in Bazdawi's reasoning.

Key Terms
خراج موظف (kharāj muwazzaf): A fixed annual land tax assessed and imposed on a person's dhimmah.
خراج مقاسمه (kharāj muqāsama): A proportional share of the land's produce taken by the imam, not a fixed debt on the dhimmah.
نائبہ (نوائب) (nā'ibah (nawā'ib)): A calamity or unforeseen levy imposed on the people, either justly or unjustly.
قسمت (qismat (qisma)): A portion or apportioned share, often used for a fixed regular levy as opposed to an occasional one.
دین (dayn): A financial liability established in the dhimmah, typically as a substitute for something destroyed, a loan, a sale, mahr, or hire.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is kafala for kharaj muwazzaf permissible according to the Hanafi school, while kafala for kharaj muqāsama is not?
A) Because kharaj muwazzaf is a fixed debt (dayn) on the dhimmah, while kharaj muqāsama is a proportional share not held as a personal liability.
B) Because kharaj muwazzaf is collected by the imam, while kharaj muqāsama is collected by the landowner.
C) Because kharaj muwazzaf is wajib, while kharaj muqāsama is sunnah.
D) Because kharaj muwazzaf is mentioned in the Quran, while kharaj muqāsama is not.

Key Takeaway
Kafala is only valid for a dayn (debt) fixed on the dhimmah; therefore, kharaj muwazzaf and just nawā'ib qualify, while kharaj muqāsama and unjust nawā'ib are either invalid or disputed.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
10 min
Kafāla for Nawā'ib: Rightful and Unjust Taxes
Analyzing kafala for two types of nawā'ib and scholarly disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of nawā'ib (rightful and unjust) and state the intra-Hanafi disagreement on kafāla for unjust taxes, identifying the reasoning of each side.

Core Idea
Kafāla for unjust taxes is disputed: some prohibit it because the underlying debt is not binding; others permit it because kafāla attaches to the demand itself.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah addresses the ruling of kafāla (guarantee) for nawā'ib (taxes/levies). It first categorizes taxes into rightful and unjust, then presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute on whether one can validly guarantee an unjust tax. Understanding this nuance is crucial for muftis dealing with state-imposed levies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of nawā'ib
Recognize that the text divides taxes into two types: those that are rightful (ḥaqq) and those that are unjust (ẓulm). Note the examples given for each category.
Examples of rightful include taxes for public canals, guards' salaries, military preparation, and ransoming captives when the treasury is empty. Unjust examples include taxes on craftsmen and general taxation in certain regions.
2
Understand the unanimous ruling for rightful taxes
The text states that kafāla for rightful taxes is unanimously permissible because such taxes are debts (dayn) binding on Muslims.
The justification is that obedience to the Muslim ruler is obligatory when the tax is for public benefit.
3
Identify the contested issue for unjust taxes
For unjust taxes, there is disagreement among Hanafi mashā'ikh. Locate the two positions: some say kafāla is invalid, others say it is valid.
This ikhtilaf forms the core of the lesson.
4
Analyze the proof of the first group (prohibition)
The first group argues that kafāla was legislated to make a demand binding only when the demand is itself binding on the principal. Since unjust taxes are not binding in Sharīʿa, kafāla is invalid.
Their dalil is textual : kafāla is for something that is 'wājib ʿalā al-aṣīl'.
5
Analyze the proof of the second group (permissibility)
The second group, led by Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, argues that kafāla is about undertaking the demand (iltizām al-muṭālabah), and the ruler's demand creates a debt in effect, regardless of the tax's legitimacy.
Their focus is on the effective demand, not the original obligation.
6
Examine the discussion on qismat
The text concludes by relating 'qismat' (apportioned levy) to nawā'ib. Some equate them, while others distinguish qismat as a permanent regular tax and nawā'ib as occasional levies.
The ruling follows the same classification: if qismat is rightful, kafāla is valid; if unjust, it is disputed.

اس چیز کے مطالبہ کو لازم کرنے کیلئے مشروع ہوا ہے جو چیز خود اصیل یعنی مکفول عنہ پر لازم ہو ۔ حالانکہ جو نوائب خود اصیل یعنی مسلمانوں پر شرعاً لازم نہیں ہیں تو ان کا کفالہ بھی جائز نہ ہوگا اور بعض حضرات مشائخ مثلا فخر الاسلام الامام علی بزدوی نے فرمایا کہ اس قسم کے نوائب کا کفالہ بھی جائز ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ بادشاہ کی طرف سے جو ٹیکس بھی مقرر کیا جائے گا خواہ بجا اور حق طور پر ہو خواہ بے جا اور ظلما ہو وہ دین ہوگا۔ بایں معنی کہ بادشاہ کی طرف سے اس کا مطالبہ کیا جاتا ہے اور کفالہ میں مطالبہ ہی معتبر ہے۔ کیونکہ کفالہ التزام مطالبہ کیلئے مشروع ہوا ہے۔ پس جب ہر طرح کے ٹیکس کا مطالبہ کیا جاتا ہے تو ہر طرح کے ٹیکس کا کفالہ بھی درست ہوگا اور رہا لفظ قسمت تو بعض حضرات نے کہا کہ قسمت اور نوائب دونوں ایک ہی چیز ہیں ان میں کوئی فرق نہیں ہے۔ پس اس صورت میں نوائبہ اور قسمتہ کے درمیان واؤ ہو گا اور عطف، عطف تفسیری ہوگا اور جو حکم نوائب کا ہے وہی حکم قسمت کا ہوگا۔ اور یا قسمت نوائب کا ایک حصہ ہے۔ مثلا کسی حادثہ کے موقع پر مسلمانوں پر کچھ مقرر کر دیا ہو جیسے پل ٹوٹ گیا اور اس کا خرچہ تمام لوگوں پر پھیلا یا گیا تو ایک آدمی پر جو پڑا وہ اس کی قسمت ہے ۔ اب اگر کوئی شخص اس کی طرف سے کفیل ہو گیا تو جائز ہے اس صورت میں نوائبہ اوقسمتہ کے درمیان اؤ ہوگا۔ اور ترجمہ یہ ہوگا کہ نوائب کی کفالت کی یا قسمت کی کفالت کی ۔ اور بعض حضرات نے نوائب اور قسمت کے درمیان یہ فرق کیا ہے کہ قسمت سے مراد وہ نائبہ (ٹیکس) ہے جو مستقلاً مقرر کر دیا گیا ہو اور اس کو وقت خاص پر پابندی کے ساتھ وصول کیا جاتا ہو جیسے چوکیدار کی تنخواہ دینے کیلئے اہل محلہ پر ماہانہ مقرر کر دیا ہو اور نوائب سے میرا دوہ ہیں جو ہنگامی طور پر کبھی کبھار مقرر کر دیا جائے۔ مثلاً اچانک کوئی پل ٹوٹ گیا ہو اس کو درست کرانے کیلئے جو وظیفہ لوگوں پر مقرر کیا جائے گا۔ اس کو نائبہ کہتے ہیں اور نوائب کا حکم سابق میں مذکور ہو چکا کہ نوائب اگر بجا اور برحق ہوں تو ان کا کفالہ بالا تفاق جائز ہے۔ اور اگر بیجا اور نا حق ہوں تو ان کی کفالت کے جواز اور عدم جواز میں مشائخ کا اختلاف ہے۔
It is permissible. And as for the ruling of nawā'ib (taxes/levies), nawā'ib are of two types: one which is justified and rightful, such as the king wanting to dig a canal that is not for anyone specific but for the general public benefit, and there is no capacity in the treasury, so the king imposes a small amount on the people. And like when a guard is appointed for the protection of a neighborhood and the treasury is empty, so some money is imposed on the people to pay his salary. And like when an army is prepared against infidels and the treasury is empty, or to free Muslim captives from the clutches of infidels, money is imposed on the people when the treasury is empty. Additionally, any levy that is imposed on Muslims in a right and permissible manner is included in this type. The second type: those that are not rightful, but rather the king has imposed them unjustly, like in Persia and other countries, tailors, dyers, and other craftsmen and servants have monthly or annual taxes on their earnings. And especially nowadays in India, by the security and protection [of God], there is tax on everything: tax on houses, tax on travel, tax on purchases, tax on sales. All these taxes are included in this second type, which are collected unjustly; they have no obligation in Sharia. So if by nawā'ib is meant those taxes that are justified and rightful, then kafāla for them is unanimously permissible because every new tax that the leader of the Muslims has obligated on the Muslims for their benefit, its payment is obligatory on the Muslims, and its payment is obligatory because obedience to the leader of the Muslims is obligatory. So when the payment of these nawā'ib is obligatory, they are what is obligatory in the dhimmah, and what is obligatory in the dhimmah is debt. Therefore these nawā'ib will be debts, and kafāla for debts is permissible, so kafāla for them will be permissible. As for the second type, i.e., those nawā'ib and taxes that the king has imposed unjustly, there is disagreement among the mashā'ikh regarding the permissibility or impermissibility of kafāla for them. Indeed, some of the mashā'ikh said that kafāla for such nawā'ib is not permissible, because kafāla was legislated to make the demand binding for something that is itself binding on the principal (the one on whose behalf guarantee is given). However, these nawā'ib are not themselves binding on the principal, i.e., the Muslims, in Sharia, so their kafāla will not be permissible. And some of the mashā'ikh, like Fakhr al-Islam Imam Ali al-Bazdawi, said that kafāla for this type of nawā'ib is also permissible. The proof is that any tax imposed by the king, whether it is justified and rightful or unjust and tyrannical, will be a debt, meaning that the king demands it, and in kafāla, the demand is what matters, because kafāla was legislated for undertaking the demand. So when every type of tax is demanded, kafāla for every type of tax will be valid. As for the word 'qismat', some have said that qismat and nawā'ib are the same thing; there is no difference between them. In that case, between nawā'ib and qismat there is a wāw, and the conjunction is explanatory, and the ruling for nawā'ib is the same as the ruling for qismat. Or qismat is a part of nawā'ib, e.g., on the occasion of an incident, something is imposed on Muslims, like a bridge breaking and its cost is spread over all people, so what falls on one person is his qismat. Now if someone becomes a guarantor for it, it is permissible. In that case, between nawā'ib and qismat there is a wāw, and the translation would be: 'Kafāla for nawā'ib or kafāla for qismat.' And some have made this distinction between nawā'ib and qismat: that by qismat is meant that tax which is imposed permanently and collected regularly at a specific time, like a monthly imposition on the residents of a neighborhood to pay the guard's salary. And by nawā'ib is meant those levies that are imposed occasionally, e.g., when a bridge suddenly breaks, the levy imposed on people to repair it is called nā'ibah. And the ruling of nawā'ib has already been mentioned: if nawā'ib are justified and rightful, then kafāla for them is unanimously permissible; and if they are unjust and not rightful, then regarding the permissibility or impermissibility of their kafāla, the mashā'ikh have disagreement.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya discusses the ruling of kafāla (guarantee) for nawā'ib (taxes/levies imposed by the state). It opens by defining two types of nawā'ib: (1) those that are rightful (ḥaqq), such as taxes for public works, guards' salaries, military preparation, or ransoming captives when the treasury is empty; these are obligatory on Muslims. (2) Those that are unjust (ẓulm), such as taxes on craftsmen and general taxation in Persia and India, which are not obligatory in Sharia. For the first type, kafāla is unanimously valid because the taxes are debts (dayn) in the dhimma. For the second type, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: some mashā'ikh argue that since these taxes are not binding on the principal (aṣīl), kafāla is invalid because kafāla is contingent on a binding demand. Others, like Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, argue that kafāla is valid for any tax that is demanded by the king, regardless of its legitimacy, because kafāla is about undertaking the demand (iltizām al-muṭālabah). The passage then explains the term 'qismat' (apportioned levy) and its relation to nawā'ib. Some equate them, while others differentiate: qismat is a permanent regular tax, while nawā'ib are occasional levies. The ruling follows accordingly. The text aligns with the Hanafi principle that kafāla is valid for any financial obligation that is demanded, even if the underlying obligation is not strictly binding in Sharia, as long as the demand is effective (such as from a ruler).

Key Terms
نوائب (nawā'ib): Taxes or levies imposed by the state on the populace, used for public or emergency purposes.
کفالہ (kafālah): A contract of guarantee where the guarantor (kafīl) undertakes to satisfy a demand against the principal (aṣīl).
قسمت (qismat): An apportioned share of a levy imposed on individuals, usually for a specific purpose and collected regularly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT an example of a rightful tax (nawā'ib ḥaqq) given in the text?
A) Tax for digging a public canal.
B) Tax for a guard's salary for neighborhood protection.
C) Tax on craftsmen's earnings.
D) Tax for ransoming Muslim captives.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of kafāla for taxes hinges on the nature of the tax: rightful taxes are unanimously valid, while unjust taxes are disputed based on whether kafāla requires a binding obligation or merely a demand.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Whose Statement is Accepted When Term is Disputed? Iqrar vs Kafala
Explaining whose statement is believed when term is disputed in iqrar vs kafala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whose statement is accepted when a term is disputed in debt acknowledgment versus guarantee, and explain the reasoning (wajh al-farq) behind the distinction.

Core Idea
In debt, term is incidental so the creditor is believed; in guarantee, term is integral so the guarantor is believed.

Imagine two scenarios: someone says 'I owe you $100 due in one month,' but you say it's immediate. In another, someone says 'I guarantee that amount for you, due in one month,' and you say it's immediate. Hanafi law treats these two cases differently because of the nature of the term (ajal) in each transaction. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Note the two distinct scenarios: (1) a person acknowledges a debt (iqrar) with a claimed term, and the creditor disputes the term; (2) a person acknowledges a guarantee (kafāla) with a claimed term, and the beneficiary disputes the term.
The muqir (acknowledger) is the debtor in the first case and the guarantor (kafīl) in the second.
2
State the ruling for each case
In iqrar, the statement of the creditor (muqarr lahu) is accepted (with oath). In kafāla, the statement of the guarantor (kafīl) is accepted (with oath).
The ruling is procedural: determines the burden of proof in court.
3
Analyze the reasoning (wajh al-farq)
In iqrar, the term is an incidental condition that is not established without explicit stipulation; the creditor denies the condition, so his word is taken. In kafāla, the term is part of the guarantee itself (type of it); the guarantor's acknowledgment of the guarantee does not contradict his claim of a term because he did not acknowledge the debt.
The key is whether the term is incidental or integral.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i makes the guarantee follow the debt (if the debt is immediate, the guarantee is also immediate). Abu Yusuf (in a nawādir narration) makes the debt follow the guarantee. The standard Hanafi position is the one explained above.
The practical difference: in kafāla, if the debtor had no term, the Shafi'i view would deny the guarantor's claim of a term.

Source Text

ضمنت لک عن فلان مائة الى شهر وقال المقرله هى حالة فالقول قول الضامن ووجه الفرق ان المقر اقربا لدين ثم ادعى حقا لنفسه وهوتا خير المطالبة الى اجل وفى الكفالة ما اقر بالدين لانه لادين عليه في الصحيح انما بمجرد المطالبة بعد الشهر ولان الاجل في الديون عارض حتى لا يثبت الابشرط فكان القول قول من انكر الشرط كما في الخيار اما الاجل فى الكفالة فنوع منها حتى يثبت من غير شرط بان كان مؤجلا على الاصيل والشافعي الحق الثانى بالاول وابو يوسف فيما يروى عنه الحق الاول بالثاني والفرق قد أوضحناه ترجمہ اور اگر ایک آدمی نے دوسرے سے کہا کہ تیرے مجھ پر ایک سو درہم ایک ماہ کے ادھار ہیں اور مقرلۂ نے کہا کہ یہ قرضہ فی الحال ہے تو مدعی کا قول معتبر ہے اور اگر کسی نے کہا کہ میں نے تیرے لئے فلاں کی طرف سے ہمیعاد ایک ماہ ایک سو درہم کی کفالت کی تھی اور مقرلہ نے کہا کہ یہ قرضہ فی الحال ہے تو ضامن کا قول معتبر ہوگا اور وجہ فرق یہ ہیکہ مقر نے قرضہ کا اقرار کیا پھر اپنے واسطے ایک حق کا دعویٰ کیا اور وہ ایک وقت تک مطالبہ کی تاخیر ہے اور کفالہ میں مقر نے قرضہ کا اقرار نہیں کیا ہے کیونکہ صحیح قول کے مطابق کفیل پر قرضہ نہیں ہوتا . اس نے ایک ماہ بعد محض مطالبہ کا اقرار کیا ہے اور اس لئے کہ قرضوں میں میعاد ایک عارضی چیز ہے حتی کہ بغیر شرط کے ثابت نہیں ہوتی پس اسی شخص کا قول معتبر ہو گا جو اس شرطت انکار کرے۔ جیسے خیار میں ہے۔ رہی کفالہ میں میعاد تو اس کی ایک قسم ہے حتی کہ بغیر شرط کے میعاد ثابت ہو جاتی ہے بایں طور کہ اصیل پر قرضہ میعادی ہو اور امام شافعی نے ثانی کو اول کے ساتھ لاحق کیا ہے اور ابو یوسف نے اپنی نوادر کی روایت میں اول کو ثانی کے ساتھ لاحق کیا ہے ہم نے فرق کو وضاحت کے ساتھ بیان کر دیا ہے۔ تشریح ۔ اس عبارت میں دو مسئلے مذکور ہیں، 1) ایک شخص نے دوسرے سے کہا کہ تیرے مجھ پر ایک ماہ کے ادھار پر ایک سو درہم ہیں یعنی ان کی ادائیگی کا وقت ایک ماہ بعد ہے اور جس کے لئے اقرار کیا تھا اس نے کہا کہ یہ قرضہ فی الحال واجب الاداء ہے تو مقر لۂ جو فی الحال واجب الاداء ہونے کا مدعی ہے مع الیمین اس کا قول معتبر نہ ہوگا۔ (۲) ایک شخص نے دوسرے سے کہا کہ میں تیرے لئے فلاں کی طرف سے ایک ماہ کی میعاد کی شرط کے ساتھ ایک سو درہم کا کفیل ہوا تھا اور مقرلۂ نے کہا کہ یہ کفالہ فی الحال ہے اس میں کوئی میعاد نہیں ہے تو کفیل (مقر ) کا قول مع ایمین معتبر ہو گا۔ ان دونوں مسئلوں کے درمیان وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ قرضہ کے اقرار کی صورت میں مقر نے قرضہ کا اقرار کیا ہے اور اپنے لئے ایک ماہ کی میعاد کا دعویٰ کیا ہے پس مقرلا نے قرضہ کا تو اعتراف کر لیا مگر میعاد کا انکار کر دیا تو مقر میعاد کا مدعی ہوا، مقرل منکر ہوا اور مدعی یعنی مقربینہ پیش کرنے سے عاجز ہے۔ لہذا منکر یعنی مقر لہ کا قول مع الیمین معتبر ہوگا۔ کیونکہ مدعی کے پاس بینہ موجود نہ ہونے کی صورت میں
A person made an acknowledgment (iqrar) for another of a hundred rupees for one month; the muqarr lahu (the one in whose favor the acknowledgment was made) said that this debt is due immediately. Then the statement of the claimant (muqarr) is accepted. And (the Arabic text): 'Whoever said to another: you have a hundred [dirhams] from me until a month, and the muqarr lahu said it is immediate, then the statement of the claimant (muqarr) is accepted. And whoever said: I have guaranteed for you from so-and-so a hundred until a month, and the muqarr lahu said it is immediate, then the statement of the guarantor (ḍāmin) is accepted. And the difference (wajh al-farq) is that the muqarr acknowledged the debt, then claimed a right for himself, which is deferral of demand until a term. In kafāla, he did not acknowledge the debt, because according to the correct view, the guarantor does not have a debt; he only acknowledged mere demand after the month. Also, the term in debts is incidental, such that it is not established without a condition, so the statement of the one who denies the condition is accepted, as in khiyār. As for the term in kafāla, it is a type of it, such that it is established without a condition if the debt on the principal was deferred. Al-Shafi'i made the second follow the first, and Abu Yusuf, in what is related from him, made the first follow the second. We have explained the difference.' Translation: And if a person said to another: 'You have a hundred dirhams from me as a loan for one month,' and the muqarr lahu said: 'This debt is due immediately,' then the statement of the claimant (muqarr) is accepted. And if someone said: 'I guaranteed for you from so-and-so a hundred dirhams with a term of one month,' and the muqarr lahu said: 'This debt is due immediately,' then the statement of the guarantor is accepted. The difference is that in the first case, the muqarr acknowledged the debt and then claimed a right for himself, i.e., deferment of demand. In kafāla, the muqarr did not acknowledge the debt because, according to the correct view, the guarantor does not owe the debt; he only acknowledged the demand after a month. Also, the term in debts is incidental, so it is not established without a condition; therefore, the statement of the one who denies the condition is accepted, as in khiyār. As for the term in kafāla, it is a type of it, so it is established without a condition if the debt on the principal was deferred. Imam al-Shafi'i made the second (kafāla) follow the first (debt), and Abu Yusuf, in a narration from him, made the first follow the second. We have explained the difference clearly. Commentary: In this passage, two issues are mentioned: 1) A person said to another: 'You have a hundred dirhams from me as a loan for one month,' and the muqarr lahu said: 'This debt is due immediately.' Then the muqarr lahu, who claims immediate payment, his statement with oath is not accepted. 2) A person said to another: 'I became a guarantor for you from so-and-so for one hundred dirhams with a condition of a one-month term,' and the muqarr lahu said: 'This kafāla is immediate; there is no term in it.' Then the statement of the kafīl (muqarr) with oath is accepted. The difference between these two issues is that in the case of acknowledgment of debt, the muqarr acknowledged the debt and claimed a one-month term for himself, so the muqarr lahu acknowledged the debt but denied the term; thus, the muqarr is the claimant regarding the term, and the muqarr lahu is the denier. The claimant (muqarr) is unable to produce evidence, so the denier's (muqarr lahu) statement with oath is accepted.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya addresses a nuanced point in Hanafi fiqh: when there is a dispute about the term (ajal) in a debt acknowledgment (iqrar) versus a guarantee (kafāla), whose statement is believed? The ruling is that in iqrar, the statement of the muqarr lahu (creditor) is accepted if he says the debt is immediate (i.e., no term), because the debtor admitted the debt and then claimed a right to deferment, so he is the claimant. In kafāla, the statement of the kafīl (guarantor) is accepted if he claims a term, because the guarantor did not admit to the debt itself (according to the correct view, the guarantor is not liable for the debt but only for demand after the term). The text provides the reasoning: in debts, the term is an incidental condition that is not established without explicit stipulation, so the one who denies the condition (creditor) is believed. In kafāla, the term is part of the nature of the guarantee (it is a type of it) and can be established without a stipulation if the debt on the principal was deferred; hence, the guarantor's claim of a term is accepted. The passage also notes an ikhtilaf: Imam al-Shafi'i analogized kafāla to debt (the second follows the first, meaning if the debt has no term, the kafāla also has no term), while Abu Yusuf (in a nawādir narration) made the first follow the second (i.e., the debt's term is determined by the kafāla's term). The Hanafi relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that in kafāla, the guarantor's statement is accepted. The commentary (Tashrih) further clarifies the two cases and the procedural point about who is the claimant and who is the denier.

Key Terms
مقر (muqir): The person who makes an acknowledgment (iqrar) – here, the debtor in a debt case or the guarantor in a kafāla case.
مقر لہ (muqarr lahu): The person in whose favor the acknowledgment is made – the creditor or the guarantee beneficiary.
أجل (ajal): A fixed term or deadline for payment. In this passage, it is the point of dispute whether the debt or guarantee is immediate or deferred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, in a dispute over the term of a guarantee (kafāla), whose statement is accepted (with oath)?
A) The beneficiary (muqarr lahu)
B) The guarantor (kafīl)
C) The original debtor (aṣīl)
D) Both are accepted

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the nature of the term differs between debt and guarantee: incidental in debt (creditor believed), integral in guarantee (guarantor believed).

Distinguishing Debt and Kafalah in Disputes Over Deferment
Explanation of the distinction between two cases of dispute over deferment in debt vs. kafalah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whose statement is accepted in a dispute over deferment in debt acknowledgment versus kafalah, and explain the legal distinction based on the nature of ajal (term).

Core Idea
In debt, deferment is incidental and requires a condition; in kafalah, deferment is integral to the type of surety — so the burden of proof shifts.

When two parties disagree on whether a payment is immediate or deferred, Islamic procedural law must decide who is believed. This lesson from al-Hidayah examines two scenarios: a debt acknowledgment and a guarantee (kafalah). Although both involve identical factual disputes, the Hanafi school rules differently in each case based on the nature of the term (ajal). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying procedural rules in financial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two factual scenarios
Read the passage and list the two disputes: (1) a person acknowledges a debt of 100 dirhams payable in one month, and the recipient claims it is immediate; (2) a person acknowledges a kafalah for 100 dirhams with a one-month term, and the recipient claims it is immediate.
Note that the same factual pattern appears, but the legal nature differs.
2
State the Hanafi ruling in each case
In the debt case, the recipient's statement is accepted; in the kafalah case, the guarantor's statement is accepted.
This is the procedural rule: the one who denies the additional condition (deferment) is believed in debt, while the one who acknowledges the deferred guarantee is believed in kafalah.
3
Analyze the distinction: the nature of ajal
In debt, ajal is incidental (arid) and requires an explicit condition; therefore, the one who denies the condition (the recipient) is believed. In kafalah, ajal is a type (naw') of kafalah itself, so the guarantor's acknowledgment of a deferred kafalah is accepted.
The passage gives an analogy: 'like khiyar in sales' for debt, and for kafalah, ajal is like 'natiq' to 'hayawan' — an essential attribute.
4
Examine the opposing views
Imam al-Shafi'i attached kafalah to debt, ruling that in both cases the recipient's statement is accepted. Abu Yusuf (in one narration) attached debt to kafalah, ruling that in both cases the acknowledger's statement is accepted. Later commentators (al-'Ayni, Ibn al-Humam) noted a possible scribal error reversing these attributions.
The correction: al-Shafi'i attached debt to kafalah (both favor acknowledger), Abu Yusuf attached kafalah to debt (both favor recipient).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: If a guarantor claims deferred kafalah and the recipient claims immediate, the Hanafi judge accepts the guarantor's statement, while al-Shafi'i would accept the recipient's statement.
This affects the outcome of disputes in court.

Book of Kafalah. Ashraf al-Badani, volume 9. ... And if they are unjust and false, then there is a difference among the mashayikh regarding the permissibility of their kafalah. A person acknowledged for another a hundred rupees due in one month; the one in whose favor it was acknowledged said, 'This debt is immediately due,' so the statement of the plaintiff (al-muddai) is accepted. And whoever says to another, 'You have against me a hundred until a month,' and the one addressed says, 'It is immediately due,' then the statement of the plaintiff is accepted. And whoever says, 'I guaranteed for you from so-and-so a hundred until a month,' and the one in whose favor it was acknowledged says, 'It is immediately due,' then the statement of the guarantor is accepted. The face of the distinction is that the acknowledger (al-muqr) acknowledged the debt, then claimed a right for himself, which is delaying the demand until a term. And in kafalah, he did not acknowledge a debt because in the correct view, there is no debt upon him; he merely acknowledged the demand after a month. And because the term (ajal) in debts is incidental such that it is not established without a condition. So the statement of the one who denies the condition is accepted, as in the option (khiyar). As for the term in kafalah, it is a type of it such that it is established without a condition, if the debt was deferred on the principal (asil). Al-Shafi'i attached the second to the first, and Abu Yusuf, in what is narrated from him, attached the first to the second. We have clarified the distinction. Translation: ... Explanation: In this passage, two issues are mentioned: 1) A person said to another, 'I owe you one hundred dirhams on credit for one month,' and the one in whose favor it was acknowledged said, 'This debt is immediately due,' then the one in whose favor it was acknowledged (al-muqarr lahu) who claims it is immediately due, his statement is not accepted (instead the defendant's statement is accepted with oath). 2) A person said to another, 'I guaranteed for you from so-and-so one hundred dirhams with a condition of a one-month term,' and the one in whose favor it was acknowledged said, 'This kafalah is immediate,' then the guarantor's (muqir's) statement is accepted with oath. The distinction between these two issues is... (continues similar to the Arabic).

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah discusses the ruling on whose statement is accepted when there is a dispute over the deferment (ajal) of a debt versus a guarantee (kafalah). The original text of al-Hidayah presents two cases: (1) A person acknowledges a debt of one hundred dirhams payable in one month, and the recipient claims it is immediate. (2) A person acknowledges a kafalah for one hundred dirhams with a one-month term, and the recipient claims it is immediate. The Hidayah rules that in the first case, the recipient's statement is accepted (because the acknowledger is claiming an additional condition of deferment), while in the second case, the guarantor's statement is accepted. The distinction is based on two principles: First, in debt acknowledgment, the acknowledger admitted the debt and then claimed a right for himself (deferment), making him the plaintiff; whereas in kafalah, the guarantor did not admit a debt because in the correct view, the guarantor is not liable for the debt itself but only for the demand (mutalaba) after the term. Second, deferment (ajal) in debts is incidental (arid) and requires an explicit condition, so the one who denies the condition (the recipient) is believed. In kafalah, deferment is a type (naw') of kafalah itself, so when the guarantor acknowledges a deferred kafalah, it is inherent and accepted. The passage then notes that Imam al-Shafi'i attached the second case (kafalah) to the first (debt), ruling that in both cases the recipient's statement is accepted. Abu Yusuf, in a narration, attached the first to the second, ruling that in both cases the guarantor's/acknowledger's statement is accepted. However, later commentators like al-'Ayni and Ibn al-Humam clarified that there is a scribal error; the correct attribution is that al-Shafi'i attached the first to the second (debt to kafalah), and Abu Yusuf attached the second to the first (kafalah to debt). The commentary then elaborates on the distinction, emphasizing that deferment is essential (dhati) to deferred kafalah, much like speech (natiq) is essential to humans, whereas in debt it is incidental.

Key Terms
میعاد (أجل) (mī'ād / ajal): A specified time for payment or performance, i.e., deferment or term.
کفالہ (kafālah): Suretyship or guarantee, where a person (kafil) undertakes to present the debtor or pay the debt if the debtor fails.
مقرله (muqarr lahu): The person in whose favor an acknowledgment is made; the recipient or beneficiary.
مقر (muqir): The person who makes an acknowledgment or admission.
عارض (arid): Incidental; something that is not essential to the nature of the thing.
نوع (naw'): Type or species; a category that is essential to the thing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in al-Hidayah, in a dispute over kafalah where the guarantor claims the guarantee was for one month and the recipient claims it was immediate, whose statement is accepted?
A) The recipient's statement is accepted.
B) The guarantor's statement is accepted.
C) Neither, a judge decides based on evidence.
D) The statement of the original debtor (asil) is accepted.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes debt from kafalah in disputes over deferment: in debt, deferment is incidental so the recipient is believed; in kafalah, deferment is integral to the type of surety, so the guarantor is believed.

Kafalah — Surety

12 min
Dispute Over Acceptance of Guarantor's Word in Kafalah vs. Iqrar
Explaining the difference between iqrar and kafalah regarding whose statement is accepted when a term is disputed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling on whose statement is accepted in a disputed term in iqrar vs. kafalah, and evaluate the analogies of Imam Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
In iqrar the term is incidental, in kafalah it is essential — different natures yield different rulings.

This passage from the Hidayah clarifies a critical distinction often misunderstood: while both a debtor and a guarantor may claim that payment is deferred, the legal weight of their statements differs. The key lies in whether the term (mi'ad) is an incidental attribute or an essential part of the obligation. We'll walk through the Hanafi position, the alternative views of Imam Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf, and why the Hanafi school rejects the analogies between the two cases.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling for each case: in iqrar (acknowledgment of debt), the debtor's statement is accepted when he claims a term; in kafalah (guarantee), the guarantor's statement is accepted when he claims the guarantee is deferred.
Remember that in iqrar, the debtor is the plaintiff claiming an additional condition; in kafalah, the guarantor is specifying the type of guarantee.
2
Identify the opponents and their analogies
Imam Shafi'i is reported to have analogized iqrar to kafalah, holding that the debtor's statement should be accepted in both. Imam Abu Yusuf analogized kafalah to iqrar, holding that the creditor's statement should be accepted in both.
Later commentators corrected the transmission: Shafi'i actually analogized iqrar to kafalah, and Abu Yusuf analogized kafalah to iqrar.
3
Analyze the opponents' proofs
Shafi'i argued: just as in kafalah the guarantor's statement is accepted (because he acknowledges a type with a term), so in iqrar the debtor's statement should be accepted. Abu Yusuf argued: just as in iqrar the creditor's statement is accepted (because the debt is admitted and the term disputed), so in kafalah the creditor's statement should be accepted.
Both rely on qiyas (analogy) based on the formal similarity of a disputed term.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school rejects both analogies as fasid (invalid) because the nature of the term differs: in iqrar, the term is 'arid (incidental) and not established without a condition; in kafalah, the term is dhati (essential) to the type of guarantee. Therefore, the two cases cannot be analogized.
The denier's statement is accepted in both cases, but who is the denier differs based on the default rule for each context.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: If a debtor claims a deferred debt and the creditor demands immediate payment, the creditor's statement (denying the term) is accepted. If a guarantor claims a deferred guarantee and the creditor demands immediate payment, the guarantor's statement (claiming the term) is accepted.
The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position as explained in the Hidayah.

كِتَابِ الْكَفَالَةِ اَشْرَف الْبِدَایَہ جلد نهم منکر کا قول معتبر ہوتا ہے اور دوسرے مسئلہ میں یعنی کفالہ کی صورت میں مقر یعنی کفیل نے قرضہ کا اقرار نہیں کیا ہے۔ کیونکہ صحیح قول کے مطابق کفیل پر قرضہ مقر لۂ دعویٰ کرتا ہے کہ مجھ کو فی الحال مطالبہ کا حق حاصل ہے اور کفیل یعنی مقرفی الحال مطالبہ کا انکار کرتا ہے تو مکفول ل ( مقرہ ) مدعی ہوا۔ اور کفیل ( مقر ) منکر ہوا اور مدعی یعنی مقرا کے پاس چونکہ ثابت نہ ہوتی ہو وہ چونکہ عارضی ہوتی ہے اس لئے میعاد قرضوں میں ایک عارضی چیز ہوگی یہی وجہ ہے کہ عقد بیع میں شمن مہر اور تلف کردہ چیزوں کی قیمتیں فی الحال واجب الادا ہوتی ہیں ان میں بغیر شرط کے میعاد بیٹہ نہ ہونے کی صورت میں منکر کا قول مع الیمین معتبر ہوگا۔ اور رہا کفالہ تو میعاد اس میں عارضی چیز نہیں ہوتی۔ بلکہ میعاد کفالہ کی ایک نوع ہے کیونکہ کفالہ کی دو قسمیں ہیں، ١) كَفَالَةُ مُعَجَّلٍ ٢) كَفَالَةُ مُؤَجَّلٍ جیسے ناطق ایک نوع ہے کیونکہ حیوان کی دو نوع ہیں۔ ١) حیوان ناطق ٢) حیوان غیر ناطق پس جس طرح ناطق بعض حیوان کی ذاتیات میں سے ہے اسی طرح میعاد بھی کفالہ مؤجل کی ذاتیات میں سے ہے یہی وجہ ہے کہ کفالہ مؤجل میں میعاد بغیر شرط کے ثابت ہو جاتی ہے بایں طور کہ جب اصیل یعنی مکفول لا پر میعادی قرضہ ہو تو یہ میعاد کفیل کے حق میں بھی ثابت ہوگی ۔ کیونکہ کفیل نے کفالہ کی ایک نوع یعنی کفالہ مؤجل کا اقرار کیا ہے اور جب اس نے کفالہ کی ایک نوع کا اقرار کیا ہے تو دوسری نوع کا حکم نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ اور جب کفالہ مؤجل کا اقرار کرنے سے دوسری نوع یعنی كفالہ مقتل کا حکم نہیں کیا جا سکتا تو کفالہ دونوں مسئلوں میں مقرلہ کا قول معتبر ہوگا اور امام ابو یوسف نے پہلے مسئلہ کو دوسرے مسئلہ کے ساتھ لاحق کیا ہے یعنی دونوں مسئلوں میں مقر کا قول معتبر ہوگا علامہ بدرالدین عینی اور علامہ ابن الہمام اور دیگر شارحین ہدایہ نے تحریر کیا ہے کہ امام شافعی اور امام ابو یوسف کے اقوال کو نقل کرنے میں کاتب کا سہو ہے ورنہ صحیح اس کا بر عکس ہے یعنی امام شافعی نے مسئلہ اول کو ثانی کے ساتھ لاحق کیا ہے اور امام ابو یوسف نے مسئلہ ثانی کو اول کے ساتھ لاحق کیا ہے۔ امام شافعی کے قول کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ قرضہ کی دو قسم ہیں۔ ١) میعادی ۲) غیر میعادی پس جب میعادی قرضہ کا اقرار کیا تو دوسری قسم یعنی غیر میعادی قرضہ لازم نہ ہوگا۔ بلکہ میعادی قرضہ لازم ہوگا اور جب مقر پر میعادی قرضہ لازم ہوا تو مقر کا قول ہی معتبر ہو گا جیسے کفالہ کی صورت میں مقر یعنی کفیل کا قول معتبر ہوتا ہے حضرت امام ابو یوسف کے قول کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ دوسرے مسئلہ میں کفیل اور مکفول لۂ دونوں نے مال واجب ہونے پر اتفاق کیا ہے یعنی کفیل بھی اقرار کرتا ہے کہ مکفول عنہ پر ایک سو درہم ہیں اور میں کفیل ہوں اور مکفول لہ بھی اس کی تصدیق کرتا ہے پھر ان میں سے ایک یعنی کفیل نے میعاد کا دعوی کیا اور مکفول لڑ نے اس کا انکار کیا اور مدعی اینی کفیل کے پاس چونکہ بینہ موجود نہیں ہے اس لئے منکر یعنی مکفول لہ کا قول معتبر ہو گا جیسا کہ قرضہ کے اقرار کی صورت میں مقرلہ کا قول معتبر ہوتا ہے۔ ہماری طرف سے جواب یہ ہے کہ پہلے مسئلہ کو دوسرے مسئلہ پر قیاس کرنا جیسا کہ امام شافعی نے کیا ہے اور دوسرے مسئلہ کو پہلے مسئلہ پر قیاس کرنا جیسا کہ امام ابو یوسف نے کیا ہے دونوں فاسد ہیں۔ کیونکہ قرضہ کے اندر میعاد ایک عارضی چیز ہے اور اس کا دعوی اصل کے موافق ہے پس اس فرق کے ساتھ ایک کو دوسرے پر قیاس کرنا کس طرح درست ہو گا۔
Book of Guarantee. Ashraf al-Bidaya volume nine. The statement of the denier is accepted. In the second issue, i.e., in the case of guarantee, the confirmer (the guarantor) did not acknowledge a debt. Because according to the correct view, the debt is not obligatory upon the guarantor, rather merely the claim (right to demand) is obligatory. And he acknowledged the claim after one month, meaning he acknowledged that the creditor has the right to demand from him after one month. So the summary is: the creditor (the beneficiary of the confession) claims that he has the right to demand immediately, and the guarantor (the confirmer) denies the immediate right to demand. Thus the creditor (the confirmer) is the plaintiff, and the guarantor (the confirmer) is the defendant. And since the plaintiff (the confirmer) has no evidence, the statement of the defendant (the confirmer) with an oath is accepted. The second reason for the difference is that the existence of a term in debts is an incidental matter. Consequently, a term is not established without a condition. And what is not established without a condition is incidental, so the term in debts is incidental. For this reason, in a contract of sale, the price, dower, and values of destroyed items are immediately due; a term is not established in them without a condition. So when a term is not established in debts without a condition, then in the first case (acknowledgment of debt), the debtor claimed the condition of a term and the creditor denied it. Since the plaintiff (the debtor) has no evidence for the condition of the term, the statement of the denier of the condition (the creditor) with an oath is accepted. As in the case of option: if one of the two contracting parties claims a stipulated option and the other denies, then in the absence of evidence from the plaintiff, the statement of the denier with an oath is accepted. As for guarantee, a term is not incidental in it. Rather, the term is a type of guarantee, because guarantee has two types: 1) promptly due guarantee (Kafalah Mu'ajjal), 2) deferred guarantee (Kafalah Mu'ajjal). Just as 'speaking' is a type because animals have two types: 1) speaking animal (human), 2) non-speaking animal. So just as 'speaking' is part of the essence of some animals, similarly the term is part of the essence of deferred guarantee. For this reason, in deferred guarantee, the term is established without a condition, such that when the principal (the debtor) has a deferred debt, this term is also established for the guarantor. This is because the guarantor acknowledged one type of guarantee, i.e., deferred guarantee. And when he acknowledged one type of guarantee, the ruling of the other type is not applied. And when the ruling of the other type (immediate guarantee) cannot be applied due to the acknowledgment of deferred guarantee, then in the case of guarantee, the statement of the confirmer (guarantor) is accepted, not the statement of the beneficiary. The author of Hidayah said that Imam Shafi'i annexed the second case (the case of guarantee) to the first case (the case of acknowledgment), meaning in his view, in both cases the statement of the beneficiary is accepted. Imam Abu Yusuf annexed the first case to the second case, meaning in both cases the statement of the confirmer is accepted. Allamah Badr al-Din al-'Ayni, Allamah Ibn al-Humam, and other commentators of Hidayah have written that there is a scribal error in transmitting the statements of Imam Shafi'i and Imam Abu Yusuf; otherwise, the correct is the opposite: Imam Shafi'i annexed the first case to the second, and Imam Abu Yusuf annexed the second case to the first. The reason for Imam Shafi'i's statement is that debt has two types: 1) with a term, 2) without a term. So when he acknowledged a term debt, the other type (non-term debt) would not be binding, rather the term debt is binding. And when the term debt is binding upon the confirmer (debtor), his statement is accepted, just as in the case of guarantee the statement of the confirmer (guarantor) is accepted. The reason for Imam Abu Yusuf's statement is that in the second case (guarantee), both the guarantor and the creditor agreed that the property is obligatory. That is, the guarantor acknowledges that the principal owes one hundred dirhams and I am the guarantor, and the creditor confirms it. Then one of them (the guarantor) claimed a term, and the creditor denied it. Since the plaintiff (the guarantor) has no evidence, the statement of the denier (the creditor) is accepted, just as in the case of acknowledgment of debt the statement of the beneficiary is accepted. Our response is that analogizing the first case to the second, as Imam Shafi'i did, and analogizing the second case to the first, as Imam Abu Yusuf did, both are invalid. Because in debt the term is an incidental matter, and its claim is contrary to the origin. So with this difference, how can one be analogized to the other correctly?
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a detailed intra- and inter-madhhab dispute regarding whose statement is accepted when a term of payment is contested in cases of acknowledgment of debt (iqrar) versus guarantee (kafalah). The discussion is a commentary on the Hidayah, explaining the reasoning behind the Hanafi ruling. The text first establishes the fundamental difference: in acknowledgment of debt, the term is incidental ('arid) and not established without explicit condition, so the debtor's (muqir) statement is accepted when he claims a term. In guarantee, however, the term is an essential (dhati) attribute of a specific type of guarantee (mu'ajjal), so the guarantor's (kafil) statement is accepted when he claims a deferred guarantee. The text then clarifies a reported dispute: Imam Shafi'i is said to have analogized the guarantee case to the acknowledgment case, holding that the creditor's statement is accepted in both. Imam Abu Yusuf is said to have analogized the acknowledgment case to the guarantee case, holding that the debtor's statement is accepted in both. However, the commentators (al-'Ayni, Ibn al-Humam) correct this: the actual positions are reversed. Shafi'i actually analogized acknowledgment to guarantee, and Abu Yusuf analogized guarantee to acknowledgment. The text then provides the reasoning for each imam's position and concludes that both analogies are invalid (fasid) because the nature of the term differs in the two contexts. The Hanafi position (as per the author of Hidayah and the majority commentators) is that the two cases are distinct, and the ruling is different in each. This guards against the misunderstanding that the same principle applies uniformly to all cases involving a disputed term.

Key Terms
مقر (muqir): The person making the acknowledgment (iqrar); in the context of a debt, the debtor. He is the one who confesses to the debt.
مقرلہ (muqir lahu): The person in whose favor the acknowledgment is made; the creditor.
کفیل (kafil): The guarantor; the person who takes on the responsibility of the debt or claim of another.
مکفول لہ (makful lahu): The creditor or the person entitled to the claim in a guarantee contract.
میعاد (mi'ad): A term or due date for payment.
عارضی (aridi): Incidental; not intrinsic to the contract or obligation. In classical usul, 'arid is a temporary attribute that does not change the essence.
ذاتی (dhati): Essential; pertaining to the essence or quiddity of a thing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is the statement of the guarantor (kafil) accepted when he claims the guarantee is deferred, whereas in the case of acknowledgment of debt (iqrar), the statement of the debtor (muqir) is accepted when he claims a term?
A) Because the term is incidental in both cases, but the guarantor is the denier.
B) Because in guarantee the term is an essential part of the type (mu'ajjal), while in debt the term is incidental.
C) Because the creditor in guarantee is always reliable.
D) Because the guarantor has more rights than the debtor.

Key Takeaway
The acceptance of a statement in a disputed term depends on whether the term is incidental (iqrar) or essential (kafalah) — a distinction that prohibits analogizing between the two cases.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Guarantee of Darak: When Can the Buyer Claim from the Guarantor?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on darak guarantee obligation timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the majority Hanafi condition for the darak guarantor's obligation and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Darak guarantee obliges the guarantor only after a judgment against the seller, not upon mere claim.

When a buyer purchases an item with a darak guarantee and a third party claims ownership, does the buyer immediately have recourse against the guarantor? The Hanafi majority says no—the sale remains valid until a judicial ruling. Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding that the claim itself invalidates the sale. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
Recognise that the passage concerns a guarantee of darak made by a guarantor (kafil) for the buyer. The dispute is about when the buyer can take the price from the guarantor: immediately upon claim (istihqaq) or only after a judgment against the seller.
The ruling is specific to darak, not general kafalah.
2
State the majority Hanafi position (zahir al-riwaya)
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the buyer cannot take from the guarantor until the price is adjudged against the seller. The sale is not invalidated by mere claim; a judicial decision is required.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Explain the rationale
The guarantor's obligation is secondary to the principal (asil, the seller). Since the seller's duty to return the price arises only after a judgment, the guarantor's duty arises at the same time. Until then, the sale remains effective.
Contrast with the case of a judgment of freedom (qada bil hurriyya), where the sale is void ab initio because a free person cannot be sold.
4
Present Abu Yusuf's dissenting view
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the claim of ownership itself invalidates the sale, because it proves the seller did not own the item. Therefore, the buyer can immediately revert to both seller and guarantor without waiting for a judgment.
This view is based on analogy (qiyas) to the case of freedom, but the majority distinguishes.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference: under the majority, the buyer must sue the seller first; under Abu Yusuf, the buyer can directly claim from the guarantor. The text gives preference to the majority by stating 'ala zahir al-riwaya' and noting Abu Yusuf's view as a narration.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.

Source Text

سی نے باندی خریدی پھر ایک شخص درک کا کفیل ہو گیا با ندی کا کوئی مستحق نکل آیا تو مشتری کفیل سے لے سکتا ہے یا نہیں قال ومن اشترى جارية فكفل له رجل بالدرك فاستحقت لم ياخذ الكفيل حتى يقضى له بالثمن على البائع لان بمجرد الاستحقاق لا ينتقض البيع على ظاهر الرواية مالم يقض له بالثمن على البائع فلم يجب له على الاصيل رد الثمن فلا يجب على الكفيل بخلاف القضا بالحرية لان البيع يبطل بها لعدم المحلية فيرجع على البائع والكفيل وعن ابى يوسف انه يبطل البيع بالاستحقاق فعلى قياس قوله يرجع بمجرد الاستحقاق وموضعه اوائل الزيادات في ترتيب الأصل ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے ایک باندی خریدی پھر اس کے واسطے ایک آدمی درک کا کفیل ہو گیا پھر وہ باندی مستحق ہوگئی تو مشتری کفیل سے نہیں لے سکتا یہاں تک کہ مشتری کے لئے بائع پر شمن کا فیصلہ کر دیا جائے۔ اس لئے کہ محض استحقاق کی وجہ سے ظاہر الروایہ کے مطابق بیع نہیں ٹوٹتی ہے جب تک کہ مشتری کے واسطے بائع پر یمن کا فیصلہ نہ کر دیا جائے پس جب تک اصیل (بائع ) پرشمن واپس کرنا واجب نہیں ہو گا تو کفیل پر بھی واجب نہ ہوگا۔ برخلاف آزادی کے فیصلہ کے اس لئے کہ آزادی کے فیصلہ سے بیع باطل ہو جائے گی ۔ کیونکہ محل بیچ معدوم ہے پس مشتری بائع اور کفیل دونوں پر رجوع کرے گا اور امام ابو یوسف سے مروی ہے کہ استحقاق کی وجہ سے بیع باطل ہو جائے گی پس امام ابو یوسف کے قول پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے مشتری محض استحقاق کی وجہ سے کفیل سے رجوع کرے گا اور یہ مسئلہ زیادات کے اوائل میں اصلی ترتیب میں مذکور ہے۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے ایک باندی خریدی پھر ایک آدمی مشتری کے واسطے درک کاکفیل ہوا یعنی اس نے مشتری کہا کہ اگر کسی نے تجھ سے استحقاق ثابت کر کے یہ باندی لے لی تو میں تیرے شمن کا کفیل ہوں پھر یہ باندی مستحق ہو گئی یعنی کسی نے
(Question:) Suppose someone bought a slave girl, and then a man became his guarantor for darak (guarantee against eviction). Then someone else came forward as a rightful claimant to the slave girl. Can the buyer take from the guarantor? (He said:) 'And whoever buys a slave girl and a man becomes his guarantor for darak, and then she is claimed (by a rightful owner), he (the buyer) shall not take from the guarantor until the price is adjudged against the seller. Because, according to the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya), the sale is not invalidated merely by the claim of ownership (istihqaq) unless the price is adjudged against the seller. So as long as the return of the price is not obligatory upon the principal (the seller), it is not obligatory upon the guarantor. Contrary to the judgment of freedom (i.e., when the judge declares the slave free), because the sale is invalidated by that due to the non-existence of the subject matter (the slave not being a valid commodity). So in that case, the buyer can revert to both the seller and the guarantor. And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that the sale is invalidated by the claim of ownership. So according to his statement, by analogy, the buyer reverts immediately upon the claim of ownership. And this issue is mentioned in the beginning of al-Ziyadat in the arrangement of the original.' Translation: If someone bought a slave girl, and for him a man became a guarantor for darak, then that slave girl became claimed, the buyer cannot take from the guarantor until the price is adjudged against the seller. Because, according to zahir al-riwaya, the sale is not broken merely by the claim of ownership, until the price is adjudged against the seller for the buyer. So as long as the return of the price is not obligatory upon the seller (the principal), it will not be obligatory upon the guarantor. Contrary to the judgment of freedom, because the sale becomes void by the judgment of freedom, since the subject matter (the slave) becomes non-existent (free persons cannot be sold). So the buyer reverts to both the seller and the guarantor. And it is narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf that the sale is invalidated by the claim of ownership. So, analogizing to his statement, the buyer reverts to the guarantor immediately upon the claim of ownership. And this issue is mentioned in the early parts of al-Ziyadat in the original arrangement. Explanation of the case: It is that a person bought a slave girl; then a man became a guarantor for darak for the buyer, meaning he told the buyer: 'If someone establishes his claim to this slave girl and takes her from you, I am the guarantor for your price.' Then this slave girl becomes claimed, i.e., someone...
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the guarantee of darak (guarantee against eviction) and a dispute between the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa) and Imam Abu Yusuf. The matn from the Hidayah states that if a buyer purchases a slave girl and a guarantor guarantees darak (i.e., promises to compensate the buyer if the slave is taken by a rightful owner), then the buyer cannot claim from the guarantor immediately when the slave is claimed by a third party. Instead, he must wait until a judgment is passed against the seller for the price. The reason is that, according to the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya), the sale is not invalidated merely by the claim of ownership (istihqaq); it only becomes invalid when the court issues a ruling transferring the price from the seller to the buyer. As long as the seller is not obliged to return the price, the guarantor is not obliged either. The exception is when the slave is declared free by a judge: then the sale is immediately void because a free person cannot be the subject of a sale, so the buyer can take from both the seller and the guarantor immediately. Imam Abu Yusuf holds a differing view: in his opinion, the sale is invalidated by the mere claim of ownership, so the buyer can take from the guarantor immediately upon the claim. The text notes that this issue is found in the early parts of al-Ziyadat (one of the six books of Imam Muhammad). This guards against the misunderstanding that a darak guarantee becomes payable as soon as a claimant appears; the Hanafi majority requires a formal judicial determination.

Key Terms
درک (darak): A guarantee against eviction; a promise by a guarantor to compensate the buyer if the sold item is taken by a rightful owner.
استحقاق (istihqaq): The claim of ownership by a third party; the act of establishing a right to the property.
ظاہر الروایہ (zahir al-riwaya): The apparent or well-known transmission; the authoritative position of the early Hanafi masters (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) as recorded in the six books of Imam Muhammad.
اصیل (asīl): The principal; in this context, the seller (ba'i') who is primarily responsible for the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for the buyer to be able to take the price from the guarantor of darak according to the majority Hanafi view?
A) As soon as a third party claims ownership of the item.
B) Only after a judgment is passed against the seller for the price.
C) Only if the item is lost or destroyed.
D) Immediately, without any condition.

Key Takeaway
The darak guarantor's obligation arises only after a judicial ruling against the seller; a mere third-party claim does not obligate the guarantor.
